Introduction

Expert management of medical products safeguards their quality and safety, significantly
improving patient care. It ensures products are available promptly, minimises waste, and
maximises the efficiency of resources.

This guide replaces the intersectional guide ‘Supply of Drugs and Medical Supplies and
Management of Pharmacies’ (2008). Inspired by the WHO Medicines Management Cycle
(Selection, Quantification, Procurement, Storage and Distribution), its structure extends beyond
its scope to provide in-depth coverage of topics such as the importation of medical products,
the management of end-user units, good dispensing practices, rational use and monitoring, the
management of quality issues and medical products during the opening and closure phases.
The guide includes numerous appendices, as well as an additional digital Toolbox containing
supplementary documentation. As a result, it serves as a more comprehensive resource
tailored to the needs of MSF operations.

This guide has been developed to ensure its relevance and applicability across all MSF sections.
It has been validated by the MSF Medical Directors Platform. Supply departments from all MSF
sections were actively involved in its development and validation.

The main objectives are:

– To provide clear and reliable guidance and practical, relevant recommendations to help

MSF programs manage medical products effectively.

– To promote alignment between MSF sections regarding the management of medical

products.

– To offer common terminology that can be applied by all MSF sections, regardless of the

tools or responsibility frameworks used by each section.

This guide is aimed specifically at MSF contexts. It is primarily intended for MSF programs
staff involved in the management of medical products at any stage. It also serves as a valuable
resource for MSF headquarters staff. It is designed to be used as a reference tool rather than as
a book to be read from start to finish. Readers can access the chapters most pertinent to their
needs directly without having to read the entire document sequentially.

To keep the guide user-friendly, it is not practical to address every possible scenario that may
arise within MSF operations. Consequently, some recommendations or details need to be
adapted in certain contexts. In such cases, it is recommended to consult MSF headquarters for
further advice.

The terminology used is carefully aligned with the equitable, respectful and inclusive language
principles actively promoted by MSF in its publications.

The authors welcome any feedback that will help to ensure that this guide continues to evolve
and promote best practice, while remaining responsive to the needs of MSF programs. Despite
our best efforts, some errors may have been overlooked in this guide. If you notice any, please
report them by writing to feedback-medicalguidelines@msf.org

Chapter 1: Selection

15

1

1.1 Introduction

The selection process involves identifying medical products based on medical activities,
validated guidelines, and protocols. It also includes consulting experts or medical team
members to ensure a comprehensive understanding of the medical strategy. The outcome of
the selection process is a standard list tailored to a specific contexta.

Standard lists, such as the medical standard list (MSL), help to avoid inappropriate or
unnecessary requests for medical supplies, including unsolicited donations. They also guide
new or less experienced staff in following established guidelines and protocols, ultimately
enhancing the quality of care delivered to patients.

1.2 Product information and standardisation

1.2.1 Where to find product information

UniData: MSF central product information database

Every product within an MSL should be appropriately coded and labelled. UniData is MSF’s
central database for product information, regularly updated and validated by technical
referents and data stewards. Access to UniData is limited to specific roles within MSF, but
information on active products is accessible to all MSF users through front-end tool calledb
Unicatc.

Unicat: online standard and non-standard product catalogue

Unicat is a web-based solution that provides quick and user-friendly access to product
information stored in UniData. With Unicat, users can create and share lists, export information,
access MSF catalogues, and view related products based on compatibility, replacement options,
spare parts, consumables, or inclusion in specific kits. Unicat is synchronised with Unidata and
accessible to all MSF staff.

1.2.2 MSF international coding system for products

Each product in UniData is coded based on harmonised coding rules adopted by the
entire MSF movement. This applies to all product categories, including logistics, medical,

a Standard lists can be developed at different organisational levels, including headquarters (MML), MSF program

or project level, end-user unit level, etc. In this chapter, the term MSL refers specifically to the project level.
b https://unicat.msf.org/
c Refer to MSF catalogues for more details.

Chapter 1: Selection

16

and library items. Coding products based on their English labels or descriptions ensures
a clear, unambiguous ordering process that helps streamline the MSF supply chain.
Any MSF code is composed of a minimum of 11 and a maximum of 13 alphanumeric characters,
structured into 4 subdivisions: the group, the family, the root, and the specificationd.

Figure 1.1 - MSF product coding structure

Source: MSF catalogues

1.2.3 Product standardisation levels

Every medical product coded in UniData is assigned a standardisation level: standard (STD),
non-standard (NST), or non-standard local (NSL). The standardisation level of a product can
change based on its validation for use.

Table 1.1 - MSF product standardisation levels

Standard (STD)

• The medical product complies with MSF medical policies and
has been validated by all MSF sections’ medical directors,
making it the best choice for use in MSF programs.

• The medical product can be sourced from at least one MSF
supply centre.

• Although it is not mandatory, multiple quality-assured
sources are typically preferred to minimise supply risks.
However, a product can still be designated as an STD with
only one validated source if necessary.

Non-standard (NST)

• The medical product has been validated by at least one
medical director.

• The medical product can be sourced from at least one MSF
supply centre.

d Refer to MSF catalogues for more details.

Chapter 1: Selection

17

1

Non-standard local
(NSL)

• The medical product has been validated by at least one MSF
section for use in MSF programs and is only procured locally
due to supply issues (e.g., importation constraints).

• The procurement of these products is limited to specific MSF
projects and must follow the specific procurement validation
procedure.

All activee STD and some active NST productsf are displayed in Unicat. Inactive productsg

and medical NSL are NOT displayed in Unicat.

Every year, MSF international medical working groups and medical directors convene to review
the list of STD medical products, considering additions or removals based on requests received
throughout the year. Product standardisation can change based on several factors, including
effectiveness, alignment with MSF medical policies, and operational needs. For example, NST
products can be reevaluated and potentially upgraded to STD.

Annual standardisation process

– Any MSF staff member can propose improvements in patient care by submitting a request to

the relevant International Working Group (IWG) or International Contact Group (ICG).

– The IWG/ICG assesses the proposal to ensure it aligns with current policies and protocols,

reviews specifications, and checks for duplication within the product family.

– After approval, the IWG/ICG submits the proposal to the source of product information and

coordination (SPINCO)h.

– The quality assurance (QA) team, along with MSF supply centre representatives and

purchasers, validate the product’s sourcing and purchasing feasibility. The international
office quality assurance coordinators assign a QA referent to oversee these tasks.

– The medical director’s platform reviews and formally validates the list of requested changes.

– Once approved, new or promoted products are added to the MSF catalogues by the SPINCO

team. These STD products are then available for purchase through all MSF supply centres.

Types of requests

– New: request to add a new product.

– Change: request to modify an existing STD product.

– Removal: request to reclassify an STD product as NST and/or to designate the product as

obsolete or discontinued.

– Promotion: request to upgrade an NST product to STD.

e Active products are those holding a Unidata lifecycle status as either valid, outdated, or discontinued.
f NST articles will only be displayed if the technical referents request their publication, and the technical sheet

has been fully completed and approved by both the technical referents and the IWG/ICG.
g Inactive products are those holding a UniData lifecycle status as archived, forbidden, parked, temporary,

rejected, or in preparation.
h SPINCO is an MSF team within MSF International responsible for delivering reliable product information across

the organisation through platforms such as Unidata and Unicat.

Chapter 1: Selection

18

1.3 Medical master list

The medical master list (MML) is the list of products validated by the medical department for
procurement and use within the MSF section. The MML is based on the MSF section’s medical
activities, as well as validated guidelines and protocols.

– Each MML should align with the MSF section medical strategy and validated treatment

guidelines and protocols.

– Each MML should aim for coherence with the product portfolio used by the MSF supply

centre associated with the respective MSF section (though this is not a strict limitation, as
MSF projects may not always have access to all MSF supply centres’ catalogues, and new
products can be added to these catalogues upon request).

– Each MML should be accessible to MSF staff.

– Each MML is regularly maintainedi by warrantorsj through UniData (see Section 1.2.1). The

MML should align as closely as possible with key resources such as

• MSF catalogues and the annual summary of changes,

• MSF supply centres' catalogues,

• MSFk or Ministry of Health (MOH) guidelines and protocols,

• MSF Templates for medical activitiesl.

Since MMLs align with their respective MSF section medical strategy, they do not

necessarily contain all STD products listed in the MSF catalogues. For example, if an
MSF section does not engage in microbiology activities or advanced orthopaedic surgeries,
STD products specific to these activities will not be included in the MML.

New product request:

Occasionally, an activity may require a medical product not listed in the MML due to

country legislation, national protocol, or a new activity for a specific MSF section. In

this case, the medical team should submit a request to the HQ pharmacy referent

using a specific form.

New product use must be endorsed by the MSF section's medical direction, with

reliable sourcing and stable supply guaranteed. Refer to HQ pharmacy referent for
more information.

i Removing inactive or unnecessary products and including new ones as required.
j Warrantors are HQ pharmacy referents responsible for updating the MML.
k Such as MSF Clinical guidelines, MSF Essential drugs guideline, or other guidelines in use.
l MSF medical international working groups or medical referents provide product templates for specific medical

activities. These templates help MSF programs during the selection process to prevent the omission of
important products.

1.4 Project medical standard list

The MSL is a list of medical products required for the optimal running of the medical programs.
These products have been validated for procurement and use and must be always kept
available.

Each medical activity can have its own articlem list per activity (ALPA), tailored to the specific
needs of that activity. For example, there can be an ALPA for prenatal care and another ALPA
for family planning. The project MSL combines these ALPAs, ensuring that all necessary medical
products for various project activities are included.

1.4.1 Roles and responsibilities

The main roles and responsibilities must be well understood. Figure 1.2 illustrates the most
relevant roles and responsibilities. For further guidance, refer to the HQ pharmacy referent.

1.4.2 Planning

Effective planning is a prerequisite for achieving optimal product selection. The ideal time to
develop or revise the MSL is when the project’s operational strategy and medical activities
have been defined, as detailed in the project’s annual plan. The development or revision of

m Unidata uses the term "article" to denote a product.

Chapter 1: Selection

20

the MSL must occur prior to the forecasting, ordering, and budgeting process, ensuring that all
necessary medical products are accounted for.

Operational strategy → MSL → Forecasting and ordering → Budgeting

Note: forecasting, ordering, and budgeting are often interdependent processes. While this
sequence illustrates a general flow, forecasting and budgeting may occur simultaneously in
practice, with adjustments made to quantities or allocations as budget constraints or needs
evolve.

Under certain circumstances, it may be necessary to revise the MSL ahead of the regular
schedule. Such circumstances may include:

– Modification of the regulations within the country

– Significant changes in the project’s medical activities, including the addition or removal of

activities, changes in admission criteria, etc.

– Modifications to protocols or guidelines that require immediate attention and cannot be

postponed

– The annual renewal of the import exemption (only in some countries)

The MSL is not static and should reflect the changing operational strategy and


circumstances of each project. The project medical team must be informed of any
significant change to the MSL during the validation process. There may be a delay between the
change/addition of a product in the project MSL and its availability for implementing the new
protocol. The medical team must be informed of the final MSL validated by HQ (see Section
1.4.1).

1.4.3 Important considerations

Before initiating the selection process

– Tailoring the MSL: ensure the MSL is tailored to each project. Comprehensive awareness

and understanding of both the medical strategy and the project’s operational activities are
crucial.

– Multi-disciplinary team approach: the development or revision of the MSL is primarily led

by the medical team, with input from other staff members when necessary (e.g., biomed
and laboratory technicians, ward managers, etc.).

– MSF engagement level: MSF may operate independently, assume full responsibility for

health structures and services, or manage them partially. MSF may also adopt a direct
hands-on approach or opt for a mentorship or support model. The level of engagement
directly impacts the MSL. For example, in hands-on interventions, the MSL might include
a wider range of products needed for direct patient care. In contrast, in mentorship or
support roles, the MSL may be more limited, reflecting a reduced scope of responsibility
for direct service provision.

– Medical staff competencies: assess the availability of qualified medical specialists,

ensuring that highly specialised materials are reserved for their use. Ensure medical
equipment can be properly installed and maintained.

– Country-specific characteristics: consider the specific characteristics of each country, such

as national guidelines, protocols, and regulations, especially when working in collaboration

Chapter 1: Selection

21

1

with the MoH. Factors such as infrastructure, climate, and security may also influence the
selection of certain products or logistics arrangements.

– Diversity of the target population: including different age groups, beliefs, customsn, etc.

– Consider the age groups, medical conditions, and demographic characteristics of the

population being served, as these factors can influence the types of medical products
needed (e.g., paediatric vs. adult formulations).

– Roles and responsibilities: understand the different roles and responsibilities within the

medical departments concerning the selection process (see Section 1.4,1).

– Additional factors: such as the product’s shelf-life, service level agreement for equipment

maintenance, and any other operational constraints like budgetary limitations.

There is no fixed or predetermined way to create or update an MSL. Depending on

the medical activity and context, one or more of the following approaches may be

adopted:
• Using specific protocols and guidelines for one or several medical activities to collect the
primary needs (e.g., products needed for diagnosis, treatment, and follow-up).

• Starting from templates developed by technical referents (e.g., sexual reproductive
health referents for antenatal care or family planning), which provide ready-to-use lists
tailored to specific medical activities.

• Using the MSF catalogues or kit lists of products to guide the selection of necessary
products for different medical scenarios, as long as these scenarios align with the
project's objectives.

• Using the MSL from a similar project as a reference.

• Using MSF templates or tools, such as biomedical tools, to identify spare parts and
consumables.

During the selection process

– Morbidity and medication use patterns: examining morbidity patterns or medication

use patterns is a potent method for evaluating whether medications within the MSL
adequately fulfil the therapeutic requirements of patients.

– Product availability and restrictions: select only products that must always be kept

available, including those not frequently used but necessary. This includes both
consumables and non-consumables items (see Section 1.4.7). For example, EPREPo ,
snake antivenom, backup oxygen concentrators, and important spare parts for medical
equipment should be included in the MSL. Start by verifying the availability of the selected
product in the MML (see Section 1.3). If the selected product is not available, find a
suitable therapeutic alternative in the MML.

– Associated products: the selection of some products may imply the use of other

associated products (e.g., eye wash should be available when certain cytotoxic medications
are used).

– Medication selection: when possible, use only one strength and one form of each

medication (e.g., tablets or capsules). If possible, prioritise dispersible tablets over liquid
forms and breakable tablets over capsules.

n Beliefs and customs may be relevant in specific cases. For instance, some populations may prefer oral forms

over injectables, or vice versa, due to religious or cultural beliefs. While this may not affect the entire MSL, it
could influence the choice of specific products in certain contexts.
o Emergency preparedness plan

Chapter 1: Selection

22

– Spare parts, accessories, and consumables (laboratory and medical equipment): ensure

compatibility. Equipment-related products can be found in Unicatp (see Section 1.2.1).

– Exclusion of one-off purchases: one-off purchases, which are exceptions and not meant to

be consistently available, should not be included in the MSL. Examples may include:

• A one-time purchase made due to an urgent need or for a specific donation.

• An exceptional one-time medication or equipment (e.g., tablet planner), intended for a
single patient, particularly when the project does not involve non-communicable disease
activities.

• An exceptional deviation from a treatment protocol.

• Non-recurring products specifically used for the repair or maintenance of medical
equipment.

– Procurement strategy within the MSF program: the sourcing may influence the selection

process. Special attention should be given to the national regulations governing the
importation of medical products (see Section 1.4.5).

1.4.4 Criteria for including new products in an MSL

Before introducing new medical products into the project's MSL, the project pharmacy
manager and/or medical activity manager (MAM) should comprehensively evaluate their
potential impact. This evaluation should assess alignment with current treatment protocols,
storage and handling requirements (e.g., for bulky products, cold chain requirements, look-
alike products), and budget considerations. New products must also comply with safety and
efficacy standards.

For the efficient and appropriate selection of new medical products, the following criteria are
recommended:

Criterion 1: justification of need

– Indication: confirm that the product is suitable for the intended medical conditions.

– Clinical guidelines and protocols: ensure alignment with available validated clinical

guidelines.

– Possible alternatives: verify that no other product within the MSL satisfies the same need,

considering factors like:

• Efficacy: for example, explore differences in the microbiological spectrum of
antimicrobial medications with similar indications but different mechanisms of action.

• Contraindications: ensure the product is safe for the target population. For example,
verify that a drug intended for adults is not used for children, or that medications
contraindicated for pregnant women are avoided.

• Adverse events: choose medications with minimal adverse events when possible. Assess
the severity of potential adverse events (mild, moderate, severe, fatal) and evaluate
the need for medications to manage those side effects (e.g., loperamide for diarrhoea
caused by antiretroviral therapy).

• Route of administration: oral forms should be the first choice; parenteral medication
is only recommended for severe medical conditions or when oral forms are unsuitable
(due to gastrointestinal intolerance or malabsorption).

p Refer to the HQ Biomed referent for further guidance if needed.

Chapter 1: Selection

23

1

• Monitoring resources: consider the equipment, reagents, and other resources necessary
for monitoring the medication’s effects. Assess the risks and benefits of administering
medication without adequate monitoring protocols.

– Possible replacements: when including a new product, determine whether similar

products can be excluded from the MSL to ensure efficiency and optimal use of resources.

– Previous medication-related problems: check for past adverse events or inappropriate use

of the medication (e.g., used for unintended indication).

Criterion 2: availability and access

– MSF catalogues: prioritise STD products from the MSF catalogues, as NST may cause

further delivery delays or may not be suitable for the specific project context (see Table
1.1).

– National regulations: ensure compliance with national pharmaceutical regulations. When

possible, prioritise products on the National Essential Medicine List (NEML) or the WHO
Essential Medicines List (WHO EML).

– Product availability: assess the availability of the product from suppliers, including MSF

supply centres, and if necessary, consider external local suppliers when importation is not
feasible.

Criterion 3: cost justification

– Expense evaluation: assess the product's cost-effectiveness and its potential to reduce

therapy duration and hospital stay.

– Associated costs: consider additional costs such as:

• Related materials (e.g., intravenous bags, solutions, etc.)

• Required medication to support the primary treatment (e.g., premedication)

• Required medication to treat possible adverse events and complications

• Extra storage: if special storage conditions or additional space are required

1.4.5 Regulatory import requirements

Regulatory import requirements are established by the national regulatory authorities (NRA) or
other relevant national authorities. These requirements can affect the importation of medical
products, potentially impacting MSF’s operational capacity if medical supplies are delayed or
blocked. In some cases, it may be entirely impossible to import certain products due to specific
country regulations.

It is crucial to verify regulatory requirements when defining or updating the MSLq. This requires
interdepartmental collaboration between the medical and supply/log departments.

q Verified information for each country is accessible through MSF regulatory affairs SharePoint https://msfintl.

sharepoint.com/sites/MedicalDevicesRepository/SitePages/Regulatory-Affairs.aspx and the country supply
profiling GeoApp (CSP GeoApp) https://geo.msf.org/supplyprofiling/map, available to anyone with an MSF
email address.

Chapter 1: Selection

24

Key points to consider

– Be aware of any concessions explicitly stated in the Memorandum of Understanding(MoU)r

or Host Country Agreement (HCA)s, if available.

– Always verify that the regulations enforced at the point of entry align with those in the

national legislation or guidelines, as discrepancies may exist.

– Verify whether the medications are listed on the NEML or the more frequently updated

WHO EML1. If not, additional justification or documentation may be required for
importation.

– Occasionally, MSF may maintain a security stock to cover potential stockouts of key

products that are usually provided by the MoH through the national program, such
as vaccines distributed via the Expanded Program on Immunisation. In this case, it is
important to verify whether MSF has the required authorisation for importing these
products.

For more information regarding the importation of medical products, see Chapter 3.

1.4.6 Restricted products within an MSL

Some projects may require the use of exceptional and specialised products. The purchase and
use of these products require exceptional authorisation from HQ, which may also include the
medical director. Examples include:

– Research work: the procurement process (sourcing, supplier selection, price negotiation,

etc.) is managed by the MSF supply centre.

– Antibiotics: certain WATCHt and RESERVEu antibiotics must be pre-authorised by infectious

disease specialists.

– Cancer therapy: colony-stimulating factor should be used only as an adjunct to cancer

chemotherapy.

– Other specialised products: the use of thrombolytic medications must be authorised by

cardiologists or emergency room physicians.

1.4.7 Product categorisation in the MSL

Depending on the medical activities covered by each project, product categorisation may vary
across projects and cannot be systematically captured.

Product categorisation should be undertaken by the team responsible for creating or

updating a Project’s MSL (see Section 1.4.1).

r A formal agreement between two or more parties outlining the terms and details of a mutual understanding or

agreement, framing the conditions of MSF activities. MOUs are signed (1) at the local level with a representative
of the Ministry of Health (MOH) or of a local organisation to fix the conditions of a project/partnership (Project
MOU), or (2) at the governmental level with the MOH or the Line Ministry of NGOs to fix the conditions of MSF
presence/action in a country (Framework MOU).
s A formal agreement based on international law, providing for privileges and immunities similar to diplomatic

missions, signed by the Ministry of Foreign Affairs (MoFA) on behalf of the government for the benefit of all
MSF sections present or not in the country.
t WATCH antibiotics: broader-spectrum and higher-cost, recommended for patients with more severe clinical

infections.
u RESERVE antibiotics: broad-spectrum, to be used as a last-choice treatment for multidrug-resistant infections.

Chapter 1: Selection

25

1

Vital, essential, and desirable

The MSL can typically range from approximately 400 products in a small project to around
1,200 products in a complex project. Not all products in the MSL hold equal significance or
impact on patient care. Products can be categorised using the vital, essential, desirable (VED)
classification, which indicates their critical necessity for ongoing medical activities:

– Vital: critical for life-saving interventions, with no suitable substitutes (e.g., anaesthesia

products, adrenaline, etc.).

– Essential: critical for the continuation of core medical activities. A short-term stockoutv

would not halt activities.

– Desirable: least critical; a stockout would not significantly impact medical activities.

Product categorisation facilitates operations across various scenarios, including:

– Setting up, modifying, or resuming medical activities.

– Addressing stockout requests and facilitating medical-supply meetings for prioritisation.

– Handling exceptional validation requests for local procurement.

– Reviewing the costs associated with medical orders.

Consumables and non-consumables

Medical products within the MSL can also be categorised based on consumption patterns:

– Consumable: products intended for regular use, for which a forecasted monthly

consumption (FMC) is defined in the forecasting process. Examples include paracetamol
tablets in a primary health care project and medications used in a non-communicable
disease project.

– Non-consumable: products not typically used on a regular basis, for which a maximum

stock level is established during the forecasting process. Examples include hospital beds,
spare parts and maintenance components for medical equipment and some lab products.

Depending on the project and the intended use of the product, certain products may be
classified as consumable or non-consumable. Examples include:

– Post-exposure prophylaxis (PEP) kits: in projects covering sexual and gender-based

violence (SGBV) activities, PEP kits are considered consumable. Conversely, in MSF projects
that do not include SGBV activities, PEP kits are considered part of staff health and are
classified as non-consumable.

– Surgical instruments: some instruments are available in both consumable (e.g., plastic

amniohook) and non-consumable (e.g., stainless-steel amniohook) forms. Consumable
options are often preferred because they are easy to use and reduce the risk of cross-
contamination. However, non-consumable alternatives are best in settings where safe re-
use can be ensured (e.g., access to sterilisation facilities), to minimise waste, reduce cost,
and facilitate stock management.

v Stockout refers to a situation in which stock levels for a particular product within the MSL are depleted, making

it impossible to fulfil the operational demands for that product.

Chapter 1: Selection

26

1.5 Stock list

The stock list is a record of products currently in stock, as well as those that were in stock
within a specific timeframew (e.g., products currently in stockout or certain non-consumable
products).

Ideally, the MSL and stock list should align. However, differences between the two lists can
occur due to various reasons, such as:

– Closure of a project or specific medical activity, resulting in surplus products that are no

longer needed.

– Changes in medical protocols

– Previous donations or orders that included unnecessary products

– Special circumstances like one-time purchases

– Temporary substitution of MSL products

Occasionally, a project may need to accept non-MSL products as temporary


substitutes for MSL products. These substitute products will be included in the stock
list but should not be classified as MSL products. Once the existing stock of substitutes is
consumed, these products should be removed from the stock list.

The project MSL and stock list together form the core framework of medical products, which
should be periodically analysed. See more information in Section 1.6.2.

w The recommendation about the specific timeframe may vary among the different MSF sections. Refer to the

HQ referent for more information.

Chapter 1: Selection

27

1

1.6 Application of the MSL in the supply processes

1.6.1 Forecasting and ordering process

Forecasting and ordering should be based on the products contained within the MSL. Verifying
orders against the MSL acts as a filtering mechanism to help identify and eliminate inappropriate
medical supply requests, thereby reducing the likelihood of misuse of medical products.

However, the absence of a requested product from the MSL does not necessarily

mean the request should be rejected. There may be valid justifications for such
requests, so the absence of a product should prompt a second review rather than immediate
rejection.

1.6.2 Stock analysis

Medical stocks should be regularly reviewed against the project’s operational needs. The review
frequency is flexible and can be monthly, quarterly, or even weekly, especially in emergencies.

For effective analysis and decision-making, it is important to differentiate between products
in the MSL and those only in the stock list. For example, if several consumable products (see
Section 1.4.7) in the MSL are in sleeping stockx, additional analysis should be conducted to
understand the underlying reasons. Possible explanations may include medical activities not
yet initiated, a lack of familiarity with the protocol, or prepositioned stock, which is typically
not used regularly but kept on hand for emergencies.

x Consumable products in stock with no consumption over the last 12 months (excluding products recently

entered in stock and products within prepositioned stocks such as EPREP)

Chapter 1: Selection

1.7 Case study

In the absence of a medical coordinator, the pharmacy coordinator received a communication
from the head of mission (HoM) about alarming findings from the MSF exploratory team
concerning Northeast Mirland, a nation in Northeast Africa. An urgent intervention with
mobile clinics is planned.

Task: define the MSL for the mobile clinics, noting that laboratory resources will not be
available.

Current morbidity data:

– Watery diarrhoea: 35%

– Bloody diarrhoea: 5.7%

– Respiratory tract infections: 15.3% (upper or lower tract not specified)

– Malaria: 27.8%

1. What general considerations should be kept in mind?

– Use the MML (see Section 1.3) as a reference point.

– Use available resources such as MSF Clinical guidelines, MSF kits catalogues, etc.

– Base selection on each clinical condition, prioritising formulations already used within the

MSF program.

– Consider the age demographics of the population to be treated: children < 5 years, ages

5–15 years, and adults.

– Assess the impact of seasonal changes, such as dry or rainy seasons, on potential

pathologies.

2. Examine the example provided below. Do you observe a critical omission?

Critical omissions:

– Important staff health products, such as PEP kits, are missing.

– A first aid kit for staff should be available for any MSF intervention.

– Diagnostic and examination equipment, related consumables, and dispensing materials

should be included.

be considered.

– Ensure consumables for administering injectables (e.g., catheters, infusion sets) are

included.

– Add oral forms of artemisinin-based combination therapy for malaria treatment to ensure

comprehensive care options.

2.1 Forecasting

Forecasting is the process of estimating the quantities of products that will be consumed or
distributed based on planned activities. It considers many variables, such as the project context,
medical guidelines and protocols, and historical consumption dataa. The forecasting process
provides the foundation for ordering and budgeting activities related to the procurement of
medical products.

2.1.1 Roles and responsibilities

Successful forecasting involves more than just numerical analysis; it relies on effective
communication and sound organisation. Relevant team members must allocate adequate time
to perform this task. When initiating the forecasting process, the roles and responsibilities
should be clearly outlined.

The table below shows the common and simple setupb in MSF. For further guidance in different
contexts, refer to HQ.

Table 2.1 - Forecasting - most common setup within MSF projects

(MAM)

• Project pharmacy
manager

data.

• In collaboration with the medical and biomed/
log teams, undertakes forecasting according to the
operational strategy.

• Medical stock manager
(medical or log/supply
team)

• Provides visibility of the past outgoing movements as
well as current or past stockoutsc.

• Pharmacy coordinator
• Coordinates all the process steps, ensuring the
forecasting process is aligned with the order
chronogram.

• Offers guidance and technical support.

• Supports the medical coordinator in the revision of the
proposed forecasted monthly consumption (FMC)d or
maximum stock level for each product in the MSL.

a Consumption data reflects the demand and utilisation patterns of medical products. Consumption data is used

to calculate a fundamental metric like AMC (average monthly consumption).
b There may be additional contributors involved in the forecasting process. However, to maintain simplicity, the

table considers only the primary roles.
c Stockout refers to a situation in which stock levels for a particular product within the MSL are depleted, making

it impossible to fulfil the operational demands for that product.
d Forecasted monthly consumption: quantity of a product estimated to be consumed during an upcoming

demand (medical advices).

2.1.2 Forecasting preparation

Ensure understanding of the medical strategy and obtain collective insights

To conduct the forecasting process, it is imperative to ensure that the entire medical team
has a comprehensive understanding of the medical strategy and operational activities agreed
upon with HQ (e.g., types of medical activities, activities start date, target population). Such
alignment fosters consistency and enhances the efficacy of the forecasting process.

Moreover, it is very important to integrate a variety of perspectives into the process. The entire
medical team should be involved, with particular emphasis on locally hired staff (LHS) who
possess an in-depth understanding of the project's history.

Remember to integrate input concerning medical products from the biomedical and

laboratory departments, as well as from any other relevant departments.

The outcome of these processes should be reflected in the revision of the MSL, which is a
critical prerequisite for forecasting. For more information, see Chapter 1.

Analyse historical data

Examining historical data facilitates the following:

– Identifying seasonal variations or other possible trends: it can guide in determining which

past period should serve as a reference for forecasting, which may vary for each product.

– Detecting atypical consumption: it is important to recognise if consumption patterns are

unusual. It is crucial to be aware of changes in medical activities or protocols, as well as
whether a product was in stockout or used as a substitute and overconsumed in the past.

– Adjusting the average monthly consumption (AMC)e:

• Whenever anomalies are identified, it is essential to correct the AMC and define an
adjusted AMC (AAMCf). Not doing so might result in incorrect estimates, leading to
unintended overstockg or stockout.

• When analysing substantial fluctuations in the AMC, it is important to consider factors
such as changes in protocols, shifts in activity levels, variations in the number of cases,

e Average monthly consumption: metric that calculates the average quantity of a product consumed over a

specific period, typically measured in months.
f AAMC is a refined version of the AMC that excludes potential anomalies, such as stockouts, encoding errors

and other significant situations in consumption patterns (e.g., higher consumption of a product used as a
substitute when another product is in stockout).
g Situation where the quantity of products in stock significantly exceeds the expected consumption within a

defined period, resulting in an excessive amount of stock.

Chapter 2: Quantification: forecasting, budgeting and ordering

35

2
adjustments in admission criteria, or other relevant factors.

• Months in which consumption data is highly influenced by significant one-time activity
changes should be excluded from the calculation.

• Whenever possible, using real consumptionh data is preferable over relying solely on
metrics, such as AMC. See more information in Chapter 6.

It is critical to consider past or current stockouts, especially for consumable products,

as they reduce the AMC of the affected products while increasing the AMC of their
substitutes. Neglecting to do so could negatively impact the forecasting estimation.

Importance of data quality


The quality of data directly impacts the effectiveness and reliability of the forecasting

process, particularly the adjusted-consumption method. Investing in data quality, such

as data cleansing, verification, and validation, is essential for improving forecast

accuracy.


This practically entails:
• Assessing potential limitations affecting data quality (e.g., possible gaps in human
resources).

• Ensuring the dataset's completeness and determining if additional supervision is
necessary (e.g., due to new staff handling data).

• Addressing outlier data points (e.g., managing erratic consumption patterns resulting
from stockouts, manual encoding mistakes, etc.).

2.1.3 Forecasting methods

Choosing the appropriate forecasting method is crucial for accurately anticipating needs,
minimising the risk of overstocking or stockouts, reducing MSF program costs, and enhancing
MSF's operational efficiency.

The choice of forecasting method will depend on several factors, including data availability and
reliability, the type of medical program, and the history of the MSF program.

Remember

The forecasting process is not bound by a single, universal formula. While some

statistical models may help predict future demand, a thorough understanding of

contextual factors – such as medical strategy, potential constraints, and other relevant

considerations – remains essential.

h Quantity of a medical product that is used for or given to patients. (e.g., when a hospital pharmacy dispenses

medications directly to patients or when medical products are utilised in end-user units).

Chapter 2: Quantification: forecasting, budgeting and ordering

36

Adjusted-consumption method

This method is commonly employed in non-emergency projects where reliable consumption
data is available. For each product, an FMC is established.

Step 1 - Collect and organise historical data

– Extract the monthly consumption data for each product over the past months (3, 6, 12

months, etc.) or for the equivalent coverage period (CP)i from the previous year.

– Compare the list of products collected with the MSL.

Step 2 - Analyse historical data

– Previous medical orders or budget estimations may serve as foundational elements and

play a crucial role.

– Evaluate diseases with seasonal patterns by considering consumption figures during both

the epidemiological peak period (highest AMC) and the off-peak period (lowest AMC).

– Assess the cohort’s evolution (growth, stability, decrease) with respect to chronic diseases.

Step 3 - Estimate FMC

– Based on the previous analysis (step 2) and considering significant changes in operational

activities.

Morbidity and cohort-based method

This method uses the theoretical quantity needed for the treatment of specific diseases.

Step 1 - Collect and organise historical data or expected morbidity data

– Collect all data concerning patient attendance, disease incidence, etc. Data sources may

include software used in MSF for managing epidemiologic or clinical data, other NGOs, the
Ministry of Health (MoH), or other MSF sections.

– Compare the above data with medical guidelines and protocols currently in use that must

be followed by the medical teams.

– In cases of unreliable or unavailable data, consider data from similar health facilities in

terms of patient demographics, morbidity, and treatment practices, preferably within the
same region or country.

Step 2 - Analyse historical data

– Evaluate the current and the expected growth of the patient cohort.

– Assess disease seasonal fluctuations and possible forthcoming medical activities.

– When pertinent, reflect on past activity changes, such as activity opening, closing, or

adjustments in projected patient counts.

Step 3 - Estimate FMC

– Based on the previous analysis (step 2) and considering significant changes in operational

activities.

i Coverage period, it refers to the time period (usually expressed in months) that should cover the forecasted

needs between two consecutive order receptions.

Fixed quantity method (maximum stock level)

A maximum stock levelj is established instead of estimating an FMC. This method is typically
employed in the following situations:

– Specific settings such as initial stages of emergencies, new projects, vaccination campaigns,

EPREPk, etc.

– When the consumption may be unpredictable or challenging to estimate.

Examples of products for which this method is applied:

– Products for laboratory and those used for medical equipment maintenance.

– Treatments dependent on patient/case numbers, such as e.g., post-exposure prophylaxis,

treatments for rabies or tetanus, snake exposures, etc.

– Products located in prepositioned stocks such as EPREP, first aid kits, emergency boxes, etc.

A limitation inherent in this methodology is its inability to accurately capture


prescription behaviours or address challenges arising from deficient stock management.

Step 1 - Collect and organise historical data

– A clear scenario should be described, including the number of patients involved. This

scenario should account for relevant medical protocols and possible medical responses.

– Regarding laboratory products, medical equipment, and consumables, input from

laboratory and biomedical staff should be included.

– In vaccination campaigns, the target population and the desired vaccination coverage must

be evaluated.

– Concerning antidotes or anti-venom products, previous incidents of snake bites can serve

as a reference point.

j Commonly named as maximum stock or quota.
k Emergency preparedness plan.

Chapter 2: Quantification: forecasting, budgeting and ordering

38

Step 2 - Analyse historical data

– Adjustments must be made based on the maximum stock level during peak seasons.

– Evaluate possible changes in activities that have occurred in the past, including but not

limited to the initiation or closure of activities, among others.

Step 3 - Estimate fixed quantity

– Based on the previous analysis (step 2) and considering significant changes in operational

activities.

2.1.4 Forecast validation

By the end of the forecasting process, each product included in the MSL should have a validated
FMC and/or a maximum stock level. The validation process is established by each MSF section
and generally necessitates approval at all levels: project, coordination, and headquarters.

Remember

In some countries MSF must submit their annual forecast for the import of

controlled drugs, and precursors to the MoH for integration into the national quota

requested via the International Narcotics Control Board (INCB).

2.1.5 Forecasting updates

When there are substantial changes in context or medical activities, forecast deviations may
occur. It is crucial to verify whether these changes could lead to significant discrepancies in
future consumption patterns. This proactive approach is necessary to anticipate potential
issues such as stockouts or overstocks.

Adjustments to the forecast may become necessary due to various factors, including:

– Changes in activities: increase/decrease, opening/closing, etc.

– Shifts in admission criteria: new diseases, adjustments in age groups, etc.

– Amendments to medical protocols.

– Introduction of new equipment: requiring specific consumables (e.g., after nursing care

training, safety IV catheters are recommended for use over non-safety IV catheters).

– Seasonal variations: such as malaria treatment during peak seasons, increased use of

certain antibiotics or vaccines in winter, etc.

Chapter 2: Quantification: forecasting, budgeting and ordering

40

2.2 Budgeting the purchase of medical products

Once a year, MSF projects are required to estimate their budgets for the upcoming year. This
section highlights key budget considerations and provides guidance on estimating financial
expenditures for medical products, which constitute a fundamental component of an MSF
project's medical budget.

2.2.1 Budget-related considerations

The budget is a tool that ensures financial resources are allocated efficiently and supports
strategic decision-making for MSF projects to achieve their operational goals. While the budget
aids in planning, monitoring, and managing MSF projects, it should not be seen as a limitation.
The budget does not dictate project activities; rather, project activities define the budget.

Forecasting (see Section 2.1) and budgeting processes are closely interconnected, as forecasting
provides the data and insights needed to estimate expenditures for medical products.
The budget must be developed based on forecasted conditions and realistic assumptions.
Intentional underestimation or overestimation could disrupt the entire process and should be
avoided.

Additionally, the budget is an essential tool for validating medical orders (see Section 2.3.6). It
is used by the budget holderp–typically the medical coordinator–to ensure that expenditures
remain within the approved budget.

The standard period considered is a calendar year, beginning on 1 January and ending on 31
December. However, these dates may be adjusted to align with the project’s opening or closing
dates if they fall within the year under consideration.

2.2.2 How to estimate the annual expenditure on medical products

– Budgeting the annual expenditure on medical products for the upcoming year requires

an interdisciplinary approach that involves the participation of various stakeholders from
different departments (medical, supply/log, and finance), including the biomedical staff
when necessary. The responsibilities associated with this process can vary significantly
across MSF sections.

– All medical products should be categorised by accounting code. See Toolbox, Accounting

codes for purchase of medical products.

– Estimates should be based on updated reference prices for each product, as provided by

either MSF or external suppliers.

The associated costs typically included in the logistics budget, such as transportation

expenses, customs fees, and import taxes, are not addressed in this guideline. Refer to
the HQ referent for further guidance.

p The budget holder is the person responsible for managing and overseeing a specific budget or portion of the

budget. The budget holder ensures that spending stays within the approved budget, makes decisions about
allocating resources, and is accountable for the financial performance of their budget area to the Head of
Mission.

Chapter 2: Quantification: forecasting, budgeting and ordering

41

2
Estimation methods

The approach to budgeting the annual expenditure on medical products varies across MSF
sections, each of which may use different tools and parameters. Overall, two main methods
are recognised:

12-month forecasting method
The annual expenditure for each product is estimated by multiplying the FMC for the upcoming
year by 12 and then by the unit price. Parameters such as physical stock, backorders, and
products likely to expire are not considered, based on the assumption that these factors will
balance themselves out over time.

This method is straightforward and relatively easy to apply compared to the order simulation
method. However, it may not be entirely suitable for non-consumable products or in cases
where significant overstock or sleeping stock issues exist.

Order simulation method
The annual expenditure for each product is estimated by simulating the total cost of the
medical orders expected to be placed by the project in the following year. See Section 2.3.4.

This method takes into account various variables, such as physical stock, backorders, and
products likely to expire. In principle, it is more accurate than the previous method. However,
it introduces complexity and increases the risk of errors. The advantage of this method is that
it applies to all types of medical products, both consumable and non-consumable, and the
estimation is not influenced by overstocks or sleeping stocks.

Note

• The parameters used in this method (e.g., physical inventory, backorders, products likely
to expire) should reflect the estimated values at the time orders are expected to be
placed for the following year, rather than the values at the time the budget is calculated.

• All planned medical orders anticipated for the upcoming year must be considered, even
if they cannot be invoiced within that same year.

2.2.3 Mid-year budget revision

A mid-year budget revision is a process where an MSF project reviews and, if necessary,
adjusts its budget halfway through the calendar year. This revision allows the project to adapt
to changes in its financial situation, operational needs, or external conditions that have arisen
since the budget was initially set.

During this period, the medical team assesses whether the cost of the remaining planned
medical orders for the rest of the year will align with the project budget, or if, on the contrary,
it will be excessive or insufficient.

Mid-year budget revisions help keep medical product spending aligned with the current
realities of the project.

Chapter 2: Quantification: forecasting, budgeting and ordering

42

2.3 Ordering

The ordering process ensures the systematic replenishment of the medical stock. The principles
governing the process are the same irrespective of the supply source (MSF or external). The
process also remains consistent regardless of the stock positioning strategy set up in the MSF
program (centralised unallocated, centralised allocated, decentralised). The main distinction
arises in the scale of the quantities and volumes to be ordered.

2.3.1 Roles and responsibilities

The ordering process requires an interdisciplinary approach that involves participation from
staff members across different departments (medical and supply/log) and levels (projects,
MSF programs, HQ, etc.).

Effective communication and robust organisation are imperative. When undertaking the
ordering process, the responsibilities should be clearly outlined and well understood by
all stakeholders, along with the corresponding tasks and timelines. The assignment of
responsibilities can vary significantly across different MSF sections. Refer to the HQ referent
for further guidance.

2.3.2 Types of medical orders

In MSF, there is no standardised typology for classifying medical orders. Depending on the
supplier—either MSF supply centres or local suppliers—medical orders may be categorised
using different terms, each implying distinct service-level agreements (SLAs)q and may involve
a different validation process.

However, two key factors must be carefully considered:

– Alignment with order chronograms: whether the order is aligned with the specific order

chronogram designed for each MSF program, or if it is placed outside the established
schedule due to unexpected demands within the MSF program. These demands may result
from stockouts, planning oversights, or unforeseen events such as outbreaks or natural
disasters. Medical orders that are not aligned with the order chronogram should only
cover the quantities required until the next scheduled order within the chronogram.

– Expedited lead time: if a shorter lead time is required in comparison to the standard lead

time agreed with the supplierr, regardless of the alignment with the order chronogram, a
specified delivery date must be provided.

q Formal contract that defines the expected performance, responsibilities, and key metrics between a provider

and a customer. It outlines measurable targets such as response times, uptime guarantees, and issue resolution
deadlines.
r Priority is requested for the order.

Chapter 2: Quantification: forecasting, budgeting and ordering

43

2
Emergency orders

These orders are submitted to MSF supply centres exclusively in response to an

emergency within a country. They are authorised by the emergency cell/desk and are

given special high-priority treatment. Typically, they are processed with a reduced lead

time, as the MSF supply centre utilises emergency stock and may bypass standard

ordering procedures when necessary.

2.3.3 Order prerequisites

Order chronogram

An order chronogram is a timeline or schedule that outlines the key events and milestones
involved in handling a specific order. This tool is essential for tracking and managing the
sequence of activities within a specific order cycles used by each MSF section.

The order chronogram is crucial for effective planning as well as for:

– Communication and coordination: it serves as a communication tool, ensuring that all

stakeholders are aware of the timing of key activities.

– Resource allocation: it allows different teamst to allocate resources effectively by

scheduling when the main tasks are expected (e.g., giving the medical team visibility to
prepare for internal discussions regarding the next order).

Interdepartmental collaboration

Interdepartmental collaboration and teamwork between the medical and supply/log

teams are vital. The order chronogram must be defined and well-known by all the

teams involved in the ordering process.

Any substantial alteration to the order chronogram should be promptly communicated

to all the teams involved.

Adherence to the order chronogram helps ensure orders arrive on time, alleviating pressure
on the supply chain. The MSF program must make every effort to follow this schedule, as the
continuity of medical activities depends on it.

A project order chronogram should span the entire year. This approach helps the different
stakeholders involved plan their activities or tasks accordingly, thereby contributing to effective
interdepartmental coordination.

The chosen mode of transport should be considered in the order chronogram. For example, air
and sea transport differ in transit times and may be subject to distinct pre-clearance procedures.
Likewise, it may be necessary to establish a specific chronogram for certain medical products
with special considerations (e.g., short-shelf-life products or controlled drugs).

s The order cycle encompasses the main milestones involved in the ordering process determined by each

MSF section. It may include milestones like physical inventory, order calculation, order validation, order
confirmation, cargo preparation, cargo transport, custom clearance and cargo reception.
t Implying medical, supply/log teams.

Chapter 2: Quantification: forecasting, budgeting and ordering

44

The order chronogram must outline important fundamental parameters in the ordering
process:

Lead time (LT)
The time span from the stock dateu to the reception of the order has been completed, and the
order status is marked as closedv.

Lead time is invariably influenced by the chosen mode of transport. Other factors may also
affect lead time, including the regulatory framework for importation, the pre-clearance
process, and the geopolitical context in the region.

Coverage Period (CP)
Time period (usually expressed in months) that should cover the forecasted needs between
two consecutive order receptions.

The duration of the CP is strategically determined by factors such as stock management and
storage capacity, compliance with good storage and distribution practices (GSDP), human
resources, consumption stability, security considerations, regulatory import requirements,
difficulty in ensuring a reliable forecast, product shelf life, etc.

A longer CP results in extended order cycles, which require more accurate forecasting and
increased storage capacity, thereby requiring additional resources and expertise. A longer CP
may also reduce the ability to adapt to demand while increasing the risk of both overstock and
stockouts.

Security Stock (SS)
Security stock, also known as safety stock, may not necessarily be outlined in the order
chorogram but it plays an important role in the order calculation. It refers to the quantity of
stock that should be readily available to address unforeseen circumstances, such as delivery
delays or unexpected spikes in consumption. It can alternatively be expressed in months of
consumption. SS is intended to prevent stockouts and under normal circumstances, it should
remain unused until the reception of the next order.

Several factors can significantly influence the determination of SS. For instance, a larger SS may
be required if forecasting becomes challenging, supply is unpredictable, LTs are very long or
unreliable, or if the products are considered critical by the MSF program.

Keep in mind that increasing the SS could affect the budget and the volume of


products to store.

u Stock date is the date when physical stock data is extracted from the stock management tool. This date should

typically be the same date of physical count, unless there is a reason that hinders that.
v An order is considered closed once it has been verified and confirmed that all the products specified in the

medical order have been received at the destination defined by the MSF program. An order may be delivered
in several shipments over a period and will only be considered closed once all products have been received at
the final destination.
