MSL revised

The MSL should be consistent with the medical operational strategy. If the MSL has not been
revised for a substantial period or if there have been changes to the project's medical strategy
since the last revision, it is essential to conduct a thorough review of the MSL. This review is
crucial to avoid errors such as overlooking essential products or ordering obsolete products.
For more information, see Chapter 1.

Identification of products with special considerations

Products with short shelf life: it is prudent to order them more frequently (e.g., every two
or three months) while reducing order quantities and using faster transportation methods.
Additionally, reducing stock levels may be advisable. It is recommended that a specific
chronogram for these products be implemented in every MSF program.

Products with complex importation procedures: certain products (e.g., controlled drugs, some
laboratory reagents, etc) may require additional steps in the importation process compared
to most other products. It may be advisable to reduce the order frequency for these products,
provided they are not short-shelf-life items.

For more information, see Chapter 4.

Sources of supply: certain products may follow different supply paths, requiring individualised
ordering and monitoring. For example, products supplied through external organisations
or agencies such as UNICEF, the Global Fund, WHO, or even the MoH. In other cases, some
products may be subject to import restrictions within the country, compelling the MSF program
to source them from the local market.

Chapter 2: Quantification: forecasting, budgeting and ordering

46

2.3.4 Medical order calculation

When calculating the quantities of medical products to order, it is important to distinguish
between consumable and non-consumable products. This approach enables a more precise
calculation, thereby reducing the risk of stockouts or overstocks.

The formulas used and any necessary adaptations will vary by each MSF section. Refer to HQ
for more information.

Standard formula

Figure 2.3 – Standard formula

What I order

Consumable
products

Quantity to order
FMC x (LT + CP +SS) - Physical Stock - BO ± RE ± Do + EXP

Non-consumable
products
Quantity to order
Maximum Stock - Physical Stock - BO ± RE ± Do + EXP

Consumable
product
Products intended for regular use, with a forecasted monthly consumption (FMC)
defined (e.g., paracetamol tablets in a primary health care project)

Non-consumable
product
Products not typically used on a regular basis, for which a maximum stock level is
established instead of an FMC (e.g., hospital beds, spare parts of medical
equipment, some lab items, etc.).

FMC
Forecasted monthly consumption: An estimate of the required quantity per
month for a product based on upcoming planned activities, including outgoing
regular planned donations.

Maximum Stock
It is the maximum quantity to be reached during replenishment for products
without regular consumption (non-consumable products).

Physical Stock
Multiple medical stocks may exist within an MSF program or MSF project, spread
across different locations. When a project operates in multiple stock locations, it
is essential to account for all available stock during the ordering process,
including the products close to expiry.

LT
Lead time: Typically expressed in a number of months.

CP
Coverage period: Typically expressed in a number of months.

SS
Security stock: Typically expressed in a number of months.

BO
Backorders refers to products that have been ordered but have not yet been received.

RE
Reimbursements: It refers to borrowed products or return of loans to be received (-) or
outgoing loans or borrowed products to be supplied (+) during the period to cover (LT + CP).
To properly account for these transactions, they must be confirmed. Otherwise, it is
advisable not to include them in the calculation.

DO
Donations: It refers to donations expected to be received (-) or to be supplied (+) during the
period to cover (LT + CP).

EXP
Quantity of product that will not be used and will expire during the LT and CP, and during
the potential period to cover by the SS. It must not be considered as physical stock.

=

What I have
-
What I need
=

=

Source: MSF

Important aspects to consider

– The quality of data directly impacts the effectiveness and reliability of the order

calculation. Using incorrect physical stock levels can lead to errors, potentially resulting
in stockouts or overstocks. Therefore, it is essential to carry out a full physical count of all
medical products before placing medical orders.

– If a stockout occurs during LT, with no pipeline, loans, BO, return of loans, borrowed

products or donations received covering the full lead time, the formula can be simplified
as: FMC × (CP + SS) – BO ± RE ± DO to prevent overstock, as the products will not be
consumed during the LT. Both LT and Physical Stock are neglected. Also, when a stockout
has occurred, critical thinking is essential, and a case-by-case analysis is recommended.
For further adaptations of the basic formula calculation, please refer to the HQ referent.
Stockouts occurring during LT are easier to identify using the advanced formula (see
below).

Advanced formula (only for consumable products)
The quantity to order for consumable products may also be determined using a variation of
the standard formula. This variation incorporates the concept of the projected available stock
(PAS), which represents the theoretical quantity of a specific product that will remain in stock
upon reception of the next order.

Chapter 2: Quantification: forecasting, budgeting and ordering

47

2
The order calculation is split into two steps:

Figure 2.4 – Advanced formula (only for consumable products)

Step 1
PAS
Physical Stock – (FMCLT x LT) + BOLT ± RELT ± DOLT - EXPLT

Step 2
Quantity to order
[(FMCCP x CP) + (FMCCP x SS)] – PAS – BOCP ± RECP ± DOCP + EXPCP

PAS
Projected available stock: The theoretical quantity of a specific product that will remain in
stock upon the receipt of the order being calculated.

FMCLT
FMC during the lead time period.

BOLT
Backorders during the lead time period.

RELT
Reimbursements during the lead time period.

DOLT
It refers to donations expected to be received (-) or to be supplied (+) during the lead
time period.

EXPLT
Quantity of product that will not be used and will expire during the lead time period.

FMCCP
FMC during the lead time period.

BOCP
Backorders during the coverage time period.

RECP
Reimbursements during the coverage time period.

DOCP
It refers to donations expected to be received (-) or to be supplied (+) during the coverage
period.

EXPCP
Quantity of products that will not be used and expire during the coverage time period.

=

=

Advanced formula (only for consumable products)

Source: MSF

Although this variation increases the complexity of the calculation, it facilitates the identification
of potential stockouts that may occur prior to the arrival of the next order. It helps to evaluate
the need to place a medical order outside the order chronogram.

– High risk of stockout: PAS upon reception is < 0.

– Medium risk of stockout: PAS upon reception is > 0 but remains below the SS level.

Important aspects to consider

– The calculation of the PAS (step 1) is based on LT and should consider the FMC estimated

during LT (FMCLT). In contrast, the quantity to order (step 2) is determined by the CP and
should utilise the FMC estimated during CP (FMCCP). Note that FMCLT and FMCCP may be
identical sometimes.

– When the calculation of the PAS (step 1) results in a negative number, the PAS should

be adjusted to zero to prevent ordering an excessive amount in the quantity-to-order
calculation.

– While the FMCCP is most commonly used in SS calculation (SS x FMCCP), any other FMC

value might be used if it aligns better with the context.

– In certain special situations, the FMC's estimate of a product can drastically vary during

the LT and CP periods. For instance, the consumption of vitamin A, oral rehydration salts,
and tetracycline ointment could be substantially high during the initial stages of a measles
epidemic and then decrease thereafter. In such scenarios, multiple FMCs can be estimated
to reflect month-to-month changes in consumption, with corresponding adjustments to
the LT and CP.

Chapter 2: Quantification: forecasting, budgeting and ordering

48

Final verification

The person tasked with the order calculation should double-check the quantities to order. This
step is imperative to guarantee precision and mitigate the risk of potential errors. Reviewing
medical orders line by line requires attention and sufficient time to perform this task effectively.

Extra attention should be given to:

– Unusual quantities: for example, products for which quantity-to-order is ≤ zero.

– Vitalw (V) or essentialx (E) products: these products should be carefully double-checked,

and special attention should be paid to the SS.

– New products: these products should hold a FMCCP> 0 or max stock level > 0. Make sure

related products are not forgotten (e.g., medical equipment, medications with their
corresponding antidotes, reagents for tests, etc.).

– Products likely to be in stockout during LT: consider the possible impact on other products

that can be used as alternatives.

– Products likely to expire during LT and CP: verify the calculation of expiries.

– Non-MSL products: double-check that FMCCP= 0 or Stock max = 0 unless solid justification.

– Products with zero demand: double-check that products with FMC=0 or max stock levels =

0 are not part of the order.

– Top-cost products: monitor the products with the highest cost to ensure budget

alignment.

Once final verification is complete, the medical coordinator typically assumes responsibility
for analysing the content and value of the medical orders. The pharmacy coordinator can
support the medical coordinator by identifying specific products that require special attention
(e.g., products not listed in the MSL or MML, products reflecting budget deviations, whether
undervalued or overvalued, etc).

To facilitate the revision process, it is advisable to categorise the various medical products
ordered using classifications like the VEDy classification (see Chapter 1, Selection), the ABC
Classificationz, or the WHO therapeutic classification.aa

During the analysis of medical orders, the medical coordinator may consider making

adjustments to align the orders with the medical strategy and operational activities
agreed upon with the HQ. The project team must stay informed of any modifications to the
original order.

w A product that is critical for life-saving interventions, with no suitable substitutes (e.g., anaesthesia products,

adrenaline).
x A product that is critical for the continuation of core medical activities. A short-term stockout would not halt

activities.
y Vital, essential and desirable.
z It is a product categorisation system that divides products into A, B, and C categories, with A representing

high-value products, B for moderate-value products, and C for low-value products. Refer to HQ for more
information.
aa Anatomical Therapeutic Chemical (ATC) Classification. www.who.int. https://www.who.int/tools/atc-ddd-

toolkit/atc-classification

Chapter 2: Quantification: forecasting, budgeting and ordering

49

2
2.3.5 Purchase order

A purchase order (PO) is a formal document issued by the MSF program to a supplier to request
goods or services. It serves as an official offer to purchase and outlines the specific products
the MSF program wants to order, along with the purchase terms and conditions.

The PO must be encoded into an appropriate system designated by each MSF section. The
main elements of a PO include, but are not limited to, the following:

– Product description: detailed descriptions of each product, including product codes and

quantities.

– Order number: each order should be assigned a proper chronological order reference

number, which will be consistent across all related documents.

– Order date and requested delivery date: it should be communicated to the supplier (MSF

or external).

– Means of transport: indicating the preferred mode of transport helps the supplier arrange

the appropriate shipping schedule.

– Dispatch address: including the exact dispatch address ensures that the supplier knows

where to send the goods.

2.3.6 Order validation

The workflow related to order validation, including the roles and responsibilities involved, is
established by each MSF section. This process involves verifying and confirming the eligibility
of orders based on two essential elements:

– Content: ensuring that the products specified in the order align with the needs of the MSF

program.

– Budget: the estimated value of the order should be cross-checked with the medical

budget, considering the various accounting codes. Any discrepancies must be
appropriately justified and addressed through discussions with the medical coordination
team and ultimately with HQ if required.

2.3.7 Order quotation

In response to the order, the supplier issues an order proforma, also known as a proforma
invoice. This is a preliminary or estimated invoice issued before the actual transaction is
confirmed.

The pro forma invoice helps both parties (the supplier and the MSF program) align their
expectations regarding the order, and minimises the risk of misunderstandings by:

– Specifying order details and terms: the document outlines specific order information, such

as product descriptions, quantities, unit prices, reference number, etc.

– Providing the total price of the goods on the pro forma invoice: this allows the MSF

program to review and approve costs before committing fully to the transaction.

Chapter 2: Quantification: forecasting, budgeting and ordering

50

Note

• The pro forma invoice is not a final invoice and is not legally binding.

• If the original order has been modified (e.g., quantities, products, requested delivery
date, etc), the changes will be reflected in the pro forma invoice.

• A pro forma invoice is usually divided into multiple pro formas. This practice is often
necessary due to various factors, such as product characteristics (e.g., short shelf life,
cold-chain requirements, dangerous goods, controlled drugs), logistical constraints (e.g.,
regulatory import requirements), sourcing preferences, and transportation methods.

• Orders placed by MSF programs to the Amsterdam Procurement Unit (APU) do not go
through the quotation process.

2.3.8 Order confirmation

Every pro forma invoice must be confirmed by the MSF program or HQ cell/desk. The
responsibility of this task is determined by each MSF section.

An expected delivery date is defined at this stage. This date is influenced by several factors, such
as the SLA in place, stock availability at the supplier’s warehouse, and the context surrounding
the medical order (e.g., emergency orders placed to MSF supply centres have shorter LT), etc.

2.3.9 Cargo preparation

It refers to the period required for the supplier to prepare an order ready for shipment. This
stage typically encompasses the following activities:

– Procuring goods if the products are not available in the supplier's warehouse.

– Picking, packing, and organising the products for dispatch.

– Collecting all necessary documentation, including the invoice, packing list, and, when

required, any regulatory paperwork necessary for customs clearance.

– If the shipment involves export or import, prepare and submit the relevant documentation

to customs authorities in advance.

Once all necessary processes are complete and the goods are awaiting pickup, loading, or
transportation, the cargo enters ready-to-ship (RTS) status.
Multiple cargoes destined for the same location can be combined into a single, larger shipment.
This process is known as cargo consolidation.

2.3.10 Cargo transport

After the cargo reaches RTS status, it is still necessary to obtain approval from the MSF program
to receive the shipment, and the pre-clearance process must be secured for export or import.
Only then can the supplier arrange transportation and provide a confirmed delivery date.

The supplier determines the optimal routes, arranges carriers, and coordinates schedules
to meet order requirements (e.g., expected delivery date). The supplier must ensure the
appropriate transport conditions for the medical products and the continuous tracking of the
shipment.

Chapter 2: Quantification: forecasting, budgeting and ordering

51

2
2.3.11 Customs clearance (import)

If the cargo transportation requires importation, customs clearance is a crucial step. Customs
clearance is the process of obtaining official authorisation from customs authorities for goods
to enter or leave a country. This procedure involves preparing and submitting the required
documentation and paying any applicable duties, taxes, and fees to the customs authorities.

For more information, see Chapter 4.

2.3.12 Cargo reception

Order reception is described in Chapter 5, GSDP and medical stock management, see Section
5.10.1

2.3.13 Monitoring medical orders

Order monitoring refers to tracking in-progress orders (no closed orders). It requires the active
involvement of various stakeholders across multiple levels:

– Suppliers (either MSF or external) must provide regular updates on the order status and

promptly alert stakeholders in case of any deviations (e.g., a change in the confirmed
delivery date).

– In general, the supply/log team is responsible for updating relevant tools and maintaining

effective communication with all stakeholders.

– The medical team relies on this updated information to make informed decisions.

Early alerts of potential deviations in orders are essential for medical teams to assess

possible impacts on medical activities and determine appropriate corrective actions.

3.1 Introduction

MSF is committed to providing high-quality medical care, a central tenet of its humanitarian
mission. This commitment is guided by medical ethics and a strong focus on patient dignity,
autonomy, and confidentiality. MSF aims to address quality of care in a comprehensive manner,
ensuring a holistic, integrated, and multidisciplinary approach.

MSF firmly believes that every patient, regardless of their location, is entitled to access
adequate and safe medical products, including specialised nutrition. Consequently, MSF places
the highest importance on the quality of these products throughout the entire supply chain.

Nevertheless, MSF is also aware that the quality of medical products is not always assured in
the global market. MSF adheres to internationally recognised standards to ensure the quality,
safety, and efficacy or performance of medical products throughout their entire life cycle. This
approach aligns with the evolving global and national regulatory frameworks and the quality
assurance policies of other humanitarian organisations and institutions that share MSF’s
principles.

To reinforce this commitment, MSF promotes a culture of quality awareness and accountability
across all levels of the organisation by adopting and implementing a quality assurancea
framework.

a Quality assurance for medical products is a comprehensive approach based on risk management and

continuous improvement that ensures products are high quality, safe for use, and compliant with regulatory
standards, thereby protecting patient health and safety. It relies on a quality management system. For MSF, it
encompasses various aspects, including validation of sources and suppliers, management of quality complaints
and recalls, and post-market surveillance.

Chapter 3: Quality assurance in procurement

56

3.2 MSF internal framework

MSF has implemented internal policies to ensure compliance with legal requirements and ethical
principles in its procurement activities and the management of medical product donations.
These policies promote transparency and accountability in the procurement process, ensure
the quality of medical products, achieve cost savings, and manage risks effectively.

– MSF Quality Assurance Policy for Medical Products and Specialised Foods (under revision

- see Toolbox): this policy states that MSF adheres to recognised international standards to
ensure the quality, safety, and efficacy or performance of medical products. This approach
aligns with the evolution of global and national regulatory frameworks, as well as the
quality assurance policies of other humanitarian organisations and institutions that share
MSF's principles.

Any medical product that is not considered quality-assured according to MSF’s quality
assurance policy, particularly concerning manufacturers or distributors, is considered a
deviation from MSF policy. Such products shall be procured only after a comprehensive
risk analysis. If the results indicate that no viable alternative is available due to operational
constraints, procurement shall be authorised. In all cases, approval must be obtained from
the Medical Director or, if delegated, from the designated pharmacist.

This chapter was finalised while the updated MSF Quality Assurance Policy


was still under revision and had not yet been officially endorsed. The content

has been aligned as closely as possible with the latest drafts and discussions available

at the time of writing, based on input from key stakeholders involved in the QA policy

revision process.

– MSF global procurement policy (2025 - see Toolbox): this policy outlines the fundamental

principlesb that MSF must follow to ensure that procurement activities are conducted in
accordance with the core MSF values as defined in the Charter, the Chantilly Principles,
and the La Mancha Agreement.

– The policy outlines a seven-step procurement process that must be followed to ensure

procurement activities align with the principles outlined in the policy.

– MSF policy for in-kind donationsc of medical products & health technology (2017 - see

Toolbox): MSF does not accept in-kind donations of medical products or health technology.
Under exceptional circumstances, and on a case-by-case basis, MSF may consider
accepting such donations according to the criteria and conditions outlined in this policy.
The policy is in accordance with MSF’s Corporate Fundraising Statement and MSF Quality
Assurance Policy for medical products and specialised food.

– MSF policy on donation of medicines (2012 - see Toolbox): this policy outlines the

primary considerations to take into account for both outbound and inbound donations of
medications. managed by MSF programs.

b Effectiveness, efficiency, accountability, quality, risk mitigation, ethics and sustainable procurement.
c In-kind donation refers to the provision, free of charge or below production cost, of medical products and

health technologies by a commercial entity or its affiliated non-profit organisations. In-kind donations are
overseen by MSF Operational Directorates and not by MSF programs.

3.3 Quality-assured products in MSF programs

MSF is committed to providing patients with the highest quality of care by ensuring the
quality, safety, and efficacy or performance of medical products. From a supply standpoint,
medical products must be quality-assured, meaning they comply with international standards
as stipulated in MSF policy. Any medical product that does not fully comply with those
requirements is considered a deviation from MSF policies and requires a risk analysis before it
can be procured.

MSF shall use quality-assured medical products that comply with internationally recognised
standards. Compliance is verified by MSF QA teams through the evaluation of:

– The product and manufacturers’ dossier.

– The suppliers’ GSDP status, whether they are MSF supply centres or external suppliersd.

Compliance is determined by both the MSF QA teams' evaluation of the product dossier and
audits.

3.3.1 Requirements for products and manufacturers

The product and manufacturer qualification process includes:

– Verification of product specifications, ensuring alignment with MSF specifications

developed by technical and disease experts and practitioners, in accordance with their
clinical relevance, performance, robustness, and user-friendliness, in line with MSF
standard protocols.

– Evaluation of the product and the manufacturer's compliance with requirements set by

Highly Regulated Countries (HRC) and WHO/FAOe standards.

• Product Dossier Review (composition, formulation, regulatory approval status,
pharmacopoeia standards, stability studies, bioequivalence data ...).

• GMP certification from recognised regulatory bodies or GMP audit performed by a
habilitated auditor and review of past inspections, compliance records and history of
recalls, non-conformities or quality alerts.

Qualification processes are defined for each product type based on risk assessment. These
processes are defined, owned, and coordinated by the IO QA coordination and are detailed in
procedures.

d A supplier can be a wholesaler or a distributor.
e Food and Agriculture Organization

Chapter 3: Quality assurance in procurement

58

3.3.2 Requirements for suppliers

MSF prioritises procurement through qualified MSF supply centres, as per the MSF QA Policy
(see policy for details). This ensures that all quality assurance measures are continuously
monitored and enforced. MSF can buy nutrition-specialised food directly from qualified
suppliers when the following criteria are met:

– Be assessed and validated by HQ pharmacy referents

– Source MSF-validated products/manufacturers

– Comply with national requirements

– Comply with WHO Good Storage and Distribution Practices for medical products

MSF also requires its suppliers to implement a traceability system and establish procedures
for monitoring quality complaints reported by MSF projects, and initiate product recalls when
needed.

The process for qualifying MSF suppliers is defined, managed, and coordinated by the IO QA
team and detailed in the procedures.

Qualified MSF supply centres providing quality-assured products

• Any medical product sourced from a qualified MSF supply centre*—MSF Logistique
(MSF Log), MSF Supply, and the Amsterdam Procurement Centre (APU), along with
their affiliated regional hubs such as Dubai MSF Logistique and MSF Supply Kenya—is
considered quality-assured, as MSF maintains strict control over the supply chain and
related processes.

• For products sourced from other suppliers, quality assurance can only be confirmed
after verifying that the product, manufacturer, and supplier meet MSF’s quality criteria.

*The list of MSF supply centres and hubs is valid as of 2025 and may be subject to updates.

Chapter 3: Quality assurance in procurement

59

3

3.4 Deviations in MSF programs

3.4.1 Potential risks

The primary risks associated with deviations are as follows:

– Patient and public health: the lack of quality in medical products may include sub- or over-

dosage, poor bioavailability, poor stability, lack of sterility, lack of performance, presence
of impurities, cross-contamination with active principles, microbial contamination,
etc. Additionally, working with non-compliant distributors who do not adhere to GSDP
may result in failure to communicate batch recalls or quality alerts, improper storage
conditions, and product deterioration, which may further endanger patients and public
health.

– Legal: if medical products cause harm to patients due to defects, MSF may be held liable

for distributing these products. This is particularly relevant for imported or unregistered
products within the country.

– Reputational damage: when patients receive ineffective or harmful products, it

undermines their confidence in the medical care provided by MSF, potentially eroding the
trust of both national authorities and patients.

– Financial Losses: low-quality products can result in complications, leading to the need

for more extensive treatments, excessive replacement, repair, or consumption of medical
equipment. This, in turn, may increase the costs for MSF programs.

Furthermore, in most cases, deviations caused by unqualified suppliers lead to a lack of
full supply chain visibility, introducing additional risks, such as the presence of falsified or
substandard productsf. These risks are particularly concerning in countries where regulatory
oversight of the market is insufficiently stringent.

Additionally, the issue of double standards must be considered. A known manufacturer does
not guarantee the same quality across all markets. Products sourced from different suppliers or
regions may vary due to differences in regulatory controls, storage conditions, and distribution
practices.

Some local markets present higher risks than others, and the likelihood of purchasing a falsified
or substandard product depends on various factors. The risk associated with purchasing in
a remote village far from a country's capital is greater compared to buying directly from a
registered manufacturer. Generally, the more intermediaries involved in the supply chain, the
greater the risks assumed.

f A product that has been authorised by NMRAs but fails to meet either national or international quality

standards or specifications – or, in some cases, both.

Chapter 3: Quality assurance in procurement

60

3.4.2 MSF risk assessment

In certain situations, procuring quality-assured products that comply with international
standards can be challenging for MSF programs. In these cases, MSF still performs a risk analysis
to evaluate the potential impact on patient safety and public health before proceeding with
procurement. This analysis considers factors such as the product’s criticality, the availability of
alternatives, and operational constraints.

As part of this process, MSF performs a quality assessment focusing on two main elements:

– Good manufacturing practices (GMP)

– Good storage and distribution practices (GSDP)

As the product dossier is not available to MSF, this part of the assessment is missing, unless
proof is given that the dossier is identical to that of an MSF-approved product.

Deviation approval process

– According to the level of risk

• Low-risk deviations: can be validated by Section Pharmacists (SP), supported by
Intersectional Pharmacists (ISP).

• High-risk deviations: require exceptional validation by Medical Directors, based on SPs’
recommendations, supported by IO QA coordination. These validations are time-limited
and specific to a particular MSF section or context.

– According to the frequency or recurrence

• Ad hoc deviations: one-off deviations typically linked to urgent operational needs.

• Long-term deviations: may require a local market assessment (LMA) to identify
sustainable solutions. For high-risk deviations, there is always a time limitation,
depending on the context and/or MSF section.

As part of the approval process, MSF QA teams assess deviations by considering the product’s
registration status in HRCs and the country of use, the manufacturer’s compliance with
international standards and certifications, and the supplier’s adherence to GSDP, including
their history of recalls and quality alerts. The availability of alternative products that meet MSF
standards is also a determining factor in the decision-making process.
In all cases, the product or manufacturer assessment outcome always overrides the supplier’s
GSDP assessment outcome.

Important considerations

– This assessment cannot be qualitatively comparedg to that of quality-assured products.

The assessment of quality-assured products involves a much more stringent framework
and ensures a comprehensive evaluation of both the manufacturing site and the product.
Therefore, the outcomes of the assessment, which result in products that are not entirely
quality-assured, are invariably the consequence of a risk-benefit analysis.

– This quality assessment framework has been exclusively designed for MSF and is intended

for internal use only.

g The assessment of quality-assured products involves a much more stringent framework and ensures a

comprehensive evaluation of both the manufacturing site and the product.

Chapter 3: Quality assurance in procurement

61

3

– This assessment must be conducted by a pharmacist who has been adequately trained

and possesses the necessary skills, often a SP or somebody designated by the SP.

– The GMP assessment is divided into three main types of products:

• Drugs. See Appendix 1.

• Medical devices and in-vitro diagnostics. See Appendix 2.

• Nutrition-specialised food. See Appendix 3.

– The principles of the GSDP assessment for local suppliers are described in Section 3.4.5.

– In specific cases and upon request from MSF supply centres or HQ pharmacy referents,

additional checks or verifications must be carried out by the MSF programs when receiving
medications or before patient administration. Furthermore, reinforced vigilance should be
applied once the drug has been administered.

3.4.3 Potential entry channels for deviations

Deviations may occur in MSF programs when key quality requirements are not met.

– In-country non-qualified suppliers: medical products procured locally by MSF programs,

either through an exceptional validation request or purchased based on information
established in a country (quality) procurement database.

– Any procurement centre that is not compliant with MSF QA policy: medical products

procured by MSF supply centres or regional supply hubs that do not comply with the
international standards outlined in MSF’s Quality Assurance Policy.

– Inbound donations or loans: donations and loans received by MSF programs for which

sufficient information and documentation are not available to enable proper evaluation
or where it is impossible to guarantee that the supply chain adheres to the standards
outlined in MSF policies.

On rare occasions, medical products procured by MSF qualified supply


centres may lack sufficient information or documentation necessary for proper

evaluation, or the available information may not adequately demonstrate compliance

with the standards outlined in MSF policies.

The evaluation of these exceptional cases is conducted on an individual basis, with the
decision to use the product made following a comprehensive risk assessment. This
decision must be approved by the relevant medical directors of the MSF sections and
is always subject to a specified time limit.

Chapter 3: Quality assurance in procurement

62

3.4.4 In-country procurement

All local purchases of medical products within MSF programs or MSF non-qualified supply
centres that do not comply with MSF QA policy must be assessedh and validated by the HQ
pharmacy referents, except in the following cases:

– The GMP and GSDP have previously been verified and validated in a country (quality)

procurement database for medical products. Such databases are established in countries
where in-country procurement is predominant and where certain conditions have been
met. See more details in Section 3.4.6.

– When it involves the direct purchase of quality-assured nutrition-specialised food from

manufacturers validated by the MSF Food Safety & Quality Assurance Coordination, as
they meet the requirements defined in Section 3.3.1.

MSF Local market (quality) assessments (LMA)

LMAs are carried out by SPsi. From a quality perspective, an LMA conducted by MSF may fulfil
one or both of the following objectives:

– Improve understanding of the regulatory framework governing essential activities, such as

the importation, storage, and distribution of medical products within the country.

– Provide an analysis of the local pharmaceutical environment and the availability of quality

medical products in the local market.

– Provide recommendationsj (as a result of technical visits) concerning potential local

suppliers or, when required, local manufacturers of medical products. In countries where
importation is not feasible, these recommendations can streamline the procurement
process, allowing for faster purchase validation.

LMA is not a market search for identifying suppliers at a specific moment. To


carry out an LMA, the MSF programs must clearly define their needs and gather
comprehensive information regarding the suppliers or manufacturers to be visited. See more
details in Appendices 4 and 5.

Important considerations

– The principles governing the LMA of medical products are mutualised across the different

MSF sections. This consistency ensures coherence, optimises resources, and prevents
duplication of efforts among the different MSF sections.

– When multiple MSF sections are present in the same country, the LMA is conducted for all

of them, and the recommendations apply uniformly to each MSF section. Consequently,
the decision to conduct an LMA must be agreed upon by the SPs through the Pharmacy
Quality (PQ) platformk.

– The LMAs must be aligned with the operational demands of the MSF programs present in

the country.

– An LMA may not necessarily result in a change to the procurement strategy of the MSF

h The assessment is always conducted based on evaluations from both the manufacturer and the supplier.
i Exceptionally, SPs can authorise other HQ pharmacy referents, Intersectional Pharmacists (ISPs) or IO QA

coordinators to conduct an LMA, provided they have been adequately trained and possess the necessary
skills. In any case, the final validation of local suppliers or manufacturing sites (in the limited cases where such
assessments take place) should always be endorsed by an SP.
j These recommendations must be based on the needs of the MSF programs operating within the country and

are limited to the specific country where the assessment is conducted.
k Formerly known as the Section Pharmacist platform, the Quality Pharmacy platform is an intersectional

medical platform whose main objective is to ensure quality throughout the entire supply chain, ultimately
guaranteeing patient access to quality‐assured medical products.

Chapter 3: Quality assurance in procurement

63

3

program, as it can be conducted solely as a backup plan. Additionally, an LMA may not
necessarily lead to the implementation of a country (quality) procurement database. See
more details in Section 3.4.6.

Non‐disclosure of LMA outcomes
MSF does not share audit or technical visit reports, nor their outcomes (approved/not
approved), with external parties. Information concerning suppliers of medical products is
considered sensitive and is often obtained under confidentiality agreements. Moreover, MSF’s
internal quality-related decisions are developed exclusively for use within the organisation and
may rely on context specific risk–benefit analyses. These assessments take into account MSF’s
pharmaceutical set ups and the organisation’s capacity to monitor medicines throughout the
supply chain up to the patient.

Sharing such outcomes externally could create legal and ethical responsibilities toward non-
MSF patients, exposing MSF to liability if quality incidents occur. As MSF is not a regulatory
authority, its quality decisions apply only to MSF operations and are not intended to inform
procurement choices made by other organisations.

General criteria for determining the suitability of an LMA

The PQ platform uses the following criteria to evaluate and approve an LMA request:

– The justification for the LMA is thoroughly detailed: when MSF programs request an LMA,

the MSF Pharmacy Quality Platform (PQP) requires sufficient information to determine:

• Detail rational for local sourcing: explaining why in-country procurement is being
considered instead of importation of quality assured sources.

• Constraints identified regarding importation of quality assured medical products:
including also the efforts that have been undertaken by the MSF programs to overcome
these constraints.

• Post LMA short-medium- and long-term supply strategy once the LMA has been
completed.

– Alignment between MSF sections: the request and justification for the LMA should be

agreed upon between the different MSF programs across MSF sections.

– The list of targeted medical products is clearly defined: the MSF programs shall clearly

define the medical products targeted under the LMA.

– Preliminary market analysis has been conducted: the MSF programs should gather

comprehensive information about the suppliers that can be visited and supply the
products defined in point 3.

– Evidence of added value: each LMA requires significant coordination among multiple

teams and QA specialists; therefore, requests must clearly justify the investment of time
and resources. An LMA must deliver meaningful benefits to MSF programs. In contexts
where the local market is not reliable — due to inadequate manufacturing, poor quality
of available products, or unstable markets — an LMA is unlikely to add value and may be
refused because of unacceptable risks to patient safety.

Chapter 3: Quality assurance in procurement

64

– Designatedl qualified pharmacist is available, and visit is feasible (visa, security, etc..):

it must also be confirmed that travel to the specific country is feasible, including meeting
visa requirements, security clearance, and ensuring logistical support for the visit.

3.4.5 Local supplier quality assessment

In the context of in-country procurement, a local supplier is a commercial entity to which MSF
places an order and which is responsible for organising the delivery of products to MSF. A local
supplier may be an exclusive distributor, an importer, a wholesaler, a national procurement
centre or, in rare cases, a retail pharmacy (for staff health purposes). A manufacturer may also
act as a local supplier in the case of direct procurement.

The quality assessment evaluates the level of compliance with the WHO GSDPm. Additionally,
general information is collected regarding the following:

– The legal status, including license or registration certificates.

– A list of companies with which the local supplier works or represents, along with details of

their selection and procurement process.

– A list of products, availability and lead-time for delivery.

– Type of clients served: including public and private sectors (e.g., hospitals, retail

pharmacies, NGOs, etc.).

– Sourcing practices, whether directly from the manufacturer or through other suppliers, to

assess traceability throughout the supply chain.

General local supplier status

– 1st choice, 2nd choice and 3rd choice supplier

• Purchasing medical products from this local supplier is acceptable, provided that the
products comply with the GMP.

• Whenever possible, first-choice suppliers should be prioritised over second-choice
suppliers, and second-choice suppliers should be prioritised over third-choice suppliers.

• This status does not apply to cold chain products (for cold chain products, refer to the
section below).

Categorising a local supplier as first, second, or third choice does not exempt MSF

programs or any MSF regional supply hub from submitting a validation request to the HQ
pharmacy referents. This requirement holds unless there is a validated (quality) procurement
database in the country, and the GMP have also been verified within the database.

– Not Validated supplier

• Any purchase from suppliers categorised as “Not validated” is unacceptable unless
exceptionally validated by the medical directors or, under delegation, by the HQ
pharmacy referents.

– Outdated supplier

• Any purchase from suppliers categorised as “outdated” is unacceptable unless

l By the PQ platform
m https://www.who.int/publications/m/item/trs-1025-annex-7

Chapter 3: Quality assurance in procurement

65

3

exceptionally validated by the medical directors or, under delegation, by the HQ
pharmacy referents.

• This status applies only to local suppliers that were evaluated as acceptable (1st, 2nd, or
3rd choice) but whose last assessment was conducted more than five years ago.

– Unknown supplier

• Any purchase from suppliers categorised as “unknown” local is unacceptable unless
exceptionally validated by the medical directors or, under delegation, by the HQ
pharmacy referents.

• There is no GSDP assessment information available to determine the general status of
the local supplier.

• This lack of information may be because the supplier has never been visited or has
declined a visit.

Cold chain local supplier status

– Cold chain validated

• Purchasing cold chain products from this local supplier is acceptable, provided that the
products comply with good manufacturing practices. The local supplier is responsible for
managing the transportation.

– Cold chain validated except transport

• Purchasing cold chain products from this local supplier is acceptable, provided that
the products comply with good manufacturing practices and that MSF manages the
transportation.

– Cold chain not validated

• Any purchase of cold chain products from this local supplier is unacceptable unless
exceptionally validated by the medical directors or, under delegation, by the HQ
pharmacy referents.

It is important to note that occasionally, it may not be possible to evaluate how the supplier
manages cold chain products. This may be due to various reasons, such as the supplier not
commercialising cold chain products, the supplier's preference to avoid assessment, or other
reasons.

3.4.6 Country (quality) procurement database of medical products

A country (quality) procurement database is a tool developed and regularly updated by a
qualified pharmacist who evaluates potential local suppliers and product manufacturers
available in the country's market. These databases assist supply and pharmacy teams in
selecting the most reliable sources from a quality standpoint, streamlining the procurement
process and facilitating faster purchase validation.
MSF has recently transitioned from managing these databases in Excel format to UniLocal, a
centralised digital platform designed to consolidate and standardise quality data across MSF
sections.

Decision for establishing a database
To ensure that these databases are properly maintained and remain useful to MSF programs,
substantial investment in human resources is required for their effective maintenance and
monitoring of the local market. The implementation of a database should only be considered
after exhaustive negotiations with the NMRA to import medical products have failed.

Chapter 3: Quality assurance in procurement

66

If multiple MSF sections are present in a country, the decision to implement a


database must be agreed upon by all the involved SPs of the respective MSF sections.

Situations where implementing a database may not be viable

– Poor quality assurance in medical markets: in countries where the nature of the

local market for medical products is very poor from a quality assurance perspective
(manufacturing sites, production capacity, market stability, etc.) and entails an
unacceptable risk to patient safety.

– Lack of access to registration information: in some countries, registration information

for medical products may not be publicly accessible. It can be extremely difficult, or even
impossible, to obtain details on marketing authorisation holders, manufacturing sites,
and other critical information. This lack of transparency impedes the ability to assess the
quality of sources available within the local market.

– Unreliable human resources for database maintenance: in situations where the human

resources required to maintain the country’s procurement database are not guaranteed,
it may not be feasible to ensure the continued availability of dedicated and well-trained
pharmacists (e.g., ISP) to manage the database effectively.

3.4.7 MSF/third-party mixed supply

Mixed supply refers to a setup in which the supply chain for medical products is not entirely
under MSF’s responsibility. Instead, an external third partyn, frequently the Ministry of Health
(MOH), is also involved. Mixed supply entails a shared responsibility in managing the supply
chain between MSF and a third party.

Mixed supply might emerge across a wide variety of settings (e.g., MSF structures, MoH
structures, etc.), and across different types of medical programs (e.g., vertical programs,
primary health care programs, etc.).

While arguments related to sustainability may sometimes be raised in favour of

mixed supply, these should never override the priority of ensuring access to quality-
assured medical products. The core objective of the mixed supply risk assessment remains the
protection of patient safety through stringent quality assurance measures.

It is important to note that mixed supply does not refer to situations in which MSF

receives occasional donations or loans from an external third party, which are
subsequently integrated into MSF’s supply chain. In these cases, MSF must verify the quality
of the medical product just as it would for any other medical product. For more details, see
Section 3.4.8.

n A third party in this context refers to either an external actor to MSF, such as the Ministry of Health (MoH),

other NGOs or other types of partners.

Chapter 3: Quality assurance in procurement

67

A mixed supply setup results in two distinct supply systems, each potentially offering different
levels of quality. When considering this setup, a risk assessment should be conducted by
the MSF program in collaboration with the HQ cell/desk. This assessment should outline the
overall context, describe the MOH’s procurement and supply chain, include a list of medical
products supplied by the MOH along with sourcing information, and justify the acceptance (or
not) of a mixed supply strategy based on operational needs. The following key factors should
be carefully considered and reviewed by the SP from a technical and quality standpoint:

– Health inequalities and risks: significant quality discrepancies between the supply

chains could lead to inequalities among the patients. It is important to evaluate the
manufacturing practices of the supplied products, as well as storage and distribution
conditions, especially if sensitive products are supplied, such as cold chain products.

– Reliability and responsiveness of the supply chain: it is crucial to assess whether the

supply chain is consistent and capable of providing medical supplies in the long term, or,
on the contrary, if it is prone to frequent stockouts or disruptions.

– Accountability along the supply chain: responsibilities must be clearly delineated and

thoroughly understood by MSF and the third party.

When establishing a mixed supply setup, it is essential to clearly delineate the agreed-upon
arrangements between MSF and its partners. This should include a list of products supplied by
MSF, the cohort of patients that MSF will cover, the specific framework under which MSF may
provide gap-filling support, etc. This technical feedback should guide negotiations with MOH
on the mixed supply, aiming to limit MOH procurement to the least risky products.

The management of stocks within MSF programs needs to be defined. As a general principle,
any medical products used by MSF should be recorded in MSF’s stock management system to
ensure traceability. However, MSF’s level of responsibility for these stocks may vary based on
agreements with local health authorities and should be addressed in the risk analysis.

Chapter 3: Quality assurance in procurement

68

3.4.8 Donations and loans from a third party

Donated or loaned medical products must meet MSF's quality criteria, just like any other
medical product. However, since these products do not go through the standard procurement
process, they constitute a deviation from the QA policy. All inbound donations or loans must
be reviewed and validated in accordance with the same procedures applied to local purchases.
The source of the product (manufacturer, intermediary distributor or donor) must be evaluated
and approved by an HQ pharmacy referent.

If the third party is another MSF section, quality assessment is generally not required. However,
if the product’s purchase has been exceptionally validated, the MSF section donating the
product should inform the receiving MSF section. In this case, it is advisable to consult the HQ
pharmacy referent for further verification.
