4.1 Introduction

National authorities around the world are increasing the stringency of their regulations on
medical products, particularly pharmaceuticals, medical devices (MDs), and in vitro diagnostics
(IVDs). The requirements can vary significantly from one country to another, depending on
their national regulatory frameworks and market conditions.

Strategic planning of the importation process is crucial to ensure smooth and efficient
operations, minimising potential delays and compliance issues. MSF can avoid unexpected
costs and obstacles by proactively addressing regulatory requirements. This requires effective
coordination among MSF programs, MSF headquarters, and MSF supply centres, often
involving various disciplines such as supply, pharmacy, legal, and advocacy.

Chapter 4: Importation of medical products

72

4.2 Importation as a shared responsibility

Once a medical order is submitted and validated by an MSF program, the MSF supply centres
managing the order will do their utmost to provide the documentation required by the national
authorities, provided that the documentation has been previously verified and is included in
the specific country constraint sheeta (CCS).

If it is not possible to obtain the necessary documents, the MSF supply centres will identify
suitable alternatives (e.g., new sources or other documentation) and inform the MSF programs
of these alternatives, sharing possible options.

Similarly, MSF programs are committed to facilitating the import process and must consider
several fundamental elements, as discussed below.

4.2.1 The MSF status in the country

MSF’s capacity to import is directly linked to its legal status within a given country. Typically,
there are three possible scenarios:

– MSF is not registered in the country: in this situation, MSF typically operates under the

umbrella of another third party (e.g., another NGO, the MoH, etc) and is not permitted
to act as an importer. A Memorandum of Understanding (MoU)b must be signed with this
partner to establish the terms and conditions of the importation process.

– MSF is registered as a non-profit entity in the country: in this context, MSF typically

operates independently; however, its ability to import supplies is contingent upon its legal
status. MSF may either conduct imports autonomously or under the umbrella of another
entity, such as the MoH. Any potential facilitation in the importation process should be
guided by an MoU.

– MSF has signed a Host Country Agreement (HCA)c or framework MoU/agreement:

in some contexts, MSF can sign a framework MOU with its line Ministry, such as the
MOH, the Ministry of Planning or Budget, the Ministry of the Interior or the Ministry
of Humanitarian Affairs. These agreements may include some provisions granting MSF
importation facilities or exemptions. Depending on the applicable framework, these
documents may be signed on behalf of the entire government. Additionally, MSF may also
sign an HCA with the Ministry of Foreign Affairs (MoFA).

a The set of requirements negotiated and agreed upon with the national authorities, including any verbal

agreements, must be documented internally in the country constraints sheet (CCS). This document should
compile all the requirements negotiated in previous years and be made available to all MSF sections operating
in the country. CCS can be found in regulatory affairs pharmacist SharePoint: https://msfintl.sharepoint.com/
sites/msfintlcommunities/RArepository
b A formal agreement between two or more parties outlining the terms and details of a mutual understanding or

agreement, framing the conditions of MSF activities. MOUs are signed (1) at the local level with a representative
of the Ministry of Health (MOH) or of a local organisation to fix the conditions of a project/partnership (Project
MOU), or (2) at the governmental level with the MOH or the Line Ministry of NGOs to fix the conditions of MSF
presence/action in a country (Framework MOU).
c A formal agreement based on international law, providing for privileges and immunities similar to diplomatic

missions, signed by the Ministry of Foreign Affairs (MoFA) on behalf of the government for the benefit of all
MSF sections present or not in the country.

Chapter 4: Importation of medical products

73

4

MoU, HCA or specific agreements

This type of agreement grants several waivers, facilitations, exemptions, privileges and

immunities, sometimes of a diplomatic nature, that positively impact various aspects

of MSF operations, including the importation process. However, it is important to

note that these privileges and facilitations, whether outlined in MOUs, HCAs, or

specific agreements, do not exempt MSF from adhering to specific procedures before

importation, particularly the requirement to obtain pre-authorisation from national

authorities.

The establishment of a framework MoU or an HCA should always include a specific

paragraph addressing importation. MSF programs must seek guidance from the

intersectional legal department (ILD) in preparing these documents. See more details

in Section 4.6.

If an MSF section decides to import under the umbrella of another MSF section

registered in the country, thereby benefiting from its registration and applicable
regime, it is crucial to coordinate shipments, align operational practices, and comply with
the applicable procedures. Additionally, it is of utmost importance that all documentation is
completed in the name of the registered MSF section.

4.2.2 Relationship with national authorities

In the countries where MSF operates, it is essential to maintain a positive and effective
relationship with national authorities. When authorities recognise MSF as a reliable and
trustworthy partner, they are more likely to offer support, streamline administrative procedures,
and facilitate the granting of necessary permissions. This trust also plays a crucial role in
mitigating potential challenges or misunderstandings that may arise during the importation
process.

Maintaining an appropriate balance between providing the necessary information and avoiding
excessive compliance is crucial. In this regard, if national authorities attempt to impose
commercial importation regulations on MSF, it is essential to engage in proactive negotiations
with them. This requires robust coordination among the various departments involved in the
importation process, such as supply, pharmacy, and legal. In certain situations, importation
challenges may extend beyond technical aspects. In such cases, it is crucial to actively engage
with the Head of Mission.

In countries where multiple MSF sections operate, it is highly recommended to implement
a coordinated approach, with designated focal points across various departments to ensure
effective and cohesive management with national authorities.

Given the highly complex nature of these technical negotiations, MSF has developed internal
expertise to support its programs (see Section 4.6).

Chapter 4: Importation of medical products

74

4.2.3 Knowledge of importation regulations

MSF programs must develop a comprehensive understanding of the country’s import process
and remain informed of any new requirements enforced by national authorities.

MSF programs must verify whether the legislation enforced at the point of entry aligns with
national legislation or guidelines, as discrepancies may occur. MSF ensures compliance with
the minimum requirements because an increase in requirements can further restrict the range
of products that can be imported into the country.

Some countries operate on an annual import waiverd system, which requires submission at
least two to three months before the start of the annual waiver cycle to obtain approval before
initiating medical orders.

See more details in Section 4.3.

4.2.4 The medical standard list (MSL)

The MSL of the MSF programs should first be defined or updated in accordance with the
country’s import requirements. For instance, in countries with annual waiver systems, the MSL
revision should align with the waiver application schedule, as the waiver cannot be altered
during the year except under extraordinary circumstances (e.g., an emergency in the country).

Additionally, MSF supply centres must be aware of the MSLs for MSF programs. This knowledge
enables the MSF supply centres to anticipate needs, prepare all necessary documentation, and
identify alternative sources when required. Otherwise, the processing time for orders may be
adversely affected.

For more details about MSL, see Chapter 1.

d A waiver is an official exemption or reduction from certain requirements, fees, or regulations that are normally

applicable. A waiver can be granted by customs authorities or other national regulatory bodies and is often
used to facilitate import, reduce costs, or facilitate the rapid delivery of humanitarian aid.

Chapter 4: Importation of medical products

75

4

4.3 General requirements for medical products

MSF imports goods through humanitarian donations rather than commercial channels, and the
requirements for each differ significantly. The general process for importing medical products
is usually outlined in two key sets of guidelines:

– Importation guidelines: issued by the National Regulatory Authority (NRA), these

guidelines delineate specific requirements affecting the importation process. They also
detail the technical documentation necessary for either the pre-import permit application
or the cargo clearance stage.

– Donation guidelines: issued by the MoH.

Typically, MSF adheres to the donation guidelines issued by the MoH, where available, or the
relevant section on donations in the NRA’s importation guidelines.

4.3.1 Important considerations

– Import requirements must be verified before the MSF programs process any medical

orders that require importation, as they might impact:

• The selection of manufacturers and suppliers chosen by the MSF supply centre. Not all
suppliers can provide the same documentation, even if the product manufacturer is
identical.

• The shipping method, whether the medical order is consolidated through the MSF
supply centre or uses direct deliverye to the MSF programs.

– The import regime plays an important role in defining the importation requirements.

For example, in the case of ImpExf, the import requirements of both the importing and
final destination countries must be considered. Conversely, for transit routing, only the
requirements of the final destination country are relevant.

– It is essential to understand the origin of the request and the purpose for which the

information is sought. When MSF is unable to respond directly, an alternative document
that provides similar or equivalent information may be suggested.

e Direct delivery refers to the process where products or goods are shipped directly from the manufacturer or

the supplier to the final destination (MSF program), bypassing intermediary storage facilities like MSF supply
centres.
f Import for export: it refers to the process where goods are imported into a country with the intention of

exporting them to another country.

Chapter 4: Importation of medical products

76

Please note that providing new documentation without prior verification and

consultation, even when requested by national authorities, may set harmful

precedents for other MSF sections, MSF supply centres, and other NGOs.

• Any new documentation requested by national authorities that is not included in
the Country Constraint Sheet must first be submitted to the respective MSF supply
centres for verification and validation. The submission should clearly specify the entity
requesting the new document and the rationale for the request.

• Once the new document is approved, the MSF supply centres will ensure its systematic
availability for the concerned products. If a specific document is not available, the MSF
supply centre should propose a feasible alternative that provides the same information
in a different format. This alternative will be utilised during negotiations with national
authorities.

• Maintaining an appropriate balance between providing the necessary information and
avoiding excessive compliance is crucial. In this regard, if national authorities attempt to
impose commercial importation regulations on MSF, it is essential to engage in proactive
negotiations with them.

4.3.2 Overall requirements

The general requirements for importing medical products vary by country. Still, they typically
follow specific patterns based on the type of product (e.g., medicines, controlled drugs and
precursors, MDs, etc.).

– Upstream restrictions: import may also be subject to upstream restrictions (on the

manufacturer or supplier), such as territorial embargoes or patent limitations.

– Quantity for sampling in customs: the MSF program must be aware of any country-specific

sampling plan implemented by the national authorities. The order calculation should be
adjusted accordingly, and the MSF supply centre should be informed to minimise sampling
losses. For more details about sampling, see Section 4.4.2.

Direct deliveries are not recommended for countries that conduct random sampling

upon arrival, as there is a significant likelihood of being sampled. The main concern lies
in the delays associated with the sampling and testing process. In some countries, this process
may take up to three or four months, during which the entire quantity of the sampled batch
remains on hold under conditional release and cannot be utilised.

– Product specifications: In certain countries, products must adhere to specific

requirements. These requirements may include having product labels or leaflets written in
a designated language and ensuring a minimum shelf life upon arrival at the port of entry.

– Letter of Non-objection (LoNO): Occasionally, MSF may be required to obtain a LoNO from

the local technical representative (LTR)g. Such requests are typically managed through
the headquarters of the concerned manufacturer, as this is where the MSF supply centre
places its orders.

g MSF is considered a third-party importer in such a case.

Chapter 4: Importation of medical products

77

4

4.3.3 Medicines

– Verify whether the medicines required for the MSF program are listed on the National

Essential Medicine List (NEML) or the more frequently updated WHO Essential Medicines
List (WHO EML). If the medicines are not included in either list, assess the feasibility
of importing them, as additional justification or documentation may be required for
importation in this case.

– Verify whether MSF is required to submit any regulatory documentation.

4.3.4 Controlled drugs and precursors

– Verify whether it is possible to import controlled drugs and precursors into the country

and determine if a special import permit (SIP) in addition to the standard import permit
is required. If a SIP is necessary, the information necessary for the SIP application and the
validity period must be well known.

– Verify whether the country follows the International Narcotics Control Boardh (INCB) redi,

yellowj, and greenk lists of controlled drugs and precursors, or if the country has its own
internal list.

– Verify if MSF is required to submit the annual forecast for controlled drugs to the NRA for

integration into the national forecast. In the event of an emergency, the country may apply
to the INCB for an additional maximum quantity.

For more information about the import permit of controlled drugs, see Appendix 6. For more
information about the classification of narcotics, psychotropics, and precursors, refer to the
MSF catalogues.

4.3.5 Medical devices, biomedical equipment and in vitro diagnostics

– Verify whether the country has a list of registered MDs and IVDs (typically available

on the NRA website). If such a list exists, confirm whether it is also possible to import
unregistered MDs and IVDs.

– Determine whether any additional authority is involved in the pre-import permit process

for equipment (e.g., Bureau of Standards).

– Verify whether MSF is required to submit any regulatory documentation.

4.3.6 Nutrition-specialised food

– Identify the national authority responsible for overseeing the pre-import permit process

for nutrition-specialised food products.

– Additionally, confirm if MSF is required to submit any regulatory documentation.

h Established by the United Nations, the INCB plays a crucial role in the international control of narcotic drugs

and psychotropic substances. The INCB's responsibilities include overseeing the importation and distribution
of controlled drugs to ensure compliance with international treaties and regulations.
i https://www.incb.org/incb/en/precursors/Red_Forms/red-list.html
j https://www.incb.org/incb/en/narcotic-drugs/Yellowlist/yellow-list.html
k https://www.incb.org/incb/en/psychotropics/green-list.html

Chapter 4: Importation of medical products

78

4.3.7 Medical kits

Medical kits are pre-packaged by MSF supply centres, but the individual products contained
in these kits may not meet the overall importation requirements of the destination country
(e.g., products with French labels or a remaining shelf life of fewer than six months). To ensure
proper importation:

– Determine whether there is a national policy concerning the importation of medical kits.

– Verify if it is possible to process a medical kit as a single product (using one HSl codem)

during the pre-import permit process.

– Before placing an order containing medical kits, ensure the kits do not include any

regulated products (e.g., controlled drugs).

Importing medical Kits involves extra complexity for the MSF supply centres. In some

cases, the manufacturer of the individual products may not be able to provide the
necessary regulatory documentation for preclearance.

For more information about regulatory documentation, see Appendix 7.

l Harmonised System (HS) codes are commonly used throughout the import and export process for the

classification of goods.
m See more information in section 4.4.2, Duties and taxes

Chapter 4: Importation of medical products

79

4

4.4 The importation process

The importation process can be divided into three different stages:

– Pre-arrival stage (pre-clearance): focusing on the preparation of all necessary paperwork,

permits, and logistical arrangements.

– On-arrival (customs clearance): reflecting the activities related to arrival, inspection,

customs processing of goods, payment of any applicable taxes, and approval for entry into
the country.

– Post-arrival (post-clearance): refers to the standard documentation requirements to be

adhered to and potential re-export activities.

4.4.1 Pre-arrival stage: pre-clearance

The import permit

An import permit is an authorisation required before goods can be imported into a country.
This permit must be obtained from the relevant regulatory authorities before importation.

– MSF orders should normally be divided into medical and non-medical products, as they

adhere to distinct import permit processes.

– In general, pre-import permits for medical products are granted and issued by national

medicines NRA. However, for certain products (e.g., MDs, IVDs, specialised nutritional
food, etc), other relevant authorities may also be involved.

– Non-medical products may also require authorisation in some instances, such as chemicals

utilised in water and sanitation activities.

– In many countries, the request for an import permit can only be submitted to the NRA

by a nationally registered pharmacist, who is typically technically associated with and
responsible for the importing entity. Consequently, MSF may also need to obtain a
Wholesaler Distribution Authorisation (WDA) or a similar license to qualify as an importer
within the country. While this requirement is not universally applicable, it is a crucial
consideration that must be verified.

– The information and documentation submitted in an import permit should be carefully

verified before submission. Import permits must be error-free, as modifications cannot be
made once they have been issued.

– Orders must be placed in full package quantities. It is acceptable to import less than the

quantity declared on the permits, but importing more than the specified amount is not
allowed.

– Once the national authority has issued the import permit, it is subsequently shared with

customs for clearance, typically by the customs brokern.

For more information about the import permit for controlled drugs, see Appendix 6.

n A customs broker is a licensed professional or firm that facilitates the import and export of goods on behalf

of clients by ensuring compliance with customs regulations. They handle various tasks, including preparing
and submitting necessary documentation, calculating duties and taxes, and communicating with customs
authorities.

Chapter 4: Importation of medical products

80

If a pre-export verification of conformity (PVOCo) is required, see Toolbox, Memo, Pre-shipment.

4.4.2 On-arrival stage: clearance

Customs clearance is the process of obtaining official authorisation from customs authorities
to enter or leave a country. It involves submitting required documentation and paying duties,
taxes, and fees to the customs authorities. Customs clearance is a critical step in the import
and export process, as it ensures that goods comply with the law and national regulations.

MSF may occasionally encounter challenges related to customs, such as unclear or inconsistent
import procedures and understaffed customs offices. However, issues sometimes arise due to
MSF's limited understanding of customs regulations, leading to incomplete declarations.

The consequences of not having the proper documentation include the following:

– Delays in the process

– The incurrence of demurrage costs

– The inability to release consignments from customs

All information shared with customs must be submitted promptly. The documents presented
must be completely accurate, and MSF should ensure that all customs entries and declarations
are systematically opened and closed in the customs IT systems.

It is essential to ensure complete visibility and traceability of shipments, both


incoming and outgoing, using appropriate monitoring tools that facilitate this process.

Understanding customs regulations and procedures

To gain a comprehensive understanding of the customs arrival process, multiple elements
must be verified:

– Customs-bonded warehouse: determine whether a customs-bonded warehouse is

available, if advance booking is required, and the applicable conditions for its use.

– Detailed customs clearance and transportation procedures

• Clarify whether a licensed customs clearing agent is required for any pre-arrival and on-
arrival customs processes.

• Special attention should be given to the selection of the freight-forwarding agent, as
they will represent MSF in dealings with customs authorities.

– Conditional release procedures: Confirm whether there are procedures in place for

conditional release, such as those required for cold chain products.

– Sampling process (primarily for medical products):

• Identify the authority responsible for conducting the sampling. This authority should
issue a receipt for the sampled batch, and MSF should maintain a logbook to record the
batches that have been sampled and tested, where applicable.

• Ascertain the lead time associated with the sampling process.

• Determine the costs involved.

o PVOC is required by certain countries to ensure that imported goods meet established quality, safety, and

regulatory standards before shipment.

Chapter 4: Importation of medical products

81

4

• Confirm any specific documentation required for sampling.

• Explore the possibility of exemption from sampling or arranging for sampling to be
conducted at a later stage.

• In some countries, the presence of an MSF nationally registered pharmacist is required
during the sampling process.

If a sample is deemed out of specification, a claim should be submitted to the


relevant MSF supply centre.

– Import taxes and duties

• Investigate whether the incoming cargo is subject to import taxes or duties and
determine if MSF is eligible for any exemptions or waivers.

• Determine the specific timing for calculating and imposing these taxes. The timing can
vary depending on the country and its specific customs and taxation laws. Generally,
customs authorities assess duties and taxes based on the declared value of the goods,
their classification, and applicable agreements.

• If NGOs are eligible for exemptions under the law or through an MoU, this often involves
a specific process requiring prior approval from relevant authorities, such as the MoH,
the MOFA, etc.

Duties and taxes
The amount of duties and taxes required for a shipment is determined by several factors:

– Product classification (HS code): it determines the applicable tariff rate based on the

type of goods. Customs authorities use this commodity code to identify the goods being
shipped and apply the appropriate taxes, duties, and regulations.

– The declared value of the goods: this includes costs, insurance, and freight value (CIF

value). It is crucial to accurately declare the correct value on the commercial or pro forma
invoice. The goods description on the commercial invoice should include details such as
the product's end-use and country of originp. To ensure proper classification, the HS code
must align with the goods description.

– Trade agreements between countries: preferential or free trade agreements between

countries can reduce or eliminate duties for certain products.

– Incotermsq (International Commercial Terms): these terms specify which party—the

sender or the receiver—is responsible for handling customs clearance procedures in the
exporting and importing countries.

– Examples of incoterms may include, but are not limited to:

• Ex Works (EXW): the receiver handles all customs clearance and pays all duties and taxes
from the point of origin.

• Free on Board (FOB): the sender clears the goods for export, while the receiver is
responsible for import customs clearance and related costs.

• Delivered Duty Paid (DDP): the sender is responsible for both export and import customs
clearance, including all duties, taxes, and delivery to the receiver's location.

p Also known as country of manufacture.
q Incoterms (International Commercial Terms) are a set of standardised trade terms published by the

International Chamber of Commerce (ICC) that define the responsibilities of receivers (buyers) and senders
(sellers) in international trade transactions, particularly regarding the delivery of goods. In the context of
customs clearance, Incoterms are crucial because they determine who is responsible for specific shipment-
related costs, including duties, taxes, and customs clearance.

Chapter 4: Importation of medical products

82

4.4.3 Post-arrival stage: post-clearance

Documentation and archiving

National authorities may conduct audits up to three to five years after any importation. Failure
to maintain documentation for all MSF importation transactions could result in penalties.
Likewise, for temporarily admitted goods that have been re-exported, proof of exit may be
required for several years after re-exportation.

Even in emergency interventions, it is important to ensure that proper archiving is consistently
maintained for all importations.

Re-export

When re-exporting goods (medical products previously imported under temporary customs
admission), it is necessary to:

– Determine the necessary transport documentation and certificates.

– Identify the requirements set by the receiving country.

– Verify whether an export permit is required, and if so, identify the issuing authority.

– Verify whether a cargo examination is needed and determine which authority is

responsible for conducting this examination.

– Estimate the different lead times and associated costs.

– Review any provisions or conditions in the MOU, such as pre-authorisation from

authorities or prohibitions on certain products.

Chapter 4: Importation of medical products

83

4

4.5 Importing during an emergency state

Facilitations for import processes arising from an emergency state apply only when

the emergency is officially declared by the national or local authority.

4.5.1 General considerations

– Typically, national authorities draft specific external regulations for the importation of

relief consignments. These regulations may facilitate the issuance of pre-import permits
and the completion of customs clearance procedures.

– The level of facilitation may vary; some requirements may be lifted for certain types of

products while remaining in effect for others.

– In some instances, facilitations may also require negotiation, regardless of whether an

emergency has been declared.

– It is important to explicitly document the window period during which the emergency

importation procedure is valid. This ensures a cut-off point for the MSF supply centres,
as goods must be imported before this period expires to fully benefit from any fast-track
concessions.

– It is important to verify whether MSF has a special emergency MoU with the MoH

outlining pre-agreed specific facilitation measures for rapid response.

– In case of an emergency where MSF's activities are not covered by an existing MoU,

a letter of support from the MoH, or another relevant national authority involved in
emergency operations may be requested to facilitate the importation process.

– If an annual waiver for the importation of unregistered products has already been issued

for any MSF section, it is essential to determine whether the needs addressed by a new
emergency order are covered by the existing waiver. If the current waiver does not cover
these needs, it may be necessary to seek approval for an urgent supplementary waiver.

– Concerning controlled drugs, it is important to determine whether the WHO Emergency

Use Listing procedure (EUL) applies in the case of an emergency. Find more details in
Section 4.5.2, WHO EUL.

Remember

• If an MSF section decides to import under the umbrella of another MSF section
present in the country, thereby benefiting from its registration and applicable regime,
it is crucial to coordinate the shipments, align operational practices, and respect the
applicable procedures. Additionally, it is of utmost importance that all documentation
be completed in the name of the registered MSF section.

• Even in emergency interventions, it is important to ensure that proper archiving is
consistently maintained for all importations.

Chapter 4: Importation of medical products

84

4.5.2 WHO Emergency Use Listing procedure (EUL)

EUL is a risk-based procedure for assessing and listing unlicensed vaccines, therapeutics, and
in vitro diagnostics, with the ultimate aim of expediting their availability to people affected by
a public health emergency1. This procedure is designed to facilitate the rapid importation and
use of critical medical products in urgent situations, particularly when traditional regulatory
pathways may be disrupted or unavailabler. The EUL procedure may also apply where the
competent authority is not a member of the INCB. MSF mainly uses the EUL procedure for
controlled drugs.

This procedure should be considered only as a last option, strictly reserved for


emergencies. It must not be used to bypass regulatory requirements.

The application of this procedure may vary depending on the MSF supply centre. Typically, the
responsible pharmacist or person at the MSF supply centre engages in negotiations with the
relevant ministry of the exporting country and the INCB, based in Vienna, to secure a permit
for the export of small quantities of controlled narcotic or psychotropic drugs.

The Medical Director of the specific MSF section needs to sign a written request. This request
must include a detailed explanation of why the relevant authorities are unavailable, along with
a clear justification for the emergency. The request must be validated by key stakeholders,
such as the medical director, medical coordinator, and the responsible pharmacist (MSF supply
centre).

Once the request is ready, it should be sent to the MSF supply centre,s which will prepare
the shipment based on the request and documentation provided by the MSF program. The
controlled drugs are normally shipped along with the associated documentation. Upon
reception, the Medical Coordinator must promptly send an acknowledgement of receipt, which
is then used by the MSF supply centre to facilitate reconciliation with the national authority.

4.5.3 Searching for information

– There is no MSF section operating in the country

• It is important to complete the Checklist for Explo, see Toolbox, Checklist for EXPLO.

• If MSF has been present in the country recently, it is important to investigate the
potential existence of a previously dormant registration, MoU, or HCA that may still be
valid or could be easily reactivated. This information may be available at the level of the
intersectional legal department (ILD).

– There is an MSF section already operating in the country

• Information regarding importation processes, requirements, and constraints may be
available in different repositories. See more details in Section 4.6.

• While gathering information, the Checklist for Explo (See Toolbox) can be used to ensure
that no critical information is overlooked.

• Additionally, it may be beneficial to consult with existing logistics and health cluster
platforms that involve other NGOs or agencies within the county.

r The EUL procedure may be employed in circumstances where the competent authority is no longer

operational—such as during civil wars or in regions controlled by guerrilla forces—or where the competent
authority is unable to issue import permits.
s To the responsible pharmacist or responsible person.

Chapter 4: Importation of medical products

4.6 Internal resources for MSF programs

Managing the import requirements for medical products demands a multi-stakeholder,
interdisciplinary approach, along with a thorough understanding and expertise in highly
technical aspects.

This complexity can sometimes create challenges for MSF programs, requiring additional
support. Fortunately, valuable resources are available to assist MSF programs.

4.6.1 Tools and websites

– Country supply profile (CSP) GeoAppt

• CSP GeoApp is a dynamic Geographic Information System (GIS) designed to enhance
inter-departmental (legal, supply, and pharmacy) and intersectional collaboration
regarding critical information related to importation requirements.

• The objective of this platform is to consolidate legal, supply, and pharmaceutical
information for each country into a field-driven tool, providing valuable information for
all MSF sections present in the country.

• Its development is guided by the principles of intersectional harmonisation and
mutualisation, to minimise duplication of efforts and, consequently, reduce workload for
MSF programs.

• The tool will be gradually rolled out to various countries, with a primary focus on those
where importation poses greater challenges.

– Regulatory affairs pharmacist SharePointu: a repository designated for non-batch-related

documents about medical products. It also includes specific supporting documentation for
countries with stringent import requirements, along with a mapping of the validated and
verified importation requirements in the country constraints sheet (CCS).

– MSF supply centre websites or intranets: they may provide additional and helpful

information (e.g., a list of drugs that require an export license).

4.6.2 MSF expertise

MSF country support on importation (CSI) platform: an MSF intersectional and
interdepartmental platform that provides multidisciplinary and cross-departmental support to
MSF programs and MSF supply centres regarding importation requirements and constraints.

This platform is composed of:

– Intersectional importation and customs advisor:

Coordinates the CSI platform.

5.1 Introduction to Part 1 - GSDP

Good Storage and Distribution Practices (GSDP) refer to a set of guidelines and standards
aimed at ensuring the proper storage, handling, and transportation of medical products. GSDP
ensure that products maintain their quality, safety, and efficacy throughout the supply chain,
from manufacturers to end users.

This chapter provides MSF programs with a comprehensive framework for implementing GSDP
across the medical product supply chain. The requirements and recommendations outlined in
this chapter may need to be adapted to align with specific MSF operational contexts or comply
with national regulations.

This chapter covers all aspects of storage and distribution processes managed by MSF
programs, focusing on medical warehouses and distribution pharmacies (including hospital
pharmacies). These units differ mainly in their functions, focus areas, and operational activities
(see Appendix 8, Storage and distribution units. This chapter does not cover end-user units
(see Chapter 6).

5.1.1. Critical elements to maintain safety and effectiveness

The following elements are essential for maintaining the safety and effectiveness of medical
products during storage and distribution:

– Adequate distribution and storage conditions: all medical products must be transported

and stored according to the manufacturer’s specificationsa. Deviations from these
specifications may compromise the product’s quality, posing risks to patients due to
reduced safety and efficacy.

– Comprehensive traceability: it enhances accountability and ensures prompt recalls of

products in case of potential quality issues before reaching patients.

– Appropriate handling by trained and qualified staff supported by SOPsb: an adequate

number of trained and qualified staff must ensure that products are stored and distributed
correctly to minimise the risk of misuse or mishandling. Up-to-date, validated written
procedures must guide operational tasks while complying with national regulations.

– Efficient stock management: efficient stock management maintains the quality and safety

of medical products, prevents stockoutsc that can delay treatments and harm patient care,
and reduces waste and financial losses.

At MSF, the goal is to comply with WHO’s GSDP standards (see Toolbox, WHO Good storage
and distribution practices for medical products) as well as those set by national authorities.

During emergencies, MSF programs must adhere to GSDP to the greatest extent

possible. Support from headquarters (HQ) should be sought if necessary.

a Manufacturers' specifications, including temperature and relative humidity storage ranges, are derived from

rigorous stability studies designed to determine the thresholds for medical products.
b Standard Operating Procedures.
c Situation in which stock levels for a particular product within the MSL are depleted, making it impossible to

fulfil the operational demands for that product.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

90

5.1.2. Risks addressed by GSDP

– Risks to patients: inappropriately stored or transported medical products may become

ineffective or harmful. Additionally, a weak supply chain in terms of GSDP increases the
risk of distributing falsifiedd, substandarde, or segregated (e.g., quarantined) medical
products.

– Risk to public health: medications distributed without appropriate oversight, especially

controlled drugsf (e.g., narcotics, psychotropics, etc.), may pose a significant public health
risk.

– Financial risks: inadequate management of medical products may result in financial losses

(e.g., due to expired or damaged products, theft, etc.), which in turn increases costs
associated with disposal and reordering.

– Legal risks: non-compliance with national regulations may pose legal risks to MSF.

This includes potential repercussions for the MSF-registered pharmacist, who is often
responsible for the warehouse or pharmacy license.

d Falsified medical product: a product that has been deliberately or fraudulently misrepresented in terms of its

identity, composition or source. Such deliberate or fraudulent misrepresentation can involve the substitution,
adulteration or reproduction of an authorised product, or the manufacture of a product that is not authorised.
e Substandard medical products also known as "out of specification" products, these are products that have

been authorised by NRAs but fail to meet national or international quality standards or specifications – or, in
some cases, both.
f A controlled drug is a drug or other substance that is strictly regulated by the government due to its potential

for abuse or addiction. The regulation governs the production, use, handling, storage, and distribution of the
substance. Controlled drugs include opioids, stimulants, depressants, hallucinogens, and anabolic steroids.
Their classification can vary from one country to another, depending on national legislation.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

91

5

5.2 Staff members

5.2.1 Roles and responsibilities

As an integral part of quality assurance, the overall accountability for GSDP within MSF
programs typically falls under the medical team, particularly the pharmacists. Depending
on the HR setup of each MSF section, some GSDP responsibilities and related tasks may be
delegated to other departments, such as supply or logistics.

Successful management of GSDP requires effective communication and robust
interdepartmental collaboration.

Key responsibilities related to GSDP

While the key responsibilities related to GSDP should remain consistent across different MSF
sections, the specific positions assigned to handle them may vary. The roles and responsibilities
outlined below may be fulfilled by one or more staff members:

– GSDP responsible staff member: supervises and oversees GSDP, ensuring compliance,

providing training, implementing corrective and preventive action (CAPA)g, and developing
SOPs if required. For more information concerning CAPA, see Section 5.6.

– Stock owner: manages stock budgeting and holds overall accountability for GSDP

compliance.

– Stock manager: ensures medical products are stored and distributed according to GSDP

requirements, safeguarding their quality during daily operations. The stock manager may
also be responsible for the security and safety of the premises, ensuring that all MSF
internal procedures and mitigation measures are in place and that staff members follow
them.

5.2.2 Training

– All staff involved in the storage and distribution of medical products, regardless of

their background, must undergo tailored GSDP training specific to their roles and
responsibilities. The GSDP responsible staff member must ensure that all staff are
adequately trained in relevant GSDP procedures.

– Induction training for new staff members should include the existing written procedures to

ensure they are familiar with their tasksh.

– Refresher training should be conducted at least biannually or whenever procedures are

updated. Extra training sessions should also be arranged as needed to address specific
issues or requirements.

– Staff must receive training on critical subjects such as equipment use and maintenance,

daily record-keeping, emergency protocols, batch recall processes, cold chain and

g The corrective and preventive actions (CAPA) approach is designed to ensure that, in the event of deviations

or nonconformities from the desired standards for proper storage and distribution practices, appropriate
measures are promptly implemented, and their subsequent monitoring is ensured.
h All staff members must be aware of their tasks and responsibilities, which should be clearly outlined in their

job descriptions.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

92

controlled drugs management, handling of dangerous goodsi, and managing substandard
or falsified medications.

– All training activities must be documented in a training register and archived appropriately.

– Training materials should align with the latest MSF GSDP protocols or HQ

recommendations.

When national regulations mandate specific qualifications and training for certain

staff members (e.g., nationally registered pharmacists), MSF must comply with these
requirements.

5.2.3 Security and safety

– All staff members must be aware of securityj and safetyk procedures to safeguard their

own well-being and ensure the integrity of medical products.

– Visitors must always be accompanied by an authorised staff member and strictly adhere to

the security and safety procedures in place.

– Staff members must be provided with appropriate personal protective equipment based

on the potential risks associated with handling the products.

For more detailed information concerning the security and safety at the premises, see Section
5.3.2.

5.2.4 Hygiene and behaviour

Maintaining high standards of hygiene among staff is essential to prevent contamination and
ensure the safety, quality, and integrity of medical products.

– Hygiene procedures must be strictly respected by staff.

– Handwashing is required before handling medical products. Accessible handwashing

stations must be provided, and staff should adhere to the WHO-recommended
handwashing techniques.

– Food and beverages are prohibited except in the kitchen or canteen, if available.

– A designated area for personal belongings should be provided. (see Section 5.3.5).

– Smoking is strictly prohibited within the facilities.

– Clear signage outlining hygiene and behavioural rules should be displayed throughout the

premises to remind staff of best practices.

– Hygiene and behavioural procedures may be tailored to the specific activities conducted

within the facility.

i Dangerous goods are substances that pose potential risks to staff, property, or the environment due to their

hazardous chemical or physical properties. They are usually classified based on their immediate risk (UN code).
j Security: protection against intentionally harmful acts.
k Safety: protection against unintentional accidents.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

93

5

Note

• A cleaning programme and register should be in place to ensure the premises and
equipment are kept clean and dry.

• The cleaning materials and agents must not represent a source of contamination for the
medical products.

• To prevent an increase in humidity, avoid washing the floor with excessive water where
medical products are stored.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

94

5.3 Premises

5.3.1 Selection of premises

From the perspective of GSDP, several critical factors must be carefully considered when
selecting premises.

Physical considerations

– The insulation of the premises, particularly in the roof and walls, should be thoroughly

assessed. The premises must be sufficiently secure to prevent moisture caused by water
leakage, infiltration, or similar issues. Insulation is one of the factors that determines the
heating, ventilation, and air conditioning (HVAC) needs of the premises.

– The feasibility of maintaining well-ventilated areas should be assessed.

– The structural integrity must be thoroughly evaluated (e.g., checking for reduced load-

bearing capacity due to a damaged concrete slab, etc.).

– The floors must be robust and easy to clean.

– Access to clean water must be ensured.

– Single-level premises and ground-floor access are preferred to streamline loading and

unloading processes. The use of intermediate floors or levels (mezzanines) to enhance
storage capacity is not advisable, as it can disrupt the flow of products and staff.

If adequate infrastructure is unavailable, temporary infrastructure should meet the

requirements to the greatest extent possible.

Regulatory and administrative considerations

– It is important to understand the national regulations applicable to humanitarian partners,

as certain legislation may exist but might not apply to organisations such as MSF (e.g.,
some requirements may apply exclusively to registered pharmacies).

– National GSDP guidelines should be available and followed whenever applicable.

Location considerations

– The location must offer continuous access, with enough space for vehicle movement under

all weather conditions. Prepositioned stocks, such as EPREPl, should remain accessible at
all times, even during security incidents.

For general guidance about premises size estimation, see Appendix 9.

5.3.2 Security and safety

Maintaining a secure and safe work environment is essential for protecting staff, safeguarding
medical products, and ensuring the operational continuity of MSF activities. Achieving this
requires a proactive approach, adequate resource investment, and continuous improvement
to effectively mitigate risks.

l Emergency preparedness plan

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

95

5

This section does not provide comprehensive guidance on security and safety


management but offers key general recommendations and measures for managing
premises designated for the storage of medical products. These recommendations should be
tailored to the specific security context of each MSF program.

Security measures

Premises infrastructure

– The premises must be secured with walls or hedges, supported by robust external lighting

and power backup systems.

– Doors and windows should be reinforced (e.g., built with durable materials, secure locks,

window grills, anti-blast films, etc.).

Access control

– The number of entry and exit points to the premises should be limited.

– Access to the premises must be restricted to authorised staff members onlym.

– All visitors must receive formal approval, and their visits must be logged. Visitors should

always be accompanied. Only authorised vehicles should be parked within the premises.

– A stringent key management SOP must be implemented, including maintaining a keyholder

logn. If the integrity of the locks and keys is uncertain, they should be replaced.

Anti-theft measures

– Strict access control measures, as outlined above, should be enforced.

– Adequate lighting must be provided at entry points and storage areas to avoid

unauthorised access and improve visibility for surveillance.

– Tasks must be carried out exclusively by authorised staff members in accordance with their

job descriptions to ensure transparent accountability.

– Strict validation procedures for stock movements must be implemented (e.g., dual signingo

on key documents).

– A robust documentation system must provide comprehensive and transparent traceability

for all stock movements.

– Occasional and unannounced partial physical counts can be conducted to monitor high-

demand or easily concealed products (e.g., antibiotics, psychotropics, narcotics, controlled
drugs, and antiretroviral drugs). Additional monitoring is advised for products with
previous unexplained negative discrepanciesp or frequent stockouts.

Safety measures

– All staff should be trained in accident prevention and the management of possible

incidents.

m However, stock owners, such as medical coordinators, and representatives, such as pharmacists or medical

activity managers (MAM), should be granted access to the premises at all times, accompanied by the stock
manager or other responsible person if required.
n Keyholder log refers to a formal record used to document the management and accountability of physical keys

for accessing restricted areas.
o A control measure used to enhance security. Two staff members are required to verify and sign off on certain

activities. For more details, refer to HQ.
p Negative discrepancies identified during physical inventories should be documented and investigated (see

Section 5.10.5).

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

96

– SOPs for handling situations or incidents that could pose a risk to staff, stock, or facility

integrity must be easily accessible to all staff members.

– The necessary resources for incident management must be readily available (e.g., soda

ash, sand, fire extinguishers, gloves, cleaning materials, spill-box for cytotoxins, etc.).

– Emergency contact information and exit maps should be displayed prominently

throughout the premises. A clearly marked meeting point must be designated for
emergency assembly.

– When feasible, consider obtaining insurance to cover the premises and its contents.

Fire precautions

– Ensure compliance with fire safety standardsq for the facility and electrical setups. Store

reactive products separately, maintain cleanliness to reduce fire risks, and enforce a strict
no-smoking policy with visible signage.

– Install and regularly maintain smoke detectors and fire alarms.

– Equip the facility with fire extinguishers that are inspected regularly by certified

companies. Sand buckets and a water hose connected to an external tap should also be
available.

5.3.3 Pest control

Pests such as rodents, birds, and insects pose a risk by contaminating stored medical products
with harmful microorganisms, including Salmonella and Staphylococcus. They can also damage
packaging, compromise its sterility and posing serious health risks.

Pest control refers to the management and prevention of pests that can infest and damage
stored products. Effective pest control, combined with stringent hygiene standards, is crucial
for safeguarding the quality and safety of medical products. It must be implemented across all
premises that store medical products.

Pest control must be overseen and supervised by an environmental health or water and
sanitation (watsan) specialist or focal point. This section addresses key aspects of pest control
that stock managers and staff members responsible for GSDP should understand and be aware
of.

Note

• Pest control equipment and methodologies must not compromise the integrity of
medical products.

• The use of cats for pest control is strictly prohibited due to potential hygiene risks.

q As established by each MSF section.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

97

5

Outsourcing pest control

– Pest control should preferably be outsourced to specialised companies.

– When selecting a company, ensure it complies with national regulations.

– Contracts must clearly outline the terms, including equipment, program duration,

inspection frequencyr, targeted pests, and mutual expectations between MSF and the
service provider.

– During inspections, the contractor must examine all insect monitoring and rodent bait

stations and conduct thorough checks in high-risk areas for signs of pest activity.

– Monitoring and bait stations must be individually numbered, clearly marked with a sticker

or poster, and their locations must be recorded on a map.

– After each inspection, the contractor must provide a report to be reviewed, signed, and

properly archived by MSF staff.

Pest control handled by MSF

– Effective pest control requires a proactive approach, integrating preventive measures,

control methods, and monitoring, while ensuring compliance with national regulations.

– A calendar with scheduled internal inspections should be implemented for regular

monitoring.

– Staff must immediately report any pest sightings for prompt intervention and efficient pest

management.

Pest control devices

– A clear schedule for replacing pest control devices must be established.

– A comprehensive map should outline all rooms, doors, windows, and, where feasible,

airflow patterns, to guide the strategic placement of pest control devices. (see Appendix 10).

– Devices should be placed in hotspots, such as entrances, pathways to kitchens, and areas

with daylight or air currents, or at heights of 2 to 3 meters to ensure accessibility. Signs of
pest activity (e.g., droppings, gnaw marks, odours) should guide the placement of devices.

– Each device should be marked with a visible poster or sticker and documented on the map

(see Appendix 10).

– Pest control equipment (e.g., UV lamps, bait stations, etc.) should be inventoried within

the logistic stock for effective stock management.

Pest treatments

– Only specialised staff should be authorised to conduct pest treatments. Treatments

should be selected in accordance with national legislation, previous treatments, and WHO
guidelines.

– While treatments reduce infestation levels, they do NOT replace the need for ongoing

prevention efforts.

– If treatment is required, the entire facility must be treated to prevent pests from relocating

and returning.

– Surfaces must be cleaned and grease-free before treatment, and tools, food, and food

preparation areas must be secured to prevent contamination.

– All pesticides must be stored in a dedicated, locked, and labelled cupboard.

Key considerations for premises management

Whether pest control is outsourced or managed internally by MSF, the following measures
should be considered at the premises:

Preventive measures

– Staff must consistently uphold strict hygiene standards (see Section 5.2.4).

– Rigorous cleaning schedules must be maintained to avoid attracting pests.

– All potential entry points larger than 1.2 cm for rats and 0.65 cm for mice must be sealed.

– Grilles and meshes must be installed on windows and air vents to prevent pest entry.

Corrective actions for increased pest activity

– Conduct a physical check (preferably with an expert) to identify the root cause and

implement measures based on evidence.

– Windows and air vents must be inspected to ensure grilles and meshes are properly

secured.

– Structural openings around air conditioners, doors, and windows must be gap-free.

– Cracks and holes in floors, walls, ceilings, and around plumbing fixtures, furnace flues,

electrical outlets, and windowsills must be sealed.

– Structural damages, such as leaks in water or heating systems, blocked drainage, or

decaying wood, must be repaired.

– Vegetation must be maintained at least 1 metre away from building structures to

discourage pest shelter.

– The flow of products and staff can be revisited to minimise prolonged door openings.

– Surveillance of incoming materials, especially food, must be intensified, and the placement

of traps must be reviewed as needed.

5.3.4 Flow management

The layout of the premises should reflect a logical sequence of operations to facilitate the
smooth and efficient movement of staff members, equipment (manual or motorised), and
medical products.

Flow management refers to the systematic coordination and optimisation of the movement

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

99

5

of medical products and activities within the premises. This approach is crucial for minimising
handling time and maximising operational efficiency.

Key considerations

– When evaluating the layout, it is important to distinguish the flow of products (e.g.,

movement from reception to storage to dispatch) from the flow of staff and equipment
(e.g., accessing workstations or meeting areas) to prevent disruptions and ensure
efficiency.

– To optimise accessibility and minimise disruptions, staff movements must be considered

when arranging products.

– An optimal layout should allow staff and visitors to enter through the office, providing

visibility of entrances and storage areas.

– To uphold temperature consistency throughout the premises, it is crucial to have distinct

entrances for medical products and staff.

– An updated floor plan must be displayed at the premises entrance. This floor plan should

clearly indicate the flow of products, staff movements and the locations of products in the
storage area.

Note

• Access routes, including ramps and stairs, must always remain clear of obstructions.

• Corridors should be sufficiently wide to facilitate the smooth movement of both manual
and motorised equipment.

5.3.5 Operational areas

The specific operational areas within the premises depend on the type of storage and
distribution unit (see Appendix 8) and the activities conducted.

Below are the key operational areas:

Loading/unloading area

– This is a designated space for transferring medical products between the premises and

vehicles, designed to protect these medical products from environmental factors.

– Ideally, it should be integrated into the facility for direct loading or unloading. If direct

loading is not feasible, a protective canopy is recommended.

Reception area

– This space is designated for receiving, inspecting and sorting incoming goods before they

are stored. Unpacking tasks, such as splitting pallets into smaller units, can be performed
in the area.

– It should be located next to the loading/unloading area to maintain logical flow and

prevent misplacement into the dispatch area.

– It must be physically separated from other areas and sized to accommodate the average

volume of incoming shipments.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

100

– It must provide a temperature- and humidity-controlled environments.

– A non-conformity zonet may be established to segregate products that either fail to pass

quality control upon reception (see Section 5.10.1) or do not align with the specifications
outlined in the packaging list.

Main storage area

– Medical products must be segregated from logistical products to prevent cross-

contaminationu and ensure compliance with GSDP.

– The area must provide a temperature- and humidity-controlled environment.

– Where possible, it is highly recommended to consolidate the storage area into a single

large room to enhance operational efficiency.

– Fluorescent lights and natural sunlight must be avoided to protect products. It is

recommended to use LED lighting (8-12 w/m2). Aisle widths should be adjusted based on
the heightv of the light source to optimise lighting coverage and ensure all product labels
remain visible. Lights should be placed above pathways for maximum coverage.

– A backup lighting system, such as solar lamps or torches, should be available to maintain

visibility during power outages.

Specific storage areas

Controlled drugs area

– Controlled drugs must be stored in a dedicated, separate area with additional safety and

security measures in compliance with national legislation.

– The area must be securely locked in a visible section of the storage area, whether in a

cupboard, a cage or a room.

– The area must provide a temperature- and humidity-controlled environment.

– Access to this area must be strictly controlled; only a limited number of authorised staff

members should have access.

For specific considerations about controlled drugs, see Section 5.11.1.

Cold chain area

– This is a designated space for keeping cold chain equipment.

– Ideally, it should be located in a separate temperature-controlled room to prevent heat

transfer and better preserve the equipment.

– Continuous power for cold chain devices must be ensured for 24 hours without

interruption. A reliable backup system is essential in the event of a primary power failure.

– Each cold chain storage facility must have a validated contingency plan outlining

responsibilities and procedures to be followed in the event of equipment failure or power
outage. This plan must be updated regularly, and all listed equipment must be prepared
and readily available at all times.

s The area is equipped and designed to maintain temperature and relative humidity within predefined ranges,

preserving the quality, safety, and stability of stored products.
t The non-conformity zone implies short-term storage during the reception process. Depending on the situation,

the products may either be moved into the medical stock or transferred to the quarantine or expired and
damaged area.
u Unintended transfer of chemical or microbiological contaminants can compromise the quality of a medical

product during storage, handling, or transportation.
v The aisle width should be increased proportionally to the height of the lighting source.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

101

5

– Active cold chain devices should be placed away from the floors, walls, and other devices

to minimise heat transfer and ensure optimal functioning.

– Temperature must be recorded and monitored daily using MSF standard temperature

monitoring devices.

– A suitable workspace should be available to prevent the use of storage devices (e.g.,

refrigerator/freezer doors) as working tables.

– If a cold chain room is necessary, it must be installed by a qualified technician following

MSF standards, including temperature mapping, door-opening tests, and thermostat
calibration.

For more detailed information, refer to MSF Cold Chain Management Guideline (see Toolbox).

Dangerous goods area

– Dangerous goods must be stored in a dedicated, separated area with additional safety and

security measures.

– The area must be identified with warning signs, and products must be appropriately

labelled.

– The area must provide a temperature- and humidity-controlled environment.

– The area must be well-ventilated, dry, cool, and protected from direct sunlight.

– It must be free of flammable materials (e.g., soaked rags or cardboard).

– Ideally, the area should be secured with a lock (e.g., aerated metallic cupboards) while still

allowing easy access to minimise risks (e.g., bottom shelves).

– Depending on the type of dangerous products stored, it may be necessary to have close

access to firefighting and spill clean-up equipment.

For specific considerations about dangerous goods, see Section 5.4.3.

Quarantine area

– This is a designated space for the temporary isolation of products affected by quality

issues (see Chapter 9), including non-conforming products received, returned products, or
suspected falsified products.

– The area must be separated from other areas and clearly identified to prevent any

unauthorised access to the products.

– It must provide a temperature- and humidity-controlled environment, as the products may

be released later and deemed suitable for use.

– The release of products requires approval from the responsible pharmacistw.

– An up-to-date list of all medical products temporarily stored in this area should be

maintained and readily accessible.

– Controlled drugs and cold chain products must be quarantined within their respective

areas, identified and isolated.

Expired and damaged area

– This area is designated for storing expired or damaged products awaiting proper disposal.

w This decision should be based on input from the appropriate quality team (HQ or MSF supply centres).

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

102

– It must be segregated from other storage areas, preferably in a separate building.

– It should be securely locked, located indoors, and clearly identified.

– The area does not require temperature and humidity control.

– Stock records must be maintained to ensure proper stock management.

For specific considerations about handling expired and damaged products, see Section 5.11.3.

Specific areas in prepositioned stocks

If a kit or module within a prepositioned stock (e.g., EPREP stock) contains controlled
drugs, cold chain products, or dangerous goods, these products must be clearly
marked and segregated (see GSDP, Part 2).

These products may be stored in the regular or general project medical stock or within

the prepositioned stocks.

Preparation area

– Designated space for sorting, packing, repacking, and preparing products for outgoing

shipment.

– Area must be a temperature and humidity-controlled environment.

– Equipped with sufficient worktables and packing equipment (e.g., boxes, tape, etc.) to

facilitate efficient order assembly.

– Ideally located near the dispatch area to streamline the workflow and minimise handling

time.

– In distribution pharmacies, the preparation area may include a deconditioning room for

repacking products according to the end-user unit’s needs.

Dispatch area

– Designated space for final preparation of outgoing shipments, where medical products are

correctly packed, labelled, and arranged for transportation.

– Area must be a temperature and humidity-controlled environment.

Other areas

Office

– It must be separated from the storage areas to maintain security and smooth workflow.

– Ideally located within the premises, providing visibility of entrances and storage areas to

oversee staff and visitor access.

Staff area

– Designated space for staff use (e.g., storing personal items, changing clothes, and taking

breaks).

– It must be separated from the storage area to preserve hygiene and security.

– Includes handwashing stations, which should also be located outside the storage area.

– Ideally connected to the office for easy access.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

103

5

5.4 Storage of medical products

Specific requirements for the different storage areas are described in Section 5.3.5.

5.4.1 Products arrangement

General considerations

– Medical products must not be stored directly on the floor or against walls. Instead,

shelves and pallets must be used. Plastic sheeting may be employed as a temporary and
exceptional measure.

– Products must be clearly identified by labels. Expiry date and batch number must always

be visible.

– Products must be organised to facilitate picking and prevent mix-ups. Whenever possible,

products should be stored alphabetically by product family (refer to MFS catalogues).
Product rotation, frequency of use, volume, and product weight could be considered to
optimise product organisation.

– Products without their original secondary packaging must be stored in containers made

from suitable materials that protect against direct sunlight and dust.

– Perishable products must be arranged by expiry date, clearly marked on the packaging,

preferably in the MM-YY format, to facilitate the use of the first expired, first out (FEFO)
method. Non-perishable products should be arranged by reception date to facilitate the
first-in, first-out (FIFO) method (see Section 5.10.2).

– Opened boxes should be marked (e.g., with a large cross) to facilitate identification and

prevent the opening of multiple boxes of the same product simultaneously.

– Storing more than one product per pallet should be avoided, whenever possible.

– For high-volume and heavy products, small quantities may be placed on shelves for

quick picking, with the remaining stock stored on pallets or racks. In such cases, a clear
indication should be placed on the shelves to inform staff that additional stock is located
elsewhere in the storage area.

Nutrition-specialised food

Nutrition-specialised food must always be stored on pallets, with each pallet containing only
one product type and batch number.

Stacking cartons

– The floor should be swept before placing pallets.

– Pallets should be solid, clean, and free of splinters or protruding ends.

– Cartons should be placed carefully without throwing them.

– Cartons should be piled and oriented properly on pallets.

• The first row should be positioned precisely at the edge of the pallet, to ensure that the
entire pallet area is covered.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

104

• Subsequent rows should be placed in the opposite direction to ensure self-stability.

• All rows should contain the same number of products, except for the last row if the pile
is incomplete.

Staking sacks

– The first row should not be tightly packed to ensure stability. Sack openings should face

inward within the pile.

– Pallets may be grouped in isles of four or six pallets for better stability (not advised in

humid climates). This applies only to sacks.

– For long-term storage, sacks should be re-piled monthly. Unstacking and re-piling ensure

that sacks from the bottom are moved to the top to avoid crushing and facilitate quality
controls.

Figure 5.1- Stacking cartons and sacks of specialised food

Source: MSF

5.4.2 Storage conditions: temperature and humidity

General considerations

– All medical products must be stored according to the manufacturer’s specifications.

– Temperature and humidityx must be recorded twice daily.

– Data should be monitoredy periodically. The monitoring frequency is determined by each

MSF section. The monitoring equipment should be properly calibrated and appropriate for
its intended purpose.

– HVAC systems must comply with MSF standardsz, ensuring protection against external

elements such as dust and sunlight.

– When setting up the HVAC system, it must be configured based on the temperature and

humidity requirements of the most sensitive products.

– Storage equipment (e.g., shelves, pallets, and racks) should be arranged uniformly, where

possible, to maintain consistent temperature conditions throughout the storage area.

Changes to the storage conditions must be approved by the GSDP responsible staff

member, who should provide the logistics team with the necessary technical requirements.

x Humidity refers to relative humidity, defined as the ratio of the partial pressure of water vapour in the air

to the saturation vapour pressure at a given temperature. Stating relative humidity without specifying the
associated temperature does not provide a complete picture of the air's moisture content.
y Monitoring entails the collection and analysis of data gathered.
z Defined by the Logistics department of each MSF section.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management
