5.4.3 Storage of dangerous goods

See Appendix 11 for information about common risks associated with dangerous goods.

– For requirements of the dangerous goods area, see Section 5.3.5.

– Compliance with the national regulations must be ensured.

– Any instructions related to the safe storage and handling provided by the manufacturer

must be strictly applied.

– If available, the material safety data sheetaa (MSDS) or similar documents (e.g., SDSab or

FDS ac) should be stored next to the products.

– Dangerous goods should be stored in closed boxes or containers when not in use, on

durable surfaces, and arranged to minimise risks of falling and leaking affecting other
goods.

aa MSDS provides critical information on the properties, handling, storage, transport, and emergency procedures

for hazardous materials. MSDS may also be used for importation purposes and is often provided by the supplier.
ab Safety data sheet, include details on chemical properties, hazards, protective measures, and safety precautions

for handling, storing, and transporting chemicals.
ac Fire data sheet is a technical document of a product or material that typically focuses specifically on fire-

related hazards, properties, and extinguishing methods.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

106

– Dangerous goods in prepositioned stocks may be stored either in the dangerous goods

area or, if safety conditions are met, within a specific zone of the prepositioned stock.

– Dangerous goods must never be stored next to products intended for use in humans.

– When storing cytotoxic medications, it is essential to have an accessible cytotoxic spill box

that is identified and visible. The staff members should be well-trained in its use.

Segregation of incompatible products

Depending on the type of dangerous goods, it may be necessary to physically
segregate them to prevent hazardous reactions that could lead to fires, explosions,
toxic gas releases, or other dangerous situations (see Appendix 12).

5.4.4 Storage equipment

Shelves, pallets, and racks must be positioned away from walls (ideally at least 30 cm). Storing
medical products away from walls and off the floor protects against flooding, spills, pests, dirt,
and dust. This practice also enhances air circulation, which is crucial for maintaining adequate
temperature and humidity control. Additionally, it facilitates easier cleaning and access to
products.

Shelves

– Shelves are used for storing small-volume products or products that require picking in

small quantities.

– They must be durable, stable, and made from materials that are easy to clean. Metal

shelves are recommended for areas prone to termites. While wooden shelves can be used,
they should be treated with termite repellent and painted for protection due to cleaning
difficulties.

– Ensure there is sufficient space between shelves to facilitate staff movement and efficient

handling of products.

Pallets

– Pallets are used for storing large volumes and empty boxes.

– Plastic pallets are preferable due to their ease of cleaning. If wooden pallets are used, pre-

fumigated pallets are recommended to prevent termite infestation.

– Maintain adequate distances between pallet rows to accommodate manual and motorised

handling equipment, allowing easy access.

Racks

– Use pallet racks for multi-level stacking to maximise storage space.

– Racks must be compatible with the specific dimensions and characteristics of the pallets

being stored.

– Content of pallets stored on upper racks must be wrapped in film or otherwise adequately

secured to prevent boxes from falling during handling.

– Corridors should be at least 180 cm wide for pallet jack manoeuvres and 240 cm wide for

forklift operations.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

107

5

Cold chain equipment

– The selection of cold chain equipment should involve collaboration between the medical

and logistics teams, considering aspects like energy requirements, maintenance options,
and storage capacity.

For more detailed information, refer to the MSF Cold Chain Management Guideline (see
Toolbox).

Note

Storage equipment must be well-maintained and clean. Cold chain equipment, racks
and handling equipment (e.g., pallet jacks and forklifts) should be routinely
maintained by qualified staff. A dedicated maintenance register should be established
and kept up to date.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

108

5.5 Distribution

Distribution refers to the activities involved in moving medical products from the supplier
(point of origin) to the intended recipients. This process encompasses activities such as in-
country transport, deliveries and transfer of stock. Distribution does not include dispensing
activities.

– In-country transport: it involves transporting medical products within the borders of a

specific country. It normally entails extended routes and durations and is occasionally
managed by outsourced companies.

– Delivery: it typically involves moving products between storage and distribution units

within an MSF project, as well as moving products to a third partyad (either MSF or an
external entity). It may or may not require transportation.

– Stock transfer: it typically involves moving products from one stock area to another within

the same stock location (e.g., medical warehouse, distribution pharmacy). In this context,
stock transfer is understood as a stock management process that typically does not involve
transportation.

5.5.1. Transport of medical products

Medical products should be transported in accordance with the conditions stated on the labels
and described by the manufacturer. The risk to the quality of the medical product during
transport and distribution should be eliminated or minimised to an acceptable level.1

Administrative aspects

– Comprehensive and transparent traceability must be ensured by establishing a robust

documentation system and appropriate archiving procedures.

– Medical products being transported should have the necessary transport documentation,

such as cargo manifest and packing lists.

– Compliance with national regulations and guidelines for each mode of transportation must

be maintained.

– For outsourced transport, contracts should clearly specify the route, storage requirements,

scheduled stops, and communication protocols for any route changes.

Risk management

– Risk assessments should be conducted to identify potential hazards during transport and

develop mitigation strategies.

– Orders and transport schedules should prioritise consolidating shipments into a few larger

shipments rather than many small ones.

– Medical products must not be transported alongside potentially contaminating substances

such as fuel, fresh food, or chemicals.

ad A third party refers to an external actor to MSF, such as the Ministry of Health (MoH), other NGOs or other

types of partners, or an internal actor within MSF, such as other projects or programs from the same MSF
section or from different sections.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

109

5

– Stack height should be limited to a maximum of 3 metres to ensure safety and integrity of

products during transport.

– Trucks should be equipped with locking systems to prevent tampering with parcels during

transportation.

– Whenever possible, it is recommended to obtain insurance for both the vehicle and the

cargo. For outsourced services, the extent of insurance coverage must be confirmed.

Mode of transport

– The appropriate mode of transport must be selected based on safety, cost, speed,

reliability, and the specific requirements of the medical products being transported.

– Controlled temperature transportation systems should be used whenever available, as

they are the preferred method for maintaining product integrity.

– Hard-top vehicles are recommended to protect medical products against external

environmental conditions. Canvas trucks and open pickups should be avoided to prevent
exposure to harmful weather.

Reducing carbon footprint

Efforts should be made to rationalise transportation through efficient planning that

optimises resource utilisation and minimises the carbon footprint.

Temperature management

– Temperature should be monitored during transportation using devices, such as log tags.

Freeze tags should be used in countries where the temperature may drop below 0 °C.

– Transportation should be scheduled during the cooler parts of the day, whenever possible,

to prevent exposure to temperatures that exceed the products' thermo-sensitivity limits.

For more detailed information about cold chain equipment used during transportation, refer
to the MSF Cold Chain Management Guideline (see Toolbox). For specific transport challenges
or tailored solutions, contact the supply/logistics or pharmacy referent at HQ.

5.5.2. Transport of nutrition-specialised food

– Transportation of large volumes is often required. Ensure the arrangement of an adequate

number and appropriate types of vehicles and consider loading product on pallets to
facilitate the loading and unloading.

– For detailed guidance on transporting nutrition-specialised food domestically via air

(including helicopters), refer to the WFP Guideline Managing the Supply Chain of
Specialised Nutritious Foods (see Toolbox).

5.5.3. Transport of dangerous goods

– An international regulatory framework exists for the transport of dangerous goods, which

varies depending on the mode of transportation (e.g., air, sea, road, etc.). It is important to
comply with international regulations and national regulations.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

110

– Requirements specified on the packaging must be respected, including any specified

maximum quantity limit for transportation.

– The original packaging should be maintained until the products reach the final destination.

– If the product has been repacked, the product’s hazard class must be visible on the

packaging.

– All dangerous goods must be marked on the transport documents, such as the packing list,

and the UN classification should be specified if available.

– Hard-top vehicles must be used, as they provide shelter from external environmental

conditions. Canvas trucks and open pickups must be avoided as they expose goods to
potentially harmful weather conditions.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

111

5

5.6 Self-inspection

Self-inspection is an internal process used to assess GSDP compliance across all operational
areas (see Section 5.3.5) within storage premises, including warehouses and distribution
pharmacies. Its purpose is to identify deficiencies, propose recommendations, and implement
the required CAPA.

Key aspects to consider

– Self-inspections must be conducted by the person identified as GSDP's responsible staff

member in collaboration with pertinent staff members (e.g., supply-logistics manager,
warehouse manager, warehouse supervisor, pharmacy manager, pharmacy supervisor,
storekeeper, etc.).

– Self-inspections should be conducted regularly.

– Tools and frequency for self-inspections are determined internally by each MSF section.

Refer to the HQ referent for further guidance.

CAPA follow-up

– If deviationsae or non-conformitiesaf are identified during a self-inspection, CAPA should be

initiated and monitored.

– Tasks and timelines should be clearly outlined and communicated to all relevant staff

members.

– Non-conformities or deviations should be corrected and closed within the agreed

timeframe.

– Once all CAPAs are closed, the self-inspection report must be archived and made available

for consultation as needed.

ae In this context, a deviation refers to any departure from established procedures, guidelines, or operational

standards. It may not always directly impact product quality or safety, but indicates a variance from expected
practices.
af A non-conformity is a more critical deviation that may affect product quality or safety.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

112

5.7 Documentation

“Documentation encompasses all procedures, records, and data, whether in paper or electronic
form. Documents should be appropriately designed, completed, reviewed, authorised,
distributed, and kept as required. They should also be readily available”.2

Adequate documentation supports compliance with GSDP standards, guarantees traceability
across the supply chain, and ensures accountability for processes.

Document retention (archiving)

Archiving refers to the long-term storage and preservation of documents that are no longer
actively used but must be retained for historical, legal, or regulatory purposes. The list of
documents to be retained and their retention duration is determined by each MSF section and
possible national regulations.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

113

5

5.8 Introduction to Part 2 - Stock management

The objective of this part is to provide MSF programs with a harmonised framework that
consolidates the fundamental concepts and considerations for managing medical product
stocks.

By clarifying essential concepts and aspects of stock management, this part aims to promote
alignment among the MSF sections, bridging the medical, logistics and supply teams, regardless
of the stock management tools used by each MSF section.

This part particularly concerns various storage and distribution units, including medical
warehouses, distribution pharmacies, and hospital pharmacies. The difference between these
units primarily lies in their functions, focus areas, and operational activities (see detailed
information in Appendix 8).

For more information about stock management in end-user units, see Chapter 6.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

114

5.9 Stock positioning strategies

Stock positioning strategies aim to optimise the distribution process, ensure timely delivery,
and respond efficiently to the MSF program’s needs. The choice between decentralised and
centralised stock strategies depends on various factors, including, but not limited to, the socio-
political context within countries or regions, available supply-chain routes, project locations,
types of medical activities, product characteristics, and overall supply chain objectives.

These stock positioning strategies are dynamic and can change in response to evolving contexts
and all the factors described above. Likewise, different stock positioning strategies can be
implemented for distinct projects within the same MSF program.

Additionally, in certain contexts, it may be decided to implement an intersectional mutualised
medical stock system to optimise resources among MSF sections. This approach can include
the use of shared facilities like warehouses or just specific storage areas like cold chain rooms,
among others.

Figure 5.2. - Stock positioning strategies

5.9.1 Centralised unallocated stock strategy

How it works

– The stock is kept in a single central location (central stock), which replenishes the project

stocks at specific intervals.

– The central stock remains unallocated until a demand arises from the projects.

– Storage volume and associated costs (e.g., human resources) increase at the central stock

location while decreasing at project stock locations.

Advantages

– Simplifies stock management and oversight.

– Streamlines the replenishment process, including order calculations.

– Often implies more consolidated orders, which can help lower costs and reduce the

workload involved in the import process.

– Avoids duplication of stock across multiple locations, facilitating overall stock management

and reducing, in principle, the likelihood of overstocks and sleeping stock, especially when
projects use similar products for similar activities.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

115

5

Disadvantages

– Accountability for processes such as forecasting, budgeting, and ordering is shared

between coordination and project teams, leading to sometimes unclear delineation of
responsibilities. Project teams sometimes overlook their role in budgeting, forecasting, and
order calculation, often assuming these tasks fall primarily to the coordination team.

– Allocating losses among different projects may be challenging.

– Monitoring the budget for each project becomes more complex.

– In specific scenarios, the supply chain may become more vulnerable to disruptions (e.g.,

natural disasters and political instability close to the central stock).

– Responsiveness to sudden, unexpected demand may be lower compared to a

decentralised strategy.

– Transportation costs within the country may be higher compared to a decentralised

strategy.

5.9.2 Centralised allocated stock strategy

How it works

– The stock for two or more projects is stored in a central location (central stocks) but is

physically segregated for each project (allocated). The central stocks replenish the project
stocks at specific intervals.

– Exchanges between project stocks are processed through loans or donations.

– While storage volume and associated costs (e.g., human resources) increase at central

stock locations, they decrease at project stock locations.

Advantages

– Budget monitoring is simpler compared to the centralised unallocated strategy.

– Transferring stock between projects is less costly than it is with the decentralised model.

Disadvantages

– It is more complex to obtain a comprehensive view of all the stocks compared to a

centralised unallocated strategy.

– Accountability for processes such as forecasting, budgeting, and ordering is shared

between coordination teams and project teams, leading to unclear delineation of
responsibilities. Project teams sometimes overlook their role in budgeting, forecasting,
and order calculation, often assuming these tasks are primarily the responsibility of the
coordination team.

– In-country transportation costs are higher than those associated with a decentralised

strategy.

– Responsiveness to sudden, unexpected demand is lower compared to a decentralised

strategy.

– It requires more storage capacity and often a larger workforce than a centralised

unallocated stock, as the stocks must be physically separated.

5.9.3 Decentralised stock strategy

How it works

– Stock is stored closer to the points of demand.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

116

– The replenishment process is decentralised.

– Storage volume and associated costs (e.g., human resources) increase at project stock

locations and decrease at central locations.

– If stock must pass the central location, it is dispatched as soon as possible to the

decentralised stock locations, via a cross-docking or transit stock.

– Exchanges between project stocks are processed through loans or donations.

Advantages

– Accountability for processes such as forecasting, budgeting, and ordering rests with the

project teams, making it more straightforward compared to centralised strategies.

– Stock control is increased at the point of demand, avoiding the need for intermediate

storage.

– Responsiveness to sudden, unexpected demand is higher compared to a centralised

strategy.

– In-country transportation costs are lower than those associated with centralised strategies.

– Simpler budget monitoring compared to a centralised unallocated strategy.

Disadvantages

– It is more complex to obtain a comprehensive view of all the stocks compared to a

centralised unallocated strategy.

– Exchanges between project stocks are more complicated than they are with centralised

strategies.

– There is a higher likelihood of overstocks and sleeping stocks within the MSF program.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

117

5

5.10 Handling medical products

5.10.1 Reception

During reception, incoming shipments must be carefully checked against the packing lists to
ensure the correct products and quantities have been received. Products must also be inspected
for damage, expiry dates, batch numbers, and any signs of tampering or contamination.

General considerations

– Staffing needs must be anticipated, and the reception area prepared with the necessary

documentation (e.g., packing lists, checklists, etc.) to ensure efficient handling of incoming
shipments.

– The number of parcels and the content listed on the packing list (or similar document)

must be verified. If any discrepancy is observed between the packaging list and the
physical count of received products (e.g., changes in quantities, products, etc.), the
affected products should be segregated, and a claim report should be initiated to notify
the supplier (MSF or external).

– Stock management tools (e.g., software, paper stock cards, orders follow-up, etc.) must be

meticulously updated after reception and based on the physical inventory count, rather
than the packaging list.

– Acknowledgement of reception must only be signed by an authorised staff member.

– Expired products must be rejected, and their packaging re-labelled to clearly identify them

as expired.

– Controlled drugs and cold-chain products must be immediately stored in their respective

area upon reception. When applicable, the transportation temperature data should be
checked to verify that the required conditions were maintained.

– Damaged packaging must be inspected immediately to assess the condition of the

contents.

– The packing list (or similar document) must be correctly archived and made available for

consultation as needed.

Quality control at reception

Quality control is essential upon reception, regardless of the supply source (MSF or
external, including incoming donations and loans). Quality control guarantees adherence
to MSF standards and safeguards the effectiveness and safety of medical products.
• For medical products in general, refer to HQ for the specific checklist in use (see
example of checklist in Appendix 13).

• For specialised food, see Appendix 14.

If a quality issue or noncompliance is identified or suspected during reception:
• The product must be transferred to the quarantine area.

• The pharmacist responsible for the stock must be informed and assess the situation.

• If the quality issue is confirmed, the Pharmacist Coordinator must initiate a quality
complaint (see Chapter 9).

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

118

Incoming shipments may be partially or fully rejected if the content does not adhere

to the agreed terms established with the third party, particularly concerning product
quality, specifications (e.g., shelf life, formulation, etc.), or other relevant criteria.

Specific considerations

Additional aspects must be considered for:

– Locally purchased productsag or incoming donations from non-MSF third party: the

manufacturer's details, such as the manufacturer’s name and manufacturing address, must
be verified against the information validated by the HQ pharmacy referent.

– Nutrition-specialised food:

• Special attention should be given to any signs of pests, foreign matter, decay, or
excessive humidity.

• The intersectional checklist should be used for quality control (see Appendix 14).

• If any quality issue is suspected, occasional analysis in an external laboratoryah may be
conducted to assess the condition of the product. Refer to the HQ pharmacy referent for
further information.

Returned products from end-user units

– Returned medical products should be handled following the established reception

procedures. Each return should be accompanied by a specific form (see Appendix 15).

– A pharmacist should conduct a risk analysis before determining the disposition of returned

products. This analysis can be conducted at the end-user unit or upon reception at a
higher distribution point (e.g., a medical warehouse, distribution pharmacy, or hospital
pharmacy).

– The risk analysis should consider the nature of the product, storage conditions at the end-

user unit, and the time-lapse since distribution.

– If additional time is needed to conclude the risk analysis (for example, to gather more

information), the products must be transferred to the quarantine area (see Section 5.3.5).

– Product deemed unsuitable for use must be immediately sent to the expired and damaged

area (see Section 5.3.5).

– Records of all returned products should be maintained, and stock management tools must

be updated.

If there is any uncertainty about the quality of a medical product, it must not be

reused or redistributed. Refer to the HQ pharmacy referent for more guidance.

5.10.2 Picking

Picking refers to the process of locating, collecting and verifying specific products from storage
locations to fulfil order requests.

ag Or medical products purchased by any MSF regional hubs not compliant with MSF’s procurement policy for

medical products.
ah ‘’In organoleptic evaluation, four senses are used: taste, smell, sight, and touch. Taste assesses flavour

profiles, smell focuses on aroma, sight evaluates appearance, and touch measures texture, including factors
like crunchiness. Each sense offers unique insights into a product’s overall quality and consumer appeal.’’
Organoleptic Tests & Sensory Analysis of Food | Certified Laboratories. Certified Laboratories. Published 8
December 2023.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

119

5

Picking methods

The following picking methods must be strictly followed:

– First Expired, First Out (FEFO) method: products with the earliest expiry dates are picked

first. This method is used for perishable products.

– First In, First Out (FIFO) method: products are picked in the order they were received,

ensuring older stock is used first. This method is suitable for non-perishable products.

Potential risks

Picking plays a crucial role in ensuring timely, accurate, and safe distribution of medical
products to patients. However, several risks should be mitigated during picking to safeguard
patient safety.

– Cross-contamination involves the unintended transfer of chemical or microbiological

contaminants that can compromise the quality of a medical product during storage,
handling, or transportation.

– Mix-up involves errors during product picking, such as collecting the wrong product. These

errors may occur due to:

• Products being stored too close together

• Similar-looking labels

• Poorly organised storage areas

• Insufficient pharmaceutical knowledge

Sound Alike Look Alike Drugs (SALADs)

SALADs refer to drugs with names or packaging that look or sound similar,


increasing the risk of confusion and medication errors. Staff awareness and

training, or proper product labelling, are essential to mitigate these risks and ensure

patient safety.

Certain SALADs products (e.g., water for injection and potassium chloride injectable,
morphine sulphate capsules, and morphine hydrochloride ampules) may pose a
considerable risk to patient safety. These require meticulous management across the
supply chain to minimise potential errors. To mitigate potential risks, MSF projects may

adopt supplementary measures (e.g., additional identification procedures or vigilance).

Mitigation measures

– Implement high hygiene standards (see Section 5.2.4).

– Establish continuous monitoring and process improvement (e.g., self-inspection)

– Ensure that staff members are adequately trained, with regular performance reviews and

SALADs awareness incorporated into training programs.

– Physically segregate SALAD products to minimise the risk of mix-ups.

– Use the picking ticket listai.

ai A document or digital record to guide staff during the picking process. It typically includes information such as

the products to be picked, required quantities, expiry dates, and batch numbers (if applicable).

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

120

5.10.3 Packing, repackaging, deconditioning and relabelling

Packaging for medical products serves critical functions, including protecting products from
damage, contamination, and environmental factors, ensuring regulatory compliance, and
providing critical information.

Key definitions

– Packing: preparing medical products for shipment, such as placing them into containers or

boxes.

– Deconditioning: removing a medical product from its original packaging.

– Repackaging: unpacking medical products from their original packaging (deconditioning)

and repacking them into new or different packaging.

– Relabelling: updating or rewriting the product’s information on its packageaj.

– Primary packaging: packaging that directly encloses the medical product, in direct contact

with it.

– Secondary packaging: packaging used to group and protect multiple units of the primary

packaged product.

– Tertiary packaging: outermost packaging used for bulk shipping and distribution of

products.

Note

Deconditioning, repackaging, and relabelling primary packaging are pharmaceutical
activities that can pose risks to patients if not conducted adequately.
• The handling of primary packaging is restricted to distribution pharmacies, hospital
pharmacies, or end-user units.

• In medical warehouses, handling is restricted to tertiary and secondary packaging,
unless it is supervised by medical staff.

• The handling of primary packaging is permitted only within MSF programs and is
prohibited for donations and loans to non-MSF recipients.

General considerations

– High hygiene standards must be maintained by staff members, particularly when handling

primary packaging. (see Section 5.2.4).

– Deconditioning, repackaging, and relabelling should be conducted in the order preparation

area (see Section 5.3.5).

– Storage requirements for products (e.g., protection from sunlight) must be maintained

during repacking or deconditioning.

– Repacked products must be relabelled to include the drug’s name (INNak), strength, form,

batch number, expiry date, and quantity repacked.

aj In most countries, relabelling is legally restricted or forbidden. Relabelling the secondary packaging is not

allowed; only patient-specific information (e.g., patient name, posology) may be added during dispensation.
However, regulations may vary, and some contexts may lack specific legal frameworks on this practice.
ak International non-proprietary name.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

121

5

– Relabelling must be legible, durable (e.g., waterproof labels), and guarantee identification

and traceability.

– Whenever possible, end-user units should receive products in their original packaging;

however, it may be necessary to divide large quantities in some cases.

– Each parcel should be clearly identified and match its respective packing list.

Packing cold chain products

– Proper packing of cold chain products requires trained staff.

– Only MSF-standard equipment (e.g., cool boxes or vaccine carriers) must be used.

– The passive equipment’s autonomy must exceed delivery time to accommodate any

potential delivery delays.

– Temperature monitoring devices must be incorporated to verify conditions during delivery.

Freezing indicators must be used for products sensitive to freezing temperatures.

Passive cold chainal preparation

– To prevent products from freezing and to ensure that ice packs stabilise at around 0 °C,

the frozen icepacks must be prepared in advance (conditioning process). This process is
complete when the ice packs contain a mixture of water and ice inside. The time required
for conditioning depends on external conditions.

– Conditioning ice packs in water or under direct sunlight should be avoided. Before placing

them in the passive cold chain equipment, ice packs should be wiped and dried.

– To prevent direct contact between ice packs and products, it is advisable to place a layer

of cardboard between them. Products can also be placed in plastic bags to prevent
condensation, which could potentially damage packaging and labels.

The choice of passive cold equipment, such as isothermal boxes, cold boxes, and vaccine
carriers, is determined by several factors:

– Type of activity: whether it is required for transportation only or transportation and

temporary storage (e.g., vaccination campaign).

– Temperature stability required: ability to maintain the required temperature range based

on the specific activity.

– Expected transport duration and autonomy required: duration for which the cold chain

must function independently.

– Transport method: suitability for different means of transport, such as boats, refrigerated

vehicles, or non-refrigerated vehicles.

– External conditions: exposure to ambient temperature and humidity levels.

– Volume required.

Cold Chain Management Guideline

For additional details on passive cold chain preparation and best practices, refer to
the MSF Cold Chain Management Guideline to ensure compliance with the latest MSF

standards (see Toolbox).

al Passive cold chain refers to containers or packaging solutions that do not require external power sources or

active cooling mechanisms to maintain the required temperature range for transporting temperature-sensitive
products.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

122

5.10.4 Delivery

Delivery typically involves moving products between storage and distribution units, as well as
moving products to a third partyam (MSF or external).

General considerations

– A calendar should be established to organise the delivery activities within the project,

ensuring that deliveries do not overlap and there are no conflicts during the preparation of
the deliveries.

– Delivery requires signatures from the requester, the approver and the supplier (typically a

staff member from the medical warehouse or distribution pharmacy).

– The delivery form must be signed and dated. The number of packages must be counted

and noted down. See examples in Appendix 16, Normal order and Appendix 17, Extra
order.

– At least one copy of the delivery form should be provided so that both the requester and

the sender have proof of delivery.

– Stock management tools, whether electronic or paper-based, must be meticulously

updated after deliveries.

Outgoing donations and loans

Outgoing donations refer to the act of giving medical products to a third party without expecting
anything in return. Outgoing loans involve providing medical products to a third party under
a contractual agreement (e.g., a loan certificate), with the products expected to be returned
after a specified period.

General considerations

– MSF does not accept financial reimbursement or repayments for loans.

– Outgoing donations are preferable to outgoing loans due to the greater complexity of

loans, which require follow-up for reimbursement, verification, and validationan of the
quality of the reimbursed products.

It is not recommended to make outgoing loans to external third parties. Outgoing

loans should be considered only within MSF operations, and only when there is a risk
of stockout.

– Sufficient time and careful planning are required to organise outgoing donations and loans.

This includes engaging in discussions with the recipient to ensure the suitability of the
productsao.

– The recipient should be able to use the products before they expire.

– The recipient must have trained staff available to handle and administer the donated or

loaned products safely and appropriately. If medical equipment is donated, the recipient
should also have the capacity to maintain it properly.

am A third party refers to either an external actor to MSF, such as the Ministry of Health (MoH), other NGOs or

other types of partners, or an internal actor within MSF, such as other projects or MSF programs from the
same MSF section or a different one.
an By an HQ pharmacy referent.
ao The medical products, including their formulation and strength, must be pertinent and beneficial for the

recipient; thus, they should be included in the recipient's protocol.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

123

5

– Recipients should agree to provide the MSF medical products to patients free of charge.

– Ideally, recipients should have a traceability system in place to allow MSF to notify them of

any potential quality issues with the donated or loaned products.

– Outgoing donations and loans should preferably be executed within the same country to

minimise regulatory complexities related to import and export requirements.

– When evaluating donations of medical equipment, the products should be categorised as

functional, requiring repair, designated for disposal, or not belonging to MSF. Damaged or
excessively worn equipment must be repaired or disposed of before donation.

It is important to consider the availability of spare parts and consumables within the

recipient’s country to ensure the equipment remains functional after donationap.

Product’s requirements

– The packaging and labelling of products must be in good condition and, ideally, in the

recipient country's lingua francaaq.

– Deconditioning, repackaging, and relabelling of primary packaging for medical products

are prohibited in outgoing donations and loans when the recipient is not MSF.

– Products must have a minimum remaining shelf life of six months, or longer if required

by national regulations. Under exceptional circumstances, a shorter shelf life may be
authorised, provided the product is confirmed to be consumed before expiry.

Other administrative considerations

– The recipient must sign a donation or loan agreement using the standard MSF template.

See Appendix 18 and Appendix 19.

– The agreement must include a disclaimer stating that MSF bears no further liability

regarding the quality and appropriate use of the donated or loaned products.

Further information about donations and loans

Medicines (see Toolbox).

– For the reception of incoming donations and loans, see Section 5.10.1.

– For different types of donations and loans related to stock movements, see Section

5.15.2.

their own supply.

5.10.5 Physical counting and physical inventory

Physical counting refers to the process of manually counting medical products in stock to
verify actual stockar levels. It involves recording specific product information such as expiry
dates and/or batch numbers. Physical count is part of the broader physical inventory process.

Physical inventory refers to the comprehensive process of manually counting and verifying all
products in stock to ensure that the counted stock (actual stock) matches the recorded data in
stock management tools (theoretical stockas).

The importance of physical inventory

Physical inventory is a cornerstone of effective stock management, contributing to the
following:

– Ensures accuracy of theoretical stock, identifying discrepanciesat and reconciling it with

physical stockau. This is crucial for other critical processes, such as ordering or budgeting.

– Enhances operational efficiency, mitigating risks associated with discrepancies, such as

fraud, risk of stockouts, or overstocks, for example.

– Support accurate financial reporting.

– Promotes adherence to GSDP requirements by identifying potential stock issues (e.g.,

damaged or expired products) and ensuring proper stock organisation before counting.

The importance of an accurate theoretical stock

The quality of data directly impacts the effectiveness and reliability of important processes
such as ordering, calculation, and budgeting estimation. Using incorrect data can lead to
errors, potentially resulting in stockouts or overstocks.

To mitigate such risks, a full physical inventory of all medical products must be completed
before calculating replenishment orders for the main stock (see Chapter 2).

ar Actual stock is the physical quantity that is present at a given time. It is the number of units of a product that

are physically counted or verified during a physical count.
as Theoretical stock is the number of units recorded in a stock management tool. It represents the expected stock

level based on previous physical counts and stock movements.
at A situation where the amount, expiry date or batch number of physical stocks of a specific product differs from

its current theoretical stock.
au A situation where the amount, expiry date or batch number of physical stocks of a specific product differs from

its current theoretical stock.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

125

5

Types of physical inventories

– Full physical inventory

• All products in stock are counted, which can require significant time and resources.

• Robust planning is necessary to avoid stock movements during the count. Dates
for the full physical count must be communicated in advance to all relevant health
services.

• This inventory is conducted at agreed-upon intervals set by each MSF section (e.g.,
prior to placing orders, every four months, annually, etc.).

– Partial physical inventory

• Conducted between two full physical counts, this type focuses on specific subsets of
products (e.g., oral drugs, high-consumption products, cold chain products, etc.).

• It can be scheduled regularly (e.g., monthly) or conducted on an ad hoc basis.

Conducting physical inventory

– Planning

• Determine the type, scope, and schedule of the physical inventory. When conducting full
physical inventories, stock movements (both incoming and outgoing movements) must
be avoided, and health services must be notified in advance.

• Only authorised staff members can conduct physical counting.

• Staff members must be trained according to the existing SOPs or work instructions.

• The physical count should involve at least two people, ideally including one person who
is not part of the medical warehouse or distribution pharmacy.

• For large stocks, forming multiple teams should be considered. Each counting team
should be assigned to specific areas or types of products.

• For specialised products, such as surgical instruments or medical equipment that may be
challenging to differentiate, technical experts can be engaged for assistance.

– Preparation

• The storage area must be clean and well-organised to facilitate accurate counting.

• All products must be properly labelled and accessible.

• All stock cards, whether electronic or paper-based, as used in the MSF section, must be
updated.

• Counting sheets must be printed for recording quantities, batch numbers, and expiry
dates.

– Physical counting

• The physical order of products must be respected.

• Expired and damaged products identified during the count must be removed from stock
and segregated immediately.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

126

– Reconciliation and adjustment

• Counted quantities should be compared with those recorded in the stock management
tools, including paper stock cards if used.

• If discrepancies are identified, the products should be recounted. If the second count
differs from the first, it is recommended to have a second team conduct a third recount.

• Confirmed discrepancies between theoretical stock and physical stock must be
documented and investigated.

• If no explanation for a discrepancy is found, it should be noted accordingly.

• A discrepancy report should be written following the internal guidelines of each MSF
section.

• Stock management tools must be updated to accurately reflect the actual stock.

For more details on indicators related to physical inventory performance, see Section 5.15.4.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

127

5

5.11 Handling specific medical products

The management of certain medical products requires additional considerations beyond
the general requirements or recommendations. This section highlights specific measures to
ensure the safe and compliant handling of these products while remaining consistent with the
standards outlined in this chapter.

5.11.1 Controlled drugs

The management of controlled drugs is governed by strict adherence to national regulations
and MSF standards, with a focus on restricting access while ensuring complete traceability and
exhaustive documentation.

The storage of controlled drugs must always be guided by clear procedures to ensure compliance
with legal and safety standards. These procedures typically cover roles and responsibilities,
how to use the registration book, security measures to implement, etc.

The classification and regulation of controlled drugs (including precursorsav) can vary

significantly from one country to another.

Specific considerations

– For requirements regarding the storage area for controlled drugs, see Section 5.3.5.

– In addition to updating stock management tools, a controlled drug register must be

maintained to monitor the handling and usage of these substances. This register must be
updated promptly and accurately (see Appendix 20).

– The packaging for controlled drugs must be double-checked to ensure that it is securely

sealed. This measure will make any tampering with the consignment evident.

– The packaging box must be clearly labelled indicating that it contains controlled drugs.

– Expired controlled drugs must be segregated in the damaged and expired area (see Section

5.3.5) and labelled as ‘’Expired controlled drugs for destruction’’.

5.11.2 Medical equipment

The storage and distribution of medical equipment are shared responsibilities among the
medical, supply, and logistics departments. In MSF programs or projects with dedicated
biomedical positions, oversight of medical equipment is assigned to those positions. In their
absence, a logistics focal person should assume this responsibility.

Therefore, it is crucial that responsibilities are clearly delineated and thoroughly understood
by all teams involved. The allocation of responsibilities may vary significantly across different
MSF sections. For further guidance, refer to the biomedical and pharmacy referent at HQ.

av Precursors are chemicals frequently used in the illicit manufacture of narcotic drugs and psychotropic

substances and are under international control in accordance with the United Nations Convention against
Illicit Traffic in Narcotic Drugs and Psychotropic Substances of 1988.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

128

Key considerations

– When equipment is delivered to an end-user unit, the biomedical focal point must be

notifiedaw to update the maintenance plan.

– Preventive and corrective maintenance are essential to ensure proper functionality and

avoid losses.

– A summary of the medical equipment in use within a project, including their condition,

location, and any backup equipment, must always be accessible to the medical, supply,
and logistic teams.

For more information, refer to the MSF Medical Equipment Management Guideline (see
Toolbox).

Expected lifespan of medical equipment

The expected lifespan of medical equipment refers to the period during which a device is
expected to function reliably from the time it is first used. Beyond this period, the equipment
may begin to experience more frequent or even irreparable failures.

Exceeding the equipment's expected lifespan does not necessarily mean immediate
decommissioning. Instead, the equipment's status should be evaluated, and replacement
strategies considered.

The expected lifespan may vary based on various factors:

– Context of use (e.g., location, storage conditions, frequency of use)

– Types of activities performed

– User proficiency and training level

– Frequency of preventive maintenance

The expected lifespan is often specified in the equipment’s user manual or the manufacturer's
technical specifications sheet. For more information, refer to the biomedical referent at HQ.

Decommissioning medical equipment

The decision to decommission medical equipment should be made collaboratively by both the
medical and logistics teams. Although the expected lifespan is a valuable parameter that can
assist in decision-making, it should not be the sole factor considered.

Additional considerations include, but are not limited to:

– A technical evaluation by a biomedical expert.

– The importance of the equipment to MSF medical activities, including any contingency

plans.

– Availability of backup equipment.

– Standardisation of current equipment (whether in use or available in stock). Using multiple

models of the same equipment should be avoided, as it requires additional training and
complicates management.

– Budget implications.

aw The input from the biomedical and laboratory departments should be also incorporated into the forecasting

process, see more information in Chapter 2.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

129

5

5.11.3 Expired and damaged products

Medical products can be damaged due to a variety of factorsax. Understanding these factors
enables improvements in operational processes and helps implement corrective actions to
avoid recurrence.

Specific considerations

– Regarding requirements of the expired and damaged area, see Section 5.3.5.

– Expired and damaged products must be immediately placed in the damaged and expired

area.

– Traceability must be ensured by updating stock management tools (electronic and paper-

based). A register documenting all products transferred to the damaged and expired area
is required (see Appendix 21).

– MSF must comply with the national legal framework, which may impose restrictions on the

management and disposal of expired and damaged products.

Use of expired products

Medical products are considered safe for use up to the expiry date specified by the EXP
or equivalent symbol on their packaging. The manufacturing date (MFD)
should not
be confused with the expiry date (EXP)
.

The use of expired products may harm MSF's reputation with patients, staff,


and authorities, and pose legal risks. It is NOT RECOMMENDED to use expired

medical products of any kind in all countries of intervention.

ax Some of the most common causes of damage include improper storage conditions, physical damage,

contamination, improper handling and neglected maintenance.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

130

5.12 Management of prepositioned stocks

Prepositioned stocks are strategically stored in designated locations to anticipate demand
or potential emergencies. This proactive approach ensures that critical supplies are readily
available when and where they are needed, thereby reducing response times and enhancing
supply chain resilience.

Products in prepositioned stock are reserved for specific scenarios or emergencies. They
are generally organised into kits or modulesay for rapid deployment, requiring meticulous
management to keep them ready for use. For more information regarding kits and their
composition, refer to the MSF catalogues.

The medical team is responsible for overseeing the prepositioned stock strategy. This strategy
aims to ensure readiness for timely and efficient responses to specific scenarios.

Note

• The content of the prepositioned stocks must always be inventoried and prepared for
immediate use.

• The medical team must have a comprehensive visibility of the status of the
prepositioned stocks.

Management of prepositioned stocks

– Full physical inventories should be conducted at least every six months or when

undertaking the full physical inventory of medical stocks.

– Prepositioned stocks should be monitored using stock management tools, just like any

other medical stock. Stock movements must be recorded to ensure comprehensive
traceability.

– If the stock management tool does not facilitate the management of the full content of

products within kits or modules:

• The expiry date recorded for the kits or modules should correspond to the earliest
expiry date among all the products within the kit or module.

• A parallel system (e.g., Excel sheet) should be established to ensure adequate
management of the products, ensuring visibility of the products, product quantities, and
expiry dates.

Replenishment of prepositioned stocks

– If a pre-positioned stock is incomplete, individual products from the main stock can be

utilised to fill the gaps. However, it is essential to verify that this will not result in future
stockouts.

– When a pre-positioned stock must no longer be maintained, the individual products

should be integrated into the project stock. Donations should be considered for products
that cannot be utilised for the project.

ay A module is a set of products that is either part of a kit or the optional complement to a kit.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

131

5

Rotation of perishable products

– Often, a product is available in both pre-positioned stock and main stock. In such cases, it

can be exchanged between the stocks to prevent expiration.

– Ideally, products should be rotated with at least six to seven months remaining before

their expiry date.

– If stock rotation is not possible, donation should be considered as an alternative. Any

replacement must be received no later than seven to nine months before the expiry date
to allow adequate time to organise potential donations, ensuring a minimum remaining
shelf life of six months. However, national regulations may stipulate a longer period.

Specific storage requirements for kits
Kits must be stored in a distinct, designated, and readily accessible location. The storage
requirements outlined in Section 5.4 must be applied to kits.

Additionally:

prominently displayed, ideally in a plastic
pouch as indicated in Figure 5.3. The

be numbered sequentially (e.g., cartons
1/3, 2/3, 3/3).

Source: MSF

Utilisation of prepositioned stock during stockouts

• In case of stockout, it is strongly advised not to utilise products from the prepositioned
stock to fulfil the gaps.

• The prepositioned stock must always be complete, up to date, and ready for any
emergency response. The medical team determines its utilisation and, if necessary,

specifies which kit or module should be employed.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

132

5.13 Stock management tools for medical products

5.13.1 Medical standard list (MSL)

See Chapter 1, Selection.

5.13.2 Physical counting and physical inventory

See Section 5.10.5, Physical counting and physical inventory.

5.13.3 Paper stock cards

A paper stock card is a physical stock management tool used to track stock levels and movements
of individual products. It is typically a card or sheet of paper where essential information about
product stock movements, including physical counts, is recorded manually.

The use of physical stock cards varies across the different MSF sections. For more

information about stock cards, please refer to HQ.

How to use a paper stock card

– The stock card must include the MSF code and description of the product to differentiate it

from other products clearly.

– Each stock card must correspond to a single product, and the nearest expiry date should

be recorded in the remark’s column. However, some MSF sections may choose to use a
separate stock card for each batch or expiry date; in this case, the expiry date should be
recorded on the stock card’s header.

– The stock card should be kept next to the stock.

– Stock cards can be placed next to the respective product or organised collectively in a

designated box or binder, sorted by product family (see MSF catalogues).

– Each stock movement must be recorded on a separate line using an indelible pen, even if

several stock movements occur within the same day.

– Order reference must be recorded in the origin or destination column.

– When conducting a physical count, the counted quantity should be written in another

colour pen (usually in red).

– Out movements for damaged or expired products must be recorded like any other stock

movement.

– Once the stock card is completed, the last line should be copied to a new card.

– Stock cards must be archived, with the retention duration determined by each MSF

section.

See Appendix 22 for an example of a paper stock card.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

133

5

5.14 Waste management

The management of medical product waste, particularly pharmaceuticals and hazardous
materials, requires strict adherence to regulatory compliance, environmental protection
standards, and health and safety protocols. Proper waste management safeguards both human
health and the environment.

Oversight of waste management must be conducted by an environmental health or water
and sanitation specialist. Efficient waste management requires interdisciplinary collaboration
among the medical, supply, and logistics departments.

For detailed guidance on waste management practices and destruction methods, refer to the
MSF Health-Related Hazardous Waste Management within LMIC guide (see Toolbox).

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

134

5.15 Reporting: performance measurement

Reporting refers to the systematic collection, analysis, and dissemination of information related
to stock management. It can also be considered a stock management tool. However, due to its
complexity, it is addressed in a dedicated section.

While each MSF section produces different reports, the fundamental objective of reporting
remains consistent: to facilitate informed decision-making, enhance operational efficiency,
mitigate risks, and improve overall performance.

5.15.1 Consumption and real consumption

– Consumption refers to the demand and utilisation patterns of medical products. This

information is used in important processes such as forecasting, budgeting and ordering.
Consumption data is used to calculate a fundamental metric, such as the average monthly
consumption (AMC).

• Not every outgoing movement necessarily corresponds to consumption. Additional
information is available below (see stock movements).

• Consumption data must always be greater than or equal to zero (it cannot be a negative
number).

• In cases where the distribution flow of a project is complex (involving multiple storage
and distribution units), it is necessary to ensure that the AMC is not recorded twice.

• See more information about AMC in Section 5.15.3.

– Real consumption refers to the quantity of a medical product that is used for or given

to patients. (e.g., when a hospital pharmacy dispenses medications directly to patients
or when medical products are utilised in end-user units). Real consumption is always
measured at the end-user unit level.

5.15.2 Stock movements

The movement of stock can be recorded in two ways: as an outgoing movement (OUT) when
products are shipped out of stock, or as an incoming movement (IN) when products are
received into stock.

There are two main types of stock movement within a project:
Transfer

– It typically involves moving products from one stock area to another within the same stock

location (e.g., medical warehouse, distribution pharmacy).

– This movement is never considered consumption and, therefore, is not included in the

calculation of the AMC.

– Transfer movements must be greater than or equal to zero and cannot be a negative

number.

– Examples of transfers may include, but are not limited to, movements of stock to a

quarantine areaaz, expired and damaged areasba, prepositioned stocks, etc.

az Due to a quality complaint, quality alert, batch recall or cold-chain breakdown.
ba Transfers to this area are always considered a loss, and the reason must be specified. For example, product

damage may result from a cold chain breakdown, a batch recall, sampling, etc.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

135

5

Delivery

– It typically involves moving products between storage and distribution units, as well as

moving products to a third partybb (MSF or external entities).

– Delivery movements must be greater than or equal to zero and cannot be a negative

number.

– Depending on the delivery type, it can be classified as either a consumption or non-

consumption movement.

Some examples of deliveries include, but are not limited to:

– Consumption delivery: typically directed towards hospital pharmacies or end-user units.

– Donations: a donation refers to the act of giving medical products to a third party without

expecting anything in return. Depending on the donation type, the AMC calculation could
be affected. Additional information is available below (See types of donations).

– Loans: a loan involves providing medical products to a third party (OUT from the lender’s

perspective) under a contractual agreement (e.g., loan certificate) where the products are
expected to be returned (IN from the lender’s perspective) after a specified period. Loans
must not be considered when calculating the AMC.

– Borrowings: this is the practice of temporarily lending products from a third party (IN

from the borrower’s perspective), with the expectation that the products will be returned
(OUT from the borrower’s perspective) after a specific period. Borrowings must not be
considered when calculating the AMC.

– Return of products: it is often considered a consumption movement when it originates

from end-user units or from third parties who have received a regular, planned donation. If
categorised as a consumption movement, the quantity should be deducted from the AMC
calculation, provided that the AMC does not fall below zero.

Types of donations

– Regular planned donations: these are pre-arranged donations that occur at fixed intervals.

• If the donation is received from a third party, it is recorded as an “IN” movement.

• If the donation is made to a third party, it is recorded as an “OUT” movement. OUT
movements must be considered when calculating the AMC.

– Unplanned donations: these are one-time donations that were not originally anticipated

and are associated with issues in demand planning.

• For an IN movement, the objective is usually to prevent stockouts.

• For an OUT movement, the objective is to prevent waste or reduce overstock or sleeping
stock. OUT movements must not be considered when calculating the AMC.

– Irregular donations: these are one-time donations that were not originally anticipated but

are not associated with any issue in demand planning.

• In the case of an IN movement, the objective is generally to receive an unsolicited
donation from a third party (e.g., during the closure of one project, another project may
receive several donations).

• In the case of an OUT movement, the objective is typically to assist a third party in need
(such as responding to an occasional request from the MoH). OUT movements must not
be considered when calculating the AMC.

bb A third party refers to either an external actor to MSF, such as the Ministry of Health (MoH), other NGOs or

other types of partners, or an internal actor within MSF, such as other projects or programs from the same
MSF section or a different one.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

136

In-kind donations

Donations managed by MSF programs should not be mistaken for in-kind donations,
which are overseen by MSF Operational Directorates, which bear the primary
responsibility and accountability for handling this type of donation.

In-kind donations refer to the provision, free of charge or below production cost, of
medical products and health technologies by a commercial entity or its affiliated non-
profit organisations.

Refer to the MSF Policy for In-Kind Donations of Medical Products and Health Technology

for more information.

5.15.3 Metrics

While metrics and indicators are related, they serve slightly different purposes and provide
distinct types of information. Metrics are numerical values used to describe a context or
situation. They provide the foundational data for measuring performance. Metrics are used to
calculate indicators.

AMC

Name: average monthly consumption
Definition: a metric that calculates the average quantity of a product
consumedbc over a specific period, typically measured in months.

Formula

Sum of monthly consumptions for a specific period

Number of months in the period

Observations

• This metric provides insights into past consumption patterns and
trends, which are crucial for conducting stock analysis and informing
decision-making processes.

• To calculate the AMC, use at least three months of consumption data
if project activities fluctuate. For projects with stable activities, use a
longer time period.

• To evaluate the impact of the period chosen, calculate the AMC using
different periods (e.g., 3-month and 12-month or longer periods) and
then compare the results.

• The AMC must always be greater than or equal to zero.

• Stockouts significantly impact the AMC by reducing the consumption
of unavailable products and increasing the consumption of
substitutes.

bc Consumption refers to the demand and utilisation patterns of medical products. For more information, see

Section 5.15.1.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

137

5

AAMC

Name: adjusted average monthly consumption
Definition: AAMC is a refined version of the AMC that excludes potential
anomalies, such as stockouts, encoding errors and other significant
situations in consumption patterns (e.g., higher consumption of a product
used as a substitute when another product is in stockout).

Formula

Sum of monthly consumptions for a specific

period (excluding months with anomalies)
Number of months in the period (excluding months with anomalies)

Observations

• This metric improves accuracy in calculating past regular consumption
by removing data outliers.

• AAMC must always be greater than or equal to zero.

FMC

Name: forecasted monthly consumption
Definition: FMC is the estimated quantity of a product that is expected to
be consumed during an upcoming period. It is a predictive metric based
on historical consumption data, trends, and anticipated changes in demand
(medical activities).

Formula

Sum of forescated monthly consumption for a specific period

Number of months in the period

Observations

• FMC must always be greater than or equal to zero.

• For details on the forecasting process, see Chapter 2

Forecast
accuracy

Formula

AMC of a period (AMCT1) − AMC of previous period (AMCPrev T1)X 100
AMC of a period (AMCPrevT1)

Observations

• This metric facilitates the evaluation of sudden or significant
changes in the consumption of a specific product, allowing for early
intervention if consumption patterns are significantly deviating from
expectation (e.g., due to deviations from MSF protocols, malpractices
or other issues), reducing the risk of stockouts or overstock.

• An alternative formula that leads to the same result could be as
follows: [(AMCT1/AMCPrevT1) − 1] x 100

Possible variation

• FMC deviation: to evaluate whether the FMC estimated in the past
matches the current AMC (or AAMC), the formula can be adjusted as
follows:

AMC of current period (AMCT1) - FMC estimated for the current period (FMCT1)

FMC estimated for the current period (FMCT1)
X 100

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

138

Expiry before
consumption

Formula
Quantity in stock with a specific expiry date − (AMC X number of months

until expiry)

Observations

• This measures the quantity of a product that reaches its expiry date
before being consumed.

• AMC in the numerator can be replaced with average FMC or AAMC if
more suitable.

Months of

stock

Definition: the number of months required to deplete the entire stock using
AMC, and subtracting the quantity that will expire before consumption.

Formula

Quantity in stock − Expiry before consumption

AMC

Observations

• AMC in the denominator can be replaced by AAMC or average FMC if
more suitable.

• It is used to assess the risk of both stockouts and overstocks of a
product.

Product
at risk of
stockout

Definition: a stockout is a situation in which the stock level for a particular
product within the MSL is depleted, making it impossible to fulfil operational
demand for that product.

A product at risk of stockout indicates that the current stock of a specific
product within the MSL may be insufficient to cover the estimated demand
until the arrival of the next order. When the months of stock for a specific
product are less than the remaining lead time and security stock (expressed
in months), the product is considered at risk of a stockout.

Products can be categorised in risk of stockouts using the following rationale:

Quantity in stock − Expiry before consumption < number of months until

arrival of next order

AMC

Observations

• AMC in the denominator can be replaced by average FMC or AAMC if
more suitable.

• Identifying and addressing products at risk of stockout facilitates
timely decision making when necessary (e.g., placing orders,
requesting donations or loans) to ensure the uninterrupted supply of
medical products.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

139

5

Overstock

Definition: a situation where the quantity of products in stock significantly
exceeds the expected consumption within a defined period, resulting in an
excessive amount of stock.

Observations

• Overstock is normally calculated based on 12 months but can be
adjusted according to the context.

• AMC can be replaced by average FMC or AAMC if more suitable.

Formulas

• For consumable products: Quantity in stock − (AMC x 12)

• For non-consumable products: Quantity in stock − predefined
maximum stock level.

Sleeping

stock

Definition: consumable products in stock with no consumption over the
last 12 months (excluding products recently entered in stock and products
within prepositioned stocks such as EPREP).

Observations

• Sleeping stock does not adhere to a specific formula. Rather, it is a
categorisation of products that meet the following three conditions:

▹A consumable product

▹AMC12 months = 0

▹Quantity in stock > 0
• The categorisation of products in sleeping stock does not apply to
projects initiated within the last 12 months or products contained
within prepositioned stocks (e.g., E-PREP).

Number of
products in
MSL without

any data

• Products must meet the following 4 conditions:

▹Products belonging to MSL

▹Quantity in stock = 0

▹AMC12 months = 0

▹Pipeline for the last 12 months = 0

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

5.15.4 Indicators

Indicators are specific metrics that provide insights into aspects of performance. They indicate
how well certain processes or activities are performing and often point to trends or patterns.

KPIsbd are defined by each MSF section and are not within the scope of this guideline.

% Products in

stockout

Formula

Number of products in stockout (in the MSL) X 100

Number of products in the MSL

Observations

• Stockout refers to a situation in which stock levels for a particular
product within the MSL are depleted, making it impossible to fulfil
the operational demands for that product.

• This indicator helps to identify possible recurring issues in the
forecasting process or potential issues within the supply chain.

Possible variations

• The numerator can be replaced with the number of vitalbe and/
or essentialbf products in stockout products only. Stockout of vital
products may require quick action, such as placing an emergency
order, seeking donations or loans, or requesting a local purchase.

• The numerator can be replaced with the number of products at risk
of stockout (see Metrics section).

% Products in

MSL without

data

Formula

Number of products in the MSL without any data X 100
Number of products in the MSL

Observations
This indicator helps determine if there is a need to revise or remove
products from the MSL.

bd KPIs are a subset of indicators that are considered critical. KPIs measure performance based on established

strategic goal or objectives.
be Vital product: critical for life-saving interventions, with no suitable substitutes (e.g., anaesthesia products,

adrenaline, etc.)
bf Essential product: critical for the continuation of core medical activities. a short-term stockout would not halt

activities.

Chapter 5: Good storage and distribution practices of medical products and medical stock management

5

Stock rotation

Formula

Value of consumed products during a specific period

Average stock value during the specific period

Observations

• This indicator measures how frequently the inventory is consumed
and replaced over a specific period. This metric helps assess the
efficiency of inventory management and indicates how well stock
aligns with demand.

• The indicator should be calculated exclusively for consumable
products.

• The specified period is typically one year; however, it may be
adjusted as necessary.

% Value of
total lossesbg

Formula

Value of total losses in specific period
Value of consumed products in the same period X 100

Observations

• This indicator shows the financial impact of stock management
inefficiencies; it helps evaluate the effectiveness of the demand
planning and order fulfilment process.

• The indicator can be broken down into the various causes of losses
(product damage, product expiration, unplanned donations, theft or
products being declared lost) to provide deeper insights and identify
specific areas for improvement.

• The value must be estimated in euros.

Possible variation

• The denominator can be replaced by the total value of products in
stock to provide a broader view. However, the indicator can be more
significantly influenced in situations of substantial overstock.

products in

Formula

Value of products in stock

Observations

• This indicator shows how much stock remains unconsumed for an
extended period.

Possible variations

• This indicator may be measuring the volume or number of products
instead of their value.

bg Losses: it refers to any unintentional or undesirable reduction in the quantity of product in stock. Losses need

to be identified and addressed to maintain accurate stock levels. They can result from product damage (which
may be caused by cold chain breakdowns), product expiration, unplanned donations, theft, or products being
declared lost. Understanding the cause of the loss is crucial for accurate reporting.
