6.1 Introduction

End-user units represent the final storage point for medical products before they are dispensed
or used. Examples of end-user units include hospital units such as maternity or paediatric
wards, emergency rooms, operating theatres, inpatient therapeutic feeding centres (ITFCs),
outpatient dispensaries, mobile clinics, etc.

The objective of this chapter is to provide general recommendations for managing medical
stocks at end-user units. For more details on dispensing or the rational use of medical products,
see Chapter 7 and Chapter 8.

Chapter 6: Stock management at end-user units

148

6.2 Staff members

The medical team is fully responsible for managing medical products in end-user units. Often,
multiple team members may have access to the medical stock. Responsibilities must be clearly
defined and fully understood by the team.

– A designated individual within an end-user unit must always be identified as the

responsible person for the overall management of the medical stock. For instance, in a
hospital setting, this responsibility typically falls to the unit's supervisor.

– In the absence of the responsible person (e.g., during a night shift), another individual

should be designated to oversee the medical stock.

– The project pharmacy manager is responsible for providing support to various end-user

units within the project to ensure effective stock management.

– Staff members within the units must be trained in the proper handling of specific medical

products (e.g., cold chain products, controlled drugs, antineoplastic, etc.) as required.

– Staff members within the units should be familiar with:

• Good dispensing practices (see more information in Chapter 7).

• The established reporting system for quality issues concerning medical products (see
Chapter 9).

• Routine procedures for pharmacovigilance reporting (see Chapter 8).

• Established waste management procedures (see Section 6.8).

Chapter 6: Stock management at end-user units

149

6

6.3 Storage at end-user units

– The storage area (cupboard, room, metallic box, etc.) must remain locked and be assigned

to the responsible person.

– The storage area must be kept clean, dry, and secured against sources of contamination or

pests. Stored products must be protected against direct sunlight and dust.

– To facilitate picking and prevent mix-ups, products must be identified by labels and sorted

preferably in alphabetical order by product family.

– Perishable products must be arranged by expiry date, to facilitate the use of the first

expired, first out (FEFO) method. Nonperishable products should be arranged by reception
date to facilitate the first-in, first-out (FIFO) method.

– Adequate lighting should be ensured to avoid picking errors and facilitate tasks like

physical counting.

– Whenever possible, storage areas should be located near the points where medical

products are administered or used.

Chapter 6: Stock management at end-user units

150

6.4 Handling specific medical products

6.4.1 Controlled drugs

– Controlled drugs must be stored in a dedicated and separate storage area, such as a locked

cupboard, a metallic box, or a cash box with a key lock bolted to the central cupboard, etc.
that is securely locked.

– Access to this storage area must be strictly controlled, with a limited number of staff

having access.

– A registration book must be maintained to monitor the handling and use of controlled

drugs. It is essential to manage this book rigorously and update it promptly. See an
example in Appendix 20.

– Additional measures could be required by the national legislation.

6.4.2 Cold chain

– If a cold chain is employed, standard operating procedures (SOPs) must be established.

– Continuous power supply for cold chain devices must be ensured for 24 hours without

interruption.

– A calendar of maintenance should be established by the logistics team.

– Temperature must be recorded daily by the person in charge of managing the medical

stock.

– Refer to the MSF Cold Chain Management Guideline in Toolbox for information about cold

chain management and cold chain equipment.

Chapter 6: Stock management at end-user units

151

6

6.5 Physical inventory

– A physical inventory must be conducted before the placement of regular orders.

– Ideally, this count should be conducted by at least two people.

– During counting, the physical order of the products must be respected, and the expiry

dates must be recorded.

– Expired or damaged products identified during the count must be immediately removed

from stock, segregated, and returned to the distribution pharmacya or medical warehouse.

– Products that are approaching their expiry date should be returned to the distribution

pharmacy or medical warehouse, ideally with enough lead time to allow for their use by
other units. See more details in Section 6.7.

a Also including the hospital pharmacy. For more information, see Appendix 8.

Chapter 6: Stock management at end-user units

152

6.6 Replenishment of end-user units

6.6.1 General considerations

While each MSF section may exhibit slight variations in procedures and validation processes,
the guiding principles remain consistent across all sections:

– To ensure efficient replenishment of the various end-user units within the MSF project, it

is essential to establish an order chronogram that facilitates effective planning and optimal
allocation of human resources.

– The frequency of the orders established in the chronogram depends on various factors,

such as storage capacity, temperature and relative humidity conditions, type of end-user
unit, staff skills, etc.

– A standard list of medical products should be defined for each end-user unit.

Maintaining optimal temperature and humidity conditions within end-user


units may pose considerable logistical challenges. To minimise the risk of product
deterioration, it is recommended to maintain lower stock levels in the units and increase
replenishment frequency. For example, if the temperature in the unit consistently exceeds
25°C or if the humidity level is significantly high, weekly orders are preferred. In these cases,
the frequency of orders should not exceed two weeks.

6.6.2 Order request

– Order requests placed at end-user units typically require signatures from:

• The requester: the person responsible for the unit’s stock.

• The approver: normally the medical or nurse activity manager.

• The supplier: a staff member from the distribution pharmacy or medical warehouse.

– To facilitate the identification and proper filing of orders, each order request must be

assigned a unique identification code.

– Whenever possible, it is advisable to adjust the order frequency to enable the

replenishment of the entire primary packaging for medicines (e.g., tablets, capsules).
Maintaining the integrity of the primary packaging simplifies the replenishment process
and minimises the risk of contamination.

– Depending on the MSF section, order requests may be categorised using different terms.

In general, they fall into the following classifications:

• Planned or regular order: this order aligns with the order chronogram established
within the MSF project.

• Unplanned, urgent, or extra order: this order does not align with the order chronogram
established within the project. These orders should always be justified and only cover
the quantities required until the next scheduled regular order within the chronogram.

If an end-user unit frequently submits unplanned order requests, a root cause


analysis should be conducted to identify and address the underlying issues.

Chapter 6: Stock management at end-user units

153

6

6.6.3 Order reception

– During the reception of orders, the contents listed on the packing list (or a similar

document) should be verified against the received products. This process ensures that the
correct products and quantities have been delivered.

– Products must be inspected for damage, expiry dates, and any signs of tampering or

contamination. Expired products must be rejected. If any packaging is damaged, the
contents must be inspected immediately.

– The recipient (typically the responsible person for the unit’s stock) and the supplier must

both sign an acknowledgement of reception. The supplier should retain a copy of this
document.

– The packaging list (or similar document) must be correctly archived and made available for

consultation as necessary.

– Controlled drugs and cold-chain products must be stored immediately upon reception. The

controlled drugs registration book must be promptly updated.

– Stock management tools in use (e.g., follow-up of orders sheets, etc.) must be updated

after reception.

6.6.4 Real consumption recording

The real consumptionb at the end-user unit can be recorded using tools such as daily, weekly,
or monthly tally sheets. Refer to the HQ pharmacy referent for guidance on obtaining the tally
sheets.

To verify whether the real consumption is accurately recorded, compare the quantities of
selected medicines on the daily tally sheet with the amounts listed in prescriptions, hospital
cards, or consultation records for the same day.

6.6.5 Replenishment systems

Bulk stock system (cupboard system)

– The end-user unit maintains a small stock, typically stored in a cupboard, which is

sufficient to meet the unit's needs for a predetermined periodc.

– This system is widely employed across most end-user units, including hospital wards,

dispensaries, or support services such as laboratories and biomedical departments.

– A physical inventory must be conducted to ensure the accuracy of the order calculation.

See more information in Section 6.5.

b Quantity of a medical product that is used for or given to patients. (e.g., when a hospital pharmacy dispenses

medications directly to patients or when medical products are utilised in end-user units).
c As defined in the established order chronogram within the project (usually one week in hospital settings).

Chapter 6: Stock management at end-user units

154

The order calculation can be carried out using two distinct methods: the consumption-based
method and the quota method.

Consumption–based method

• The consumption-based method is recommended when a minimum level of stock
management expertise is assured or when future consumption is unpredictable.

• Order calculation is based on the previous real consumption recorded for each product,
along with a security stock.

• The formula used is determined by each MSF section, but the general approach is as
follows:

Quantity to order = Real Consumption x (LT + CP + SS) – Physical Stock

Physical stock refers to the number of units of a product that are physically counted or verified
during a physical count. In general, lead time (LT) is considered 0, as end-user units are close to
the distribution point, especially in hospital settings. Security stockd (SS) is usually set at 50%
of the coverage periode (CP).

For example, in the case of a weekly order for a hospital ward, the CP is often 1 week, the SS is
0.5 weeks, and the LT is 0 weeks.

– Quota method

• The quota method is recommended in the following situations:

▹When consumption patterns are predictable.

▹In environments with a high volume or turnover of patients, such as emergency

rooms.

▹When the frequent rotation of medical staff makes it challenging to accurately record

real consumption.

▹When staff members have limited stock management skills.
• A quota or maximum stock level is set for each product. The quantity to order is
calculated by subtracting physical stock from the defined quota.

Quantity to order = Quota – Physical Stock

• To determine the quota, various factors must be considered, including the medical
protocols in place, the expected number of patients during the coverage period, the
order frequency, etc.

• The quota should be reviewed and adjusted as necessary, particularly in response to
changes in protocol, increased activity (e.g., due to seasonality), or other relevant
factors.

• A limitation of this methodology lies in its incapacity to accurately capture prescription
behaviours.

• It is recommended to implement close monitoring, as quota systems often lead to
increased consumption. This occurs because staff may assume that the quantity
specified in the quota can be used without justification.

d Also known as safety stock. It is the quantity of stock that should be readily available to address unforeseen

circumstances, such as delivery delays or unexpected spikes in consumption. In end-user units, it is normally
expressed in weeks.
e Coverage period refers to the time period that should cover the needs between two consecutive order

receptions. In end-user units, it is normally expressed in weeks.

Chapter 6: Stock management at end-user units

155

6

Prescription system

– This system can only be employed in inpatient departments within a hospital setting,

where the patient demand is relatively predictable (e.g., maternity ward, paediatric ward,
ITFC, etc.).

– Medications are replenished from the hospital pharmacy based on the specific

requirements of individual patients.

– Once the medical ward round is completed, all patient files are compiled, and the required

medications for all patients are calculated. An order request is prepared and submitted to
the hospital pharmacy, ensuring the delivery of the necessary medications to the unit on
the same day.

– Medications can be requested collectively (by adding up the total required medication for

all patients in the unit) or individually. The medication will be prepared and dispensed by
the hospital pharmacy in unit-dose packagesf per patient (unit-dose system).

– Typically, a 24-hour supply of medications is provided. However, this may depend on the

hospital pharmacy's opening hours.

– This system must operate alongside the bulk stock system (also known as the cupboard

system) that provides the unit with small medical materials and consumables. Additionally,
the cupboard serves as an emergency stock, utilised when the hospital pharmacy is closed.

– Medications and other medical supplies needed for newly admitted patients can be

handled with an extra or unplanned order request or by using the cupboard stock available
in the unit (in case the hospital pharmacy is closed).

– Discharged patients are normally directed to the hospital dispensary. If the dispensary is

closed, they may be supplied directly from the unit using the cupboard stock.

Selecting the appropriate replenishment system

Choosing the right replenishment systems depends on various factors. Bulk stock
systems can be implemented in any end-user unit, whereas prescription systems are
limited to hospital settings.

Prescription systems require less time for stock management at the unit level compared
to bulk stock systems and generally result in fewer medication errors, particularly when
the individual prescription variant is used.

However, prescription systems place a greater workload on the hospital pharmacy and
require enhanced supervision. This is especially relevant for the individual prescription
variant, which involves double-checking medications. This process typically involves
two healthcare professionals verifying the medication before administration. It includes
confirming the patient's identity, the medication’s name, dosage, time, and route of

administration against the prescription.

f Usually, small plastic bags are labelled correctly with all necessary information for identification (patient name,

drug name, quantity, and dose).

Chapter 6: Stock management at end-user units

156

6.7 Return of medical products

– At times, end-user units may need to return products to the distribution pharmacy or

medical warehouse. This may occur for various reasons, such as suspected quality issues,
expired or damaged products, or to prevent overstock and further product expiration.

– The reason for the return should be specified. See Appendix 15.

– All stock returns must be documented and properly archived. The return should be

recorded as an outgoing movement (OUT) from the end-user unit and an incoming
movement (IN) by the receiver.

In certain countries, the destruction of ampoules or vials for controlled drugs requires

authorisation through a special permit issued by national authorities. In such cases,
these ampoules and vials must be collected by the distribution pharmacy or medical warehouse.

For more information on the reception of returned products at the distribution pharmacy or
medical warehouse, see Chapter 5 of GSDP.

Chapter 6: Stock management at end-user units

157

6

6.8 Waste management

– Correct hazardous waste management requires a multidisciplinary effort.

– The hazardous waste generated during health-related activities must always be segregated

according to the procedures in place.

– In general, the Infection, Prevention, and Control (IPC) team is responsible for collecting

hazardous waste from the end-user units, ensuring that the segregation, handling, and
elimination of biohazardous waste are done properly, without risk of infection.

– Sometimes the collection of hazardous waste is managed by the end-user unit staff (e.g.,

vaccination campaign or mobile clinic) before being handed over to the IPC team.

– Refer to the MSF Health-Related Hazardous Waste Management within LMIC guide for

detailed information about waste management and different destruction methods (in
Toolbox).

Special attention should be given when handling antineoplastic medicines

– The medical staff must be trained in the segregation of all high-risk hazardous waste and

in the management of potential spills.

– The medical materials used during this practice (e.g., gowns, syringes, infusion bags)

may have come into contact with medication and should, therefore, be considered
hazardous pharmaceutical waste.

Chapter 7: Good dispensing practices, administration, and handling of medical products

161

7

7.1 Introduction

Dispensing refers to the process by which a qualified healthcare provider prepares and gives
medication to a named patient based on a medical prescription, provides necessary counselling
and advice, and ensures proper medication use.

Medical and paramedical staff—pharmacists, pharmacy technicians, nurses, nurse aides,
clinicians, etc.—are qualified to dispense medications as long as they get adequate training.

Dispensing can take place in various settings, from hospital pharmacies to end-user units such
as dispensaries and mobile clinics, etc.

Dispensing should not be confused with medication administration, which refers to the direct
administration of the medication to the patient, typically performed by a nurse or another
qualified healthcare provider.

Good dispensing practices (GdP) are essential for the rational use of medications. They ensure
the following:

– The right medication is dispensed to the right patient for the right indication at the right

time.

– The medication is provided in the correct form, dose, and quantity.

– Suitable packaging is used to maintain the effectiveness of the medication.

– Essential tools (e.g., measuring cups) are provided for accurate administration of the

medication.

– Patients receive education about their medications.

On the contrary, poor dispensing practices may result in several serious consequences,
including:

– Medication errors that can lead to underdosing, reducing treatment effectiveness or to

overdosing, potentially harming patients.

– Patients' non-adherencea to the prescribed treatment, due to a lack of understanding.

– Adverse events and drug interactions (see Chapter 8, Section 8.5.1) that reduce the quality

of care and endanger patients.

– Waste of medications and financial resources.

– Spread of diseases.

– Loss of trust in the MSF and healthcare system.

a According to WHO, medication adherence is defined as "the degree to which the person’s behaviour corresponds

with the agreed recommendations from a health care provider.’’ World Health Organization. Adherence to
long-term therapies : evidence for action. Published 2003. https://iris.who.int/handle/10665/42682

Chapter 7: Good dispensing practices, administration, and handling of medical products

162

7.2 Good dispensing practices

The four main pillars of GdP are environment, staff, equipment, and process.

7.2.1 Dispensing environment

A dispensary is an end-user unit whose primary function is medication dispensing. It differs
from other end-user units where dispensing occurs but is not the primary function (e.g.,
emergency rooms, maternity units, etc.).

The dispensing area should minimise patient waiting time to ensure patient well-being, safety,
and adherence to treatment. There should be a logical patient flow to avoid overcrowding
within the healthcare facility and ensure patient privacy.

Dispensary layout

This picture outlines the typical components of a dispensary. It is important to note that
each dispensary can be different depending on needs, available resources, and local rules.
Therefore, not all dispensaries may include all elements in the picture; in some places, the
dispensing may happen in an open space or through a window.

Medications are prepared for dispensing in the preparation area. This area is designated for
tasks such as unpacking medications from their primary packaging, counting, and repackaging
them into the final package for dispensing to patients. This area should be designed to facilitate
these tasks efficiently while minimising the risk of medication errors.

Depending on the needs and resources, the preparation area may be integrated within the
dispensing area or established as a separate space.

Chapter 7: Good dispensing practices, administration, and handling of medical products

163

7

Figure 7.1 - Dispensary layout

Source: MSF

Dispensing environment standards

Dispensing environment standards are the conditions and procedures required to ensure the
safe and efficient dispensing of medications and medical products. They include:

– Physical environment

To ensure safety and efficacy during dispensing, the environment must uphold strict hygiene
standards and no-touch practicesb. This involves maintaining a clean, well-lit, and hygienic
environment free of contamination and hazards. It should provide sufficient space for the
proper storage, preparation, and dispensing of medications. Access to the dispensing area
should be restricted to authorised staff to maintain this environment.

b Strict hygiene standards and no-touch practices in the context of dispensing refer to methods that minimise the

risk of contamination and maintain hygiene. This includes thorough hand hygiene before handling medications,
ensuring a clean dispensing area, and using appropriate tools such as spoons to handle medications without
touching them.

Chapter 7: Good dispensing practices, administration, and handling of medical products

164

Remember

• Maintain surfaces clear of any unnecessary clutter to reduce areas where dust and
contaminants can accumulate.

• Maintain floors clean and remove garbage to prevent pests.

• Maintain regular cleaning, disinfection (Refer to the MSF Essential drugs guideline,
Antiseptics and disinfectants) and pest control (see Chapter 5).

• Keep food and drinks out of the dispensing area. Use the refrigerator exclusively for
storing medications to prevent temperature fluctuations and avoid contamination from
food-borne sources.

• Do not smoke in the dispensing area to prevent airborne contaminants and preserve air
quality.

• Wipe up any spills immediately, especially if the spilt liquid is sticky, sweet, or attractive
to insects.

– Storage conditions

Regarding storage conditions in dispensaries, see Chapter 6.

– Patient access, confidentiality, and comfort

The dispensing area must be easily visible and accessible to all patients, including those
with disabilities.
Maintaining patient privacy is essential for building trust between patients and the medical
staff. The waiting area should provide appropriate seating, drinking water, and protection
from the sun and rain.

– Management of quality issues

A quality issue is any deviation, defect, or nonconformity that affects the safety, efficacy,
or performance of a product or service. In the context of medical products, it specifically
relates to issues that compromise their intended quality standards or specifications.
To ensure the safety and effectiveness of dispensed medications and other medical
products, a quality issues management system should be implemented. For more details,
see Chapter 9.

– Compliance with national regulations

The dispensing environment should comply with national regulations and standards,
including guidelines for medication safety, public health, and ethical considerations.
Adherence to these standards helps to ensure that the dispensing environment is safe and
appropriate, and that medications and medical products are dispensed in a manner that
protects public health and benefits to patients.

7.2.2 Dispensing staff

To ensure that MSF positively impacts patients’ health and well-being, dispensers should be
trained and educated on GdP and the administration of medications.

Chapter 7: Good dispensing practices, administration, and handling of medical products

165

7

Key responsibilities include:

– Prescription interpretation: checking that prescriptions are complete and include clear

indication, dosages, route of administration, frequency, and duration of treatment before
they are filledc.

– Dispensing medications: ensuring that the right patient gets the right medication in the

correct dose and quantity.

– Patient medication education: providing patients with clear and accurate information

about their medications, including proper use, dose, potential adverse events, necessary
precautions, and the importance of promptly reporting any adverse events back to
healthcare providers.

– Monitoring: ensuring regular monitoring and reporting of adverse events and quality

issues.

– Record keeping: maintaining accurate and up-to-date records of patients’ history of

medication dispensing and administration.

– Compliance with national regulations and MSF standards.

All staff involved in medication dispensing should possess the key skills required to perform
their duties effectively.

Key skills

• Attention to detail: dispensers must read and interpret prescriptions and labels
accurately and double-check their work to avoid errors.

• Communication: dispensers should have good skills to communicate with patients,
physicians, and other healthcare providers and to give medication information and
advice.

• Knowledge: dispensers should have a comprehensive understanding of the medications
they dispense, including indications, doses, drug interactions, and adverse events of
different medications. Dispensers should also be aware of infection prevention and
control (IPC) procedures, such as handwashing and touch-free dispensing, and ensure
they apply these in their daily activities.

• Technical skills: besides being able to read, write, count, calculate, and label, dispensers
should be familiar with the monitoring tool. Refer to the HQ pharmacy referent for more
information.

• Organisation: dispensers must be organised and able to manage their time effectively,
as they often handle multiple tasks simultaneously.

7.2.3 Dispensing equipment

Dispensing equipment includes the tools and materials essential for accurate medication
dispensing. This includes tablet counters (see Table 7.1), spoons (not intended for patient
dosing), empty oral syringes, empty dosing cups (for patients), plastic bags (see Table 7.1),

c Where legally allowed and when the dispenser is a pharmacist who can access patient medical records, it is

crucial to verify that prescriptions align with the patient's health conditions and other medications the patient
might be taking.

Chapter 7: Good dispensing practices, administration, and handling of medical products

166

tablet cutters (see Table 7.1), and other related tools. Dispensing equipment should be
regularly checked to ensure accuracy and patients’ safety.

Maintaining strict hygiene standards and employing no-touch practices with

dispensing equipment is important. While the equipment is not germ-free or sterile,
these practices significantly minimise the risk of contamination by ensuring a clean method
for handling equipment and reducing the likelihood of introducing germs into the medication
during dispensing.

Cross-contamination occurs when some traces of uncoated tablets transfer to other medications
resting on the same surface. This is particularly dangerous when these traces contain substances
to which a patient might be allergic (e.g., acetylsalicylic acid or penicillin). Dispensers should
clean used equipment (e.g., tablet counters and cutters) between applications and at the
end of the day. It is recommended to keep a separate spoon with each medication's primary
package to prevent cross-contamination. If that is not possible, those spoons should not be
used.

Tablets and capsules can be counted with or without a counting device. The most important
rule to follow is that the dispenser’s hands must come into direct contact with the medication.
Using hands for counting is considered a bad practice for both hygienic and product-quality
reasons. Counting should be done using one of the following:

– A clean piece of paper and a clean knife or spatula

– A clean tablet-counting device

– The lid of the stock container in use

– Any other clean, dust-free surface

Immediately after measuring or counting, the stock container lid should be replaced, and the
stock container label should be rechecked for drug name and strength.

In some situations (e.g., when dispensing dangerous medications, such as cytotoxic medicines
or in an environment with communicable diseases), dispensers must use appropriate personal
protective equipment (PPE) to ensure both their own and patients’ safety. This includes
wearing appropriate gloves (e.g., nitrile gloves for cytotoxic medicines) and medical masks.

If a dispenser removes a medication from its primary packaging, it must be adequately
repackaged and relabelled (see Section 7.2.4). Whenever possible, medications should be
dispensed in their original packaging.

Dispensers should package medications in materials suitable for the duration of the treatment
course. Plastic bags (see Table 7.1) should not be used for treatments lasting longer than
seven days, as they cannot guarantee the long-term quality of medications due to their lack of
opacity and airtightness. As an alternative, plastic bottles or containers (see Table 7.1) can be
used in such cases.

If tablets need to be cut, a tablet cutter (see Table 7.1) should be used. The tablet cutter must
be cleaned with a non-abrasive cleaner or soft cloth and warm water after each use. Ensure
equipment is dried after cleaning, as abrasive cleaners or rough cleaning pads may damage the
product beyond repair.

Chapter 7: Good dispensing practices, administration, and handling of medical products

167

7

At the end of the day, used dispensing tools should be properly cleaned and stored.

Dispensers should adhere to the specific medical guidelines provided to ensure consistent and
safe dispensing practicesd.

7.2.4 Dispensing process

The dispensing process must be clearly defined and familiar to all dispensing staff. This process
involves several steps from receiving the prescription to handing the medication to the patient.
It also includes providing instructions and, if necessary, demonstrating how the medication
should be used. Standard operating procedures (SOPs) should be firmly established to guide
this process, addressing prescription validation, medication preparation, patient education,
and record-keeping.

Adherence to GdP is essential to ensure that medications are dispensed correctly. GdP helps
identify and correct potential errors related to the prescription or those that may arise during
dispensing, before the medication reaches the patient.

Refer to the document Good dispensing practices: the process (Toolbox) for a summary of this
section.

Steps for dispensing

Step 1. Prescription reading and validation

Prescriptions must be clearly written and in a language that the dispenser understands.
They should include all the required information:

– Patient information: name, age, and weight for children— and some adults, depending on

the condition.

– Medication details: international non-proprietary name (INN), dose, route of

administration, frequency, duration, and instructions for use.

– Prescription date: it helps avoid the use of expired prescriptions.

d At a minimum, these should include updated versions of: MSF Essential drugs guideline, MSF Clinical guidelines,

relevant local protocols or guidelines related to the dispensing of medication in the country of operation.
e For detailed information (e.g., definition, specifications, instructions for use, etc.), refer to the MSF catalogues.

Chapter 7: Good dispensing practices, administration, and handling of medical products

168

– Prescriber information: check the prescriber’s name to make sure that they are authorised

to write prescriptions. It might be helpful to have a list of validated prescribers along with
their signatures or stamps.

Abbreviations should be avoided, except for the standard international abbreviations

(e.g., g for gram, mL for millilitre, IV for intravenous, etc.).

Upon receiving a prescription, the dispenser should screen and validate it to ensure it

is intended for the correct patient. This step is essential, particularly in high-volume
settings when there is a risk that staff or patients may mix up prescriptions. Cross-checking the
name and identity of patients (using the birth date, first and last name, patient number, etc.)
helps avoid the risk of errors.

Step 2 - Prescription understanding and interpretation

Upon receiving a prescription, the dispenser should screen and validate it to ensure it meets
all basic legal requirements and is clearly written for dispensing. The dispenser should confirm
that the prescription contains the minimum information required (see Prescription reading
and validation above).

Where the context allows, depending on the legal framework and staff competencies, the
dispenser ensures that:

– The doses are appropriate for the patient's age and weight.

– Potential drug interactions are identified and mitigated. See more information in Chapter 8.

– Where applicablef, the prescription adheres to the relevant clinical guidelines or

prescribing protocol.

In this way, the prescription validation is complete and thorough, ensuring the prescribed
medication is safe and effective for the patient. This helps minimise the risk of medication
errors and adverse reactions.

• Contact the prescriber if any part of the prescription needs to be completed, corrected,
or clarified. If it is not possible to contact the prescriber, inform the patient and return
the prescription to the prescriber, including any clarification or action needed.

• In case of a stockout of prescribed medication or a specific dose of a medication, the
dispenser typically informs the project pharmacist. The pharmacist will then coordinate
with the prescriber to identify suitable therapeutic alternatives and ensure that the

MAM (medical activity manager) is kept informed.

f According to the context's legal requirements, the diagnosis (a full name or a medical code) may or may not be

included in the prescription.

Chapter 7: Good dispensing practices, administration, and handling of medical products

169

7

Step 3 - Medication preparation, packing and labelling

Dispensers must prepare, package, and label prescribed medications separately and avoid
removing medication from its original packaging whenever possible. Dispensers should not
unpack solid oral medications from blisters.

Note: when cutting blisters to dispense individual doses, it is crucial to either retain

the part containing expiry dates and batch numbers or record this information
elsewhere, as it is vital for tracking medication use, ensuring effectiveness, and recalling
products if necessary.

Dispensers must prepare some formulations (e.g., oral suspensions) before giving them to
patients. In this case, the dispenser should:

– Adhere to the manufacturer’s instructions or an established protocol.

– Accurately calculate the quantity of medication needed.

– Wash their hands and use clean tools and equipment (e.g., tablet counter, spoons).

– Label the bottle (see Figure 7.2) with information regarding dosage, frequency, duration of

the treatment, and any other necessary information from the original container’s label.

If solid oral medication needs to be counted and dispensed from a larger original container
(e.g., 1000 tablets in a box), dispensers should use dispensing equipment (see Section 7.2.3)
and follow strict hygiene standards and non-touch practicesg.

Dispensers should always open the container one at a time and close it immediately after use
to avoid medication errors, contamination, and deterioration.

Dispensers should check the expiry dates of medications to ensure they remain valid throughout
the treatment.

All dispensed medication must be clearly labelled to ensure proper use and patient safety. The
minimum mandatory labelling requirements are:

– Patient’s name (or initials, where full names are not feasible or pose a security risk)

– Drug name (INN), strength, and form (e.g., tablet, capsule)

– Quantity

– Instructions for use (route of administration, dose, frequency, duration, etc.)

Whenever space permits, it is also preferable to include the following information:

– Name and address of the dispensing facility or hospital

– Dispensing date

– Storage conditions

– Unique dispensing number (if legally required)

– Child safety warning (e.g. “Warning – Keep Out of Reach of Children”)

g Strict hygiene standards and no-touch practices in the context of dispensing refer to methods that minimize the

risk of contamination and maintain hygiene. This includes thorough hand hygiene before handling medications,
ensuring a clean dispensing area, and using appropriate tools such as spoons to handle medications without
touching them.

Chapter 7: Good dispensing practices, administration, and handling of medical products

170

If solid oral medications are altered (e.g., crushed or split tablets for easier administration),
it is important to provide detailed documentation and clear instructions to the patient (see
Section 7.3).

Note: this is the only information that should be included. The dispenser should prioritise
information by considering the essential elements for medication safety and the specific
context.

Pre-packaged medication
If many patients are on standardised treatment with tablets or capsules that are not packed in
blisters, medication can be pre-packaged in advance.

Pre-packaging can be done in plastic bags (see Table 7.1) only if the medication is dispensed
on the same day, to avoid poor storage conditions.
If the medication is expected to be dispensed later, it should be pre-packaged in a plastic
container (see Table 7.1).

Pre-packaging enables safer, faster distribution with reduced risk of errors. The pre-packaging
procedure needs to be well-established and supervised. It includes:

– Proper labelling: medication name, strength, quantity, route of administration,

precautions, etc.

– Proper storage: in a cool place, away from direct sunlight and moisture.

If the medication appears discoloured, smells unusual, or shows any other signs of

degradation, it should not be used.

Note: it cannot be guaranteed that the expiry date of pre-packaged and dispensed medications
matches the expiry date on the original packaging.

Figure 7.2- Label for dispensing 6x8 cm pictogram for bottles and plastic containersh.

Source: MSF catalogues.

h For detailed information (e.g., definition, specifications, instructions for use, etc.), refer to the MSF catalogues.

Chapter 7: Good dispensing practices, administration, and handling of medical products

171

7

Step 4 – Final prescription checks for all medications

At this step, dispensers should double-check the prepared medication(s) against the
prescription.

The final check should include:

– Comparing the prescribed and prepared medications.

– Verifying the appropriateness of prescribed doses and the quantity of prepared

medications.

– Checking for potential drug interactions.

– Ensuring that the labels contain all the necessary information.

While the same dispenser can do this step, it is recommended that another dispenser

double-checks the prescription and prepared medication(s) to minimise the risk of
error.

To aid in this double-checking process, a useful phrase recommended for dispensers

to use is: “The physician prescribed this medication at this dose/frequency/duration

for this patient. Is this correct? Is this what has been dispensed?”

Step 5 – Medication delivery and explanation

The dispenser must ensure that the patient or caretaker understands the key elements of
medication use, for which the WWHAM acronym is a practical and recommended guide:

W – Who is this medication for?
W – What is the medication, and what is it for?

H – How to take it?

This includes when (at what time and how many times per day), for how long (especially

with antimicrobials), and details about administration such as taking it before or during
meals, whether to swallow or chew tablets, how to reconstitute powder (e.g., oral rehydration
solution) and how to crush tablets for children.

A – Any allergies or adverse events?

The dispenser should ask if the patient has any known allergies and explains potential

adverse events and drug interactions.

M – Monitoring or when to check the condition again.

The dispenser should provide instructions on what action to take in case of a missed

dose, as well as specific storage requirements.

Chapter 7: Good dispensing practices, administration, and handling of medical products

172

If a medication comes with a measuring device (e.g., syrup or oral suspension), the dispenser
should demonstrate to the patient or caretaker how to measure and administer the required
dose.
When solid oral medications are altered, detailed documentation and clear instructions must
be provided to the patient.

A good practice is asking patients to repeat the instructions to verify their

understanding. There should be sufficient time and staff to provide a more in-depth

explanation of medication use for chronic or specific diseases, if needed.

Dispensers are ultimately responsible for reinforcing adherence to treatment. Therefore,
providing proper guidance and advice on medication use is vital to achieving and maximising
treatment outcomes.

After dispensing medication, dispensers should sign the prescription to indicate that they have
verified the prescription and the dispensed medication.

Step 6 - Dispensing recording

Dispensers should document every medication dispensed. At a minimum, this should include
the MSF code, medication name, and quantities dispensed.

To streamline stock management and enable accurate forecasting, compiling this data into
weekly consumption summaries is recommended. However, the format may differ depending
on the location and context.

Dispensing records are important for supply management, quality monitoring, and clinical
purposes. Recording the types and quantities of medications dispensed is essential for
generating accurate consumption data.

Dispensers should record the dispensing of controlled drugsi in the controlled drugs register,
which tracks all stock movements and must be maintained in accordance with national
regulations.

Dispensers must regularly verify that there are no discrepancies between the actual stockj and
the theoretical stockk of controlled drugs.

All prescriptions should be retained at the project site.

7.2.5 Dispensing monitoring

Monitoring can be implemented through diverse methods, such as treatment understanding
surveys or prescribing analyses across all end-user units.

There are specific systems in place to oversee and report on various aspects of medication.
For instance, the pharmacovigilance system is designed to report adverse drug reactions. For
medication errors such as incorrect drug, route, timing, dose, or patient, a medical incident

i Drug or other substance that is tightly controlled by the government because it may be abused or cause

addiction. The control applies to the way the substance is made, used, handled, stored, and distributed.
Controlled drugs include opioids, stimulants, depressants, hallucinogens, and anabolic steroids. Controlled
drugs can differ from one country to another according to national legislation.
j Actual stock refers to the physical quantity that is present at a given time. It’s the number of units of a product

that are physically counted or verified during a physical count.
k Theoretical stock refers to the number of units that is recorded in a stock management tool. It represents the

expected stock level based on previous physical counts and stock movements.

Chapter 7: Good dispensing practices, administration, and handling of medical products

174

reporting system is available. These incidents should be documented in the patient's file by the
healthcare provider and reported through a formalised procedure.

See more details in Chapter 8.

7.2.6 Special considerations

– There are certain requirements for extemporaneous compoundingl. Pharmacists should

consult with HQ if extemporaneous compounding is needed to ensure proper preparation.

– Maintaining GdP can be challenging when the dispenser is handling numerous patients

in the dispensing process, accuracy is a priority. Dispensers should ask prescribers to
only prescribe medical products available at the pharmacy to avoid delays and patient
confusion.

– Repeat prescriptions: some patients may be unable to collect their ongoing treatment

every month due to various constraints (e.g., insecurity, living in remote areas, etc.). In
these cases, providing medication for several months at once or arranging deliverym to
the patient’s location can be appropriate solutions. Repeat prescriptions are intended
for stable and adherent patients or for caretakers who are familiar with the treatment
regimen. In such situations, direct contact with the dispenser is not required, as the
medication use is already well understood.

An illustrative example of the repeat prescriptions approach is the Community ART groups
(CAG) model. See Toolbox, Community ART Group Toolkit, how to implement the CAG model.

Controlled drugs should not be delivered or dispensed for several months.

Medications that require specific storage conditions should not be delivered nor

dispensed for several months unless there is a way to ensure the patient can properly
store them.

– Dispensers must not reintroduce any medical products returned by a patient into the

supply and distribution system. If a medical product has left the healthcare facility at any
point, it is impossible to guarantee its storage conditions or integrity.

– If medications are returned from a ward to the hospital pharmacy, a pharmacist should

assess whether they have been stored under the appropriate conditions and can be
reused, ensuring their efficacy and safety remain uncompromised.

– Specific precautions apply for controlled drugs, including separate locked storage and a

controlled drug register to track all stock movements, along with full compliance with all
national regulations. Based on national regulations, only a licensed pharmacist or medical
practitioner may dispense controlled drugs.

– A corrective and preventive action (CAPA)n is a helpful approach to addressing deviations

from GdP.

l ’’Extemporaneous compounding is the preparation of a therapeutic product for an individual patient in

response to an identified need. Ramtoola Z, Catibusic A, Ameen H, Cullen S, Barlow JW. Evaluation of the type
and frequency of extemporaneous formulations dispensed in hospital and community pharmacies. Exploratory
Research in Clinical and Social Pharmacy. 2023;12:100380. doi:10.1016/j.rcsop.2023.100380
m Medication delivery needs to be clinically appropriate and discussed with the pharmacist and the prescriber. It

might only be applicable in some cases.
n The corrective and preventive actions (CAPA) approach is designed to ensure that in the event of deviations

or nonconformities from the desired standards for proper storage and distribution practices, appropriate
measures are promptly implemented, and their effectiveness is monitored.

7.2.7 Patient medication education

Patient medication education is essential as patients often face stressful and challenging
circumstances, limited healthcare access and may have low health literacy levels. When
dispensing medication, it is necessary to provide patients with clear and accurate information
about their treatment, including the correct dosage, use, potential adverse events, drug
interactions and precautions. Prescribers, pharmacists, dispensers and other healthcare
providers must collaborate to provide patients with this information.

Dispensers should be trained to deliver patient medication education sessions during
dispensing and provide patients with information about specific medical conditions according
to the context.

This kind of initiative can initially address only a few medical conditions and, if successfully
implemented, can be expanded to address more.

Patient education during dispensing

– Welcome the patient and ask for their consent to discuss their treatment.

– Assess the patient's knowledge about the treatment by asking open-ended questions such

as "Can you tell me what you have understood from the doctor about your treatment?"

– Complete and correct the patient's information.

– Repeat the correct information to the patient, if needed.

– Ensure that the patient understands all the necessary information.

– Explore any barriers that might affect the patient’s adherence to the treatment.

– Reinforce the benefits of medications to enhance the patient’s adherence.

– Ask the patient how they will manage to take the treatment correctly; suggest putting

the medication box in the kitchen to remember it before breakfast, or next to the bed to
remember it before sleeping, etc.

Patient education is important for several reasons:

– Improving treatment outcomes: when patients are fully informed about their medications,

they are more likely to take them as prescribed, which improves the treatment outcomes.

– Enhancing patient adherence: when patients understand the reason for prescribing a

particular treatment and how it works, they are more likely to adhere to the treatment,
which ensures the effectiveness of the medication.

– Reducing the risk of adverse events: when patients are informed about potential adverse

events and necessary precautions, they are better equipped to recognise and report any
adverse reactions, which minimises the risk of harm and supports the pharmacovigilance
reporting system.

– Building trust in the healthcare system: when patients are provided with clear and

accurate information about their medication, they are more likely to trust the healthcare
system and be more engaged in their self-care.

– Improving the management of diseases: patient education also helps patients better

understand the underlying conditions and strategies for managing them effectively, which
improves their overall health and quality of life.

Chapter 7: Good dispensing practices, administration, and handling of medical products

176

Healthcare organisations and public health systems are now adopting a new approach to
increase patient engagement and treatment outcomes. This approach is called the Patient-as-
partner approach. Healthcare providers do not talk to the patient; they talk with the patient.

In this approach, healthcare providers consider patients partners in their care by respecting
and promoting their expertise and knowledge gained from living with their illness every day.
Considering patients as partners in the healthcare system significantly impacts medical practice
and helps to achieve optimal health outcomes.

Any approach to educating patients and involving them in decision-making can be achieved
through effective, practical communication skills that enable healthcare providers to build
trust and rapport with patients.

How to improve communication skills with the patient?

• Listen actively: dispensers should listen attentively to patients and ask questions to
clarify any misunderstandings.

• Ask engaging questions: engaging open-ended questioning allows dispensers to deepen
their understanding of the patient’s situation. This type of questioning helps gauge the
patient's knowledge and addresses any concerns or misunderstandings to educate the
patient and promote their health and well-being.

• Show empathy: dispensers should be able to understand and respond to the concerns
and needs of patients.

• Paraphrase: dispensers should repeat the information given by the patient in their own
words to ensure accuracy and comprehension. Paraphrasing also allows the dispenser to
clarify any misunderstandings or misconceptions the patient may have.

• Use clear language: dispensers should be able to explain medication information and
instructions clearly and concisely using language adapted to the patient's health literacy.

• Consider different cultures: dispensers should be able to communicate effectively with
patients from diverse cultural backgrounds.

• Communicate verbally and non-verbally: dispensers should use verbal and non-verbal
communication effectively to convey information and understand patients' needs.

Refer to the document Welcome, understand and facilitate framework (Toolbox) for
more information.

Chapter 7: Good dispensing practices, administration, and handling of medical products

177

7

7.3 Safe administration and handling of medical
products

All healthcare providers involved in medication preparation and administration should
receive training on recommended practices for safely and correctly handling various forms of
medications.

7.3.1 Oral medications

For syrup or oral suspension, the healthcare provider must supply the patient or caretaker
with an accurate, approved measuring device (e.g., oral or enteral syringe, measuring cup)
along with the instructions for use. This ensures they can administer the correct dose.

It is important to note the expiry date after the suspension is prepared, as well as storage
conditions, to maintain the medication's effectiveness and safety.

Oral suspensions should be prepared with a specified amount of potable or purified water and
shaken well before use (see MSF Essential drugs guideline).

Solid oral medications can be altered (i.e., crushed or dispersed) if a liquid form is absolutely
needed but not available. However, certain medications and formulations should never be
altered (e.g. sustained or extended-release formulations, cytotoxic medications). Individual
monographs and references must be verified before altering solid oral medications.

Altering the form of medication should only be considered if no other safe and effective
alternatives are available. Dispensers, pharmacists, and prescribers should collaboratively
discuss this decision, considering medical safety and legal implications.

In some cases, patients may be able to perform the alteration themselves if the process
is straightforward (e.g., splitting breakable tablets in half). In general, compounding is
recommended only in MSF settings if all requirements are met and the decision is approved
by HQ.

Needles should never be used to administer oral liquids or altered oral medications.

Any alteration of solid oral medications should be documented on the prescription and
thoroughly explained and demonstrated to the patient. The documentation should detail the
method used to ensure appropriate consistency and facilitate medication intake (e.g., crushed
and mixed with yoghurt, dispersed in 10 mL water, etc.).

If it is necessary to alter multiple solid oral medications, each medication should be prepared,
administered or dispensed one at a time. Multiple medications should never be crushed
together into a single mixture. Alteration should be done at cold or room temperature.
Purified or distilled water should be used (not hot water, which may reduce the medication's
effectiveness).

Chapter 7: Good dispensing practices, administration, and handling of medical products

178

Equipment used to alter a dosage form (e.g., mortar and pestle, tablet crusher, etc.) must be
washed and dried thoroughly after use for each patient (see Section 7.2.3).

Staff involved in altering solid oral medications must wear personal protective equipment (PPE)
when handling potent, irritant, or sensitisation-causing drugs (e.g., hormones, corticosteroids,
immunosuppressants, phenothiazines and antimicrobials) to ensure their safety and that of
others in the area. In these cases, PPE should include at least protective glasses, a medical
mask, and gloves.

A "closed system" technique (such as dispersal within a syringe) may be used per HQ advice.
Women intending to become pregnant may require additional precautions – individual
monographs and references, as well as the HQ pharmacy referent, should be consulted for
further information. In case of any doubt, the HQ pharmacy referent or other relevant HQ
medical referents should be consultedo,p.

7.3.2 Injectable medications

General principles

– In a dispensary, injectable medications should not be dispensed for patient self-

administration. Instead, they should be administered directly in wards or consultation
rooms, with some exceptions for specific medications, such as insulin.

– Always prepare injectable medications using an aseptic techniqueq according to the MSF

Manual of nursing care, the MSF Infection prevention and control medical policy, the
Neonatal Injectable Medication Handbook and the MSF Paediatric Injectable Handbook
(see Toolbox),

– Adhere to the "one-time principle" of safe injection practices: one patient, one syringe,

one needle, one time. Even if the needle has been changed, the syringe should not be
reused.

– Injectable medications should always be prepared and administered by the same person.

– Medical staff must safely discard both needles and syringes right after using them,

according to MSF protocol (e.g. sharps container).

– All injections should be adequately labelled immediately after preparation, except for

syringes intended for immediate bolus administration.

– A medication in a syringe should never be left unattended or unlabelled. Medical staff

should never hold more than one unlabelled syringe at a time, nor place an unlabelled
syringe in an electric syringe pump or similar device.

– Unused portions of vials or ampoules should be returned to the pharmacy or the

appropriate disposal area, with no exceptions. This is essential to prevent illegitimate
use and environmental risks. For controlled drugs, this return should be recorded in the
controlled drugs register.

o Refer to the respective MSF section for 2014_MSF_Intersectional_Fieldguide Compounding Medicines.
p Refer to the respective MSF section for the ‘’Don't Rush to Crush’’ document.
q For further reading, see: Nursing ORF. 4.3 Aseptic technique. Pressbooks. Published on 28 August 2023. https://

wtcs.pressbooks.pub/nursingskills/chapter/4-3-asceptic-technique/#:~:text=,urinary%20catheterization%20
uses%20aseptic%20technique

And General Questions | Injection Safety | CDC. https://www.cdc.gov/injectionsafety/providers/provider_
faqs_general.html

Chapter 7: Good dispensing practices, administration, and handling of medical products

179

7

Ampoules

– Open ampoules should never be covered with tape or gauze.

– Medication from an opened ampoule should not be stored, even in a syringe, due to

potential reactions with the syringe material.

Vials

– Before each use, the rubber top of the vial should be disinfected, as per the MSF Manual

of nursing care.

– The needle should be removed from the rubber top after each preparation.

– Reconstituted vials should never be stored.

– Open vials should never be covered with tape or gauze.

Correct use of single-dose vials

• Single-dose vials should be prepared immediately before administration and discarded
immediately after use.

• All opened single-dose vials should be discarded either at the time specified by the
manufacturer or immediately after the injection, especially in high-risk areas, such as
emergency rooms, operating theatres, dressing rooms and intensive care units, to avoid
contamination. Single-dose vials should not be stored for future use.

• In specific contexts (e.g. neonatology), a single-dose vial may be used for more than one
patient. In these cases, the same vial should only be used during a single medication
round, with small doses administered solely by the same healthcare provider. This
should be done following a strict aseptic technique to prevent cross-contamination or
infection. Always follow local guidelines and national regulations.

Correct use of multiple-dose vials

• Once prepared and opened, multiple-dose vials (i.e., vaccines) should be dated when
first opened and stored between +2 °C and +8 °C for no more than the time specified by
the manufacturer.

• All vials should be discarded once opened or used to avoid contamination, especially
in high-risk areas, such as emergency rooms, operating theatres, dressing rooms and
intensive care units.

• The medical staff should not, at any time, leave a needle inserted into the septum of a
vial, as this compromises both the sterility and stability of the vial’s contents.

See WHO Policy Statement for Multi-dose Vial Policy (MDVP)1

Chapter 7: Good dispensing practices, administration, and handling of medical products

180

IV fluids/infusions

– Discard the needle after withdrawing the fluid from the bag.r

– Always use a new needle and syringe every time.

– After the medication round, do not store used bags; instead, discard them along with any

remaining liquid.

– According to the MSF Manual of nursing care, the medical staff must adequately label

syringes and infusion bags containing injectable medication. There should be a maximum
expiry time of 24 hours for all prepared infusions, starting from the time of preparation,
unless otherwise specified by the manufacturer.

For flexible plastic bags, but NOT nipple head bottles and EUROHEADs bottles:

– In certain contexts (e.g., neonatology), where small volumes of medication are needed,

it may be acceptable to use one IV infusion bag for multiple patients. It is only allowed
during the same medication round.

– Frequently accessing the IV infusion bag through the gummi/needle entrance point is not

advised, as this increases the risk of leakage and potential contamination. If available, use
10 mL normal saline ampoules to minimise the need for reassessing IV fluids and reduce
contamination risks.

– If multiple accesses to an IV infusion bag (or bottle) are required, use a specific tool, such

as the dispensing spike, designed for this purpose (See Figure 7.5).

– Before each use, make sure to thoroughly disinfect the access point, as per the MSF

Infection prevention and control medical policy.

Figure 7.5 - Dispenser spike with needleless connector

r Using a dispensing spike instead of the needle is preferred to avoid compromising the rubber.
s EUROHEAD is a specific type of closure system for plastic infusion bottles.

Chapter 7: Good dispensing practices, administration, and handling of medical products

7.3.3 High-risk medications

High-risk medications have a narrow therapeutic index and a high risk of causing significant
patient harm when used incorrectly. These include categories of medications often remembered
by the A-PINCH acronym:

– A: Antimicrobials

– P: Potassium and other electrolytes

– I: Insulin

– N: Narcotics and other sedatives

– C: Chemotherapeutic agents

– H: Heparin and other anticoagulants

High-risk medications can also include neuromuscular blockers, epidural and intrathecal
agents, clinical trial medications and sound-alike look-alike drugs (SALADs)t.

High-risk medications should be prepared, administered, and monitored by qualified, trained,
and supervised staff. Although dispensers should ensure proper labelling and storage for all
medications, they should give extra care and separate high-risk medications to reduce the risk
of errors.

According to national legislation, appropriate systems must be implemented to facilitate safe
and proper waste management of medications and medical products, especially hazardous
ones.

t SALADs refer to drugs with names or packaging that look or sound similar, increasing the risk of confusion and

medication errors.

Chapter 7: Good dispensing practices, administration, and handling of medical products

182

7.4 Case study

Case 1

In an outpatient department dispensary, the following observations were made:

– Many adults are given syrups instead of tablets.

– After dispensers provide the medications, the consumption is not recorded in a register or

software.

– Controlled drugs are stored on the shelves alongside other medications.

– There are no temperature control devices in the refrigerator.

– Physicians do not write prescriptions clearly (especially dosage and duration). Nonetheless,

dispensers continue to dispense the medications.

– Dispensers handle the tablets with their bare hands.

– Dispensers give medications without explaining how to take them.

Please correct the deviations using the CAPA approach.
A timeframe and a focal person must be allocated for each deviation.

Case 2

The dispensary is overwhelmed with patients seeking their medications. Amid the rush,
a patient approaches, presenting with a rash and constant itching all over the body. These
symptoms began after starting a prescribed course of ibuprofen for acute lower back pain.

During the conversation with the pharmacist, the patient reports having taken ibuprofen in the
past without adverse events. However, they also report an allergic reaction to penicillin while
being treated for a respiratory infection the year before. The doctor had firmly advised them
not to take any more penicillin due to this allergic reaction.

Given the patient’s symptoms and their history with ibuprofen and penicillin, what could be
the cause of this sudden rash and itching?

What preventive measures and solutions would be recommended to avoid such incidents in
the future?

Possible cause: the patient's symptoms might have resulted from cross-contamination
between penicillin and ibuprofen if the same spoon was used for both medications without
proper cleaning, or if both medications were prepared at the same time and on the same
surface without cleaning.

Note

– Although less common, it is possible for the patient to develop a new sensitivity or delayed

allergic reaction to ibuprofen.

– The rash could also be a reaction to another substance or ingredient in the medication.

To prevent this from happening:

– Ensure all staff understand the importance of preventing cross-contamination.

– Use a separate spoon for each medication to avoid mixing.

– If using the same spoon for different medications, clean it thoroughly in between uses. Use

disposable wipes or napkins for this purpose.

– Ensure that the surfaces where medications are prepared are kept clean and sanitised,

especially when handling different medications successively.

Chapter 7: Good dispensing practices, administration, and handling of medical products

184

– Regularly check that spoons and surfaces are cleaned properly, especially during busy

times.
