8.1 Introduction

Rational use of medical products includes the wise application and management of medications
and other medical products to ensure their efficacy and safety. This means providing patients
with appropriate treatment for the correct indications, using the right formulations and doses
for an adequate duration. The aim is to achieve optimal therapeutic outcomes, promote patient
adherence, avoid preventable adverse eventsa and drug interactions, minimise treatment
costs, and enhance the quality of care.

Rational use is crucial in minimising harm, maximising treatment efficacy, reducing healthcare
costs, and enhancing patient and public health. Conversely, irrational use can increase
morbidity and mortality, imposing a significant burden on healthcare systems.

Pharmacists play a fundamental role in maintaining and promoting the rational use of medical
products within MSF and global healthcare systems.

General and common examples of irrational use

– Unjustified polypharmacy (use of multiple medications per patient).

– Non-adherence to clinical protocols and prescribing guidelines.

– Inappropriate self-medication practices.

– Non-compliance with prescribed therapy regimen.

– Extended duration of antibiotic therapy beyond recommended guidelines.

– Overprescription of antibiotics for viral infections.

– Incorrect dosing or frequency of medications.

– Prescribing medications without considering potential drug interactions.

a An adverse event is any new sign, symptom, laboratory test abnormality or worsening condition occurring

during or after the use of a medication and which does not necessarily have a causal relationship with that
medication.

Chapter 8: Rational use and monitoring

190

8.2 General recommendations

To ensure the rational use of medical products, the following general recommendations should
be considered:

– Ensure the availability, knowledge, and adherence to the latest versions of MSF’s Clinical

guidelines and Essential drugs guideline, MSF catalogues, medication handbooks, and
validated protocols for the preparation and administration of medical products.

– Maintain a medical standard list (MSL) that aligns with these guidelines and protocols to

ensure consistency and evidence-based practices across all operations.

– Uphold good dispensing practices. See Chapter 7. Foster teamwork and interdisciplinary

cooperation within the medical team to achieve the highest standards of care (e.g.,
antimicrobial stewardship committee, drugs and therapeutics committee, etc.) based on
contextual needs. See Section 8.4.3.

– Promote continuous therapeutic education for patients and training for medical staff. See

Chapter 7.

– Standardise and implement monitoring procedures (e.g., pharmacovigilance and medical

incident reporting). See Section 8.5.2.

– Regularly monitor and evaluate prescribing practices through various investigation

methods (e.g., prescription audits) and feedback.

– Ensure the continuous availability of essential medical products. See Chapter 2.

Chapter 8: Rational use and monitoring

191

8

8.3 Investigation of irrational use

Investigating the irrational use of medical products includes different aspects:

– Types of irrational use: identifying specific types of irrational use, such as polypharmacy,

inappropriate dosing, and extended durations of therapy (see Section 8.1), is essential for
developing targeted interventions.

– Reasons for irrational use: understanding the underlying causes is crucial for developing

efficient interventions. Common reasons include unrestricted medication availability,
insufficient knowledge among health staff and/or patients, non-adherence to medical
protocols and guidelines, and poor surveillance. High workloads for health staff can also
lead to rushed decisions and inadequate patient counselling on medication use.

– Extent of irrational use: assessing the magnitude of irrational use is important for

monitoring interventions. This includes evaluating the frequency of inappropriate
prescriptions (e.g., prescribing antibiotics for viral infections), the prevalence of
polypharmacy (e.g., prescribing two or more drugs for the same condition), rates of
medication non-adherence (e.g., patients are often readmitted due to not taking drugs as
prescribed), and the incidence of adverse drug reactions (e.g., high incidence of adverse
drug reactions due to polypharmacy).

Various quantitative and qualitative methods are available for investigating medication
practices. Combining methods provides a comprehensive understanding, enabling the design
of effective interventions to promote rational use. Below are some examples of these methods:

– Point prevalence survey (PPS)1 b: this is a one-shot evaluation that takes a snapshot of all

antibiotic prescriptions for all inpatients in a hospital at a specific time (e.g., 8:00 am). It is
often used to assess the quality of antimicrobial prescribing. PPS allows projects or units
to evaluate how antibiotics are used and develop targeted plans to improve patient safety
and quality of care by optimising antibiotic use.

– Focused drug use evaluation (drug utilisation review)2: this involves reviewing

prescription data before, during, and after dispensing or administration to identify issues
with specific medications, usually in hospitals.

– Aggregation of consumption data analysis: this method compares real consumption with

expected consumption based on morbidity data.

– MSF rational prescription monitoring tool: see the Toolbox, MSF Rational prescription

monitoring tool.

– WHO drug use indicators3: these include indicators related to prescribing practices,

patient care, and complementary drug use. They help determine the quality of prescribing
in primary healthcare facilities. Additionally, MSF has introduced the rational prescription
audit score (%) as a component of the quality core indicators that each MSF program is
required to report on. See the Toolbox, MSF Rational prescription monitoring tool.

– ATCc/DDDd methodology: this can be used to:

b For further details on point prevalence surveys, additional resources can be found on specialised platforms

such as the Global-PPS: https://www.global-pps.com/.
c The anatomical therapeutic chemical system is a classification system for active substances and

pharmaceuticals. It categorises drugs according to the organ or system on which they act, as well as their
therapeutic, pharmacological, and chemical properties. The classification system is maintained by the World
Health Organization (WHO).
d As per WHO, defined daily dose (DDD) is defined as “assumed average maintenance dose per day for a drug

used for its main indication in adults”. Additional information and training are available through the MSF
website via the "TEMBO" training platform. https://tembo.msf.org/

Chapter 8: Rational use and monitoring

192

– Examine changes and trends in drug use over time.

– Compare drug use across various healthcare settings, including different regions or

countries.

– Document the relative therapy intensity with various groups of medications.

– Track changes in the use of a drug class.

– Evaluate regulatory and intervention effects on prescribing patterns.

– Other qualitative methods: these can include interviews, focus group discussions, etc.

– Sentinel system:e this surveillance method focuses on a specific list of sentinel medical

products, recommended to monitor a maximum of 20–30 products from the MSL. The
sentinel list may include:

• Essential products for project activities, such as first-line treatments for common or
targeted diseases.

• High consumption products.

• Products with a history of stockout.

• Expensive or high-value products.

• Products vulnerable to deviation, such as those that have not adhered to protocols, have
been overused or underused, or have unexplained inventory discrepancies.

Note

Data from prescriptions can be collected either retrospectively or prospectively from
medical prescriptions, patient records, clinical registers, and morbidity databases.
Prospective data collection and analysis are favoured to allow real-time intervention if
issues with medication prescribing are identified.

e Additional information and training are available through the MSF website via the "Sentinel System for

Medical Items" training on the platform ‘’TEMPO’’. https://tembo.msf.org/

Chapter 8: Rational use and monitoring

193

8

8.4 Rational use of medical products

8.4.1 Rational use of injectable medications

Many patients request injectable treatments, believing they are more effective. Some
prescribers may also feel that the technical aspect of administering injections enhances their
professional credibility. However, injections come with potential risks not typically associated
with oral medications.

A primary concern with intravenous lines is the increased risk of transmitting infectious
diseases such as bacterial infections, hepatitis B, hepatitis C, etc.

Other common problems associated include:

– Inaccurate weight-dependent dosing

– Incorrect reconstitution, which may involve the wrong concentration or unsuitable diluent

– Incorrect administration practices (e.g., not flushing lines or reversing of IV lines)

– Storage issues or inadequate facilities for safe disposal of used injection supplies and

equipment

When oral and injectable medications are equally effective, parenteral administration is
justified in some severe cases or when oral administration is not feasible, such as due to
digestive intolerance or inability to take oral medication. It is advisable to transition from
injectable to oral medications as soon as possible during the treatment course.

Parenteral treatment is generally more expensive than oral treatment due to higher
manufacturing costs (even for the same quantity of the active ingredient), the additional
expenses for disposable materials (vials, syringes, needles), and increased waste associated
with their use.

Considering pharmacokinetic parameters, such as absorption, distribution, metabolism,

and excretion (ADME), is important. To ensure the most suitable formulation is
administered, factors like bioavailability and the patient's condition must be considered.

8.4.2 Rational use of liquid medications

Taking liquid medications, such as syrups or drops, is often easier for certain age groups (e.g.,
young children), especially if they are sweetened or flavoured. However, there are some
concerns associated with liquid medications, including:

– The lack of proper measuring devices can lead to unintentional overdosing or underdosing,

potentially risking the patient's health.

– Inadequate preparation of oral suspensions, such as using non-potable water, can

compromise the safety of oral suspensions (see Chapter 7).

– Misidentification of syrups as cough medications, causing confusion between cough

mixtures and antibacterial suspensions or syrups in many countries.

Some oral suspensions may require refrigeration or have a limited shelf life at room temperature
(depending on their formulation and the manufacturer’s instructions). Improperly stored
syrups could ferment.

Chapter 8: Rational use and monitoring

194

Multidose formulations need specific storage conditions once opened and should be discarded
after a certain period (usually indicated by the manufacturer) to avoid contamination.

The cost of syrups and oral suspensions is considerably higher than that of tablets or capsules.
Even powdered forms for reconstitution can be two to seven times more expensive, primarily
due to the cost of the container and increased transportation costs resulting from the added
weight and volume.

8.4.3 Rational use of antimicrobials

Antimicrobials include antibiotics, antivirals, antifungals, and antiparasitics, which are used
to prevent and treat infections. The emergence and spread of drug-resistant and multidrug-
resistant pathogens threaten the capacity to treat common infections, leading to increased
morbidity, mortality, prolonged hospital stays, and higher healthcare costs.

AWaRe Classification

WHO developed the ACCESS, WATCH and RESEVE (AWaRe) classification to promote
rational use and support antibiotic stewardship efforts. Based on the risk of antimicrobial
resistance and the spectrum of activity, antibiotics are classified into:
• ACCESS antibiotics: these are narrow-spectrum antibiotics that are recommended as
first- or second-choice treatments for common infections. They have a low resistance
potential, a good safety profile, and are affordable.

• WATCH antibiotics: these are broader-spectrum and higher-cost antibiotics
recommended for patients with more severe clinical infections.

• RESERVE antibiotics: these are broad-spectrum antibiotics, to be used as a last-choice
treatment for multidrug-resistant infections.

Watch-and-Reserve antibiotics are highly recommended to correlate with microbiology

access and results4.

Antimicrobial resistance (AMR)5

Antimicrobial resistance (AMR)f is driven by several factors, including the misuse of
antimicrobials, inadequate vaccination, poor infection prevention and control measures,
limited diagnostic ability, lack of prescribing guidelines and surveillance, and lack of awareness
and education.

Addressing AMR is a priority for MSF which uses a three-pillar framework: antimicrobial
stewardship (AMS), infection prevention and control (IPC), and diagnostics and surveillance.

Antimicrobial stewardship (AMS)

AMS aims to ensure the appropriate use of antimicrobials to improve treatment outcomes and
reduce AMR.

AMS programmes can enhance infection cure rates, reduce treatment failures, adverse events,
and antibiotic resistance, and consequently reduce hospitalisation and healthcare costs.

f Antimicrobial resistance (AMR) is the ability of microorganisms (bacteria, viruses, fungi, and parasites) to

adapt and resist the effects of antimicrobials that aim to destroy them or prevent their growth.

Chapter 8: Rational use and monitoring

195

8

A key component of an AMS programme is a functional antimicrobial stewardship committee
(AMSC). The AMSC investigates current antibiotic use, develops and implements action plans
to address potential issues, and regularly monitors and improves antibiotic use and related
activities (IPC, microbiology) within the project. AMSC also promotes the rational use of
antimicrobials at the patient, health facility, and community levels.

The AMSC should include multidisciplinary members from different sectors, including health
facility management, prescribers, pharmacists, nurses, IPC staff, laboratory staff, microbiologists
and infectious disease specialists. The MoH team should also be involved in AMSC activities to
ensure adherence to rational use principles and the programme's sustainability.

An appointed AMR focal point should lead and oversee the AMS programme and AMSC.

Continuous assessment of antibiotic prescribing will help to identify problems with antibiotic
use and potential targets for interventions, which should be adapted to each context.

Role of the pharmacist

Pharmacists are essential members of the AMSC and contribute to various tasks:

– Guarantee supply and access: pharmacists forecast orders for restricted Watch/Reserve

antibiotics, considering the estimated blood culture activity and positivity rates. This
ensures an adequate supply of antimicrobials by implementing standard operating
procedures (SOPs) to manage and restrict supply as necessary.

– Ensure good dispensing practices: pharmacists review prescriptions during dispensing,

and if issues are identified, contact prescribers. In projects where an Antimicrobial
Stewardship (AMS) or clinical pharmacist is involved, they contribute to treatment
decisions, such as switching from intravenous to oral therapy and adjusting doses for
patients with organ dysfunction (e.g., renal impairment), based on patient medical history.
Preauthorisation from a specialist may be required for dispensing certain antibiotics. See
Chapter 7.

– Counsel and advice: pharmacists provide counselling and education to patients and their

caretakers on the correct use of antimicrobials. They also advise prescribers, contributing
to informed decision-making.

– Monitor and report: pharmacists play an important role in rational use evaluations and

quality audits, focusing on rational prescription practices (see Section 8.3). They provide
feedback during AMSC meetings, participate in the creation of monthly and quarterly
reports, and assist in reporting adverse events and medication errors.

– Assure continuous education: pharmacists engage in continuous education on new

antimicrobials, including their selection, dosing regimens, bioavailability, and potential
adverse events. They also provide ongoing education to healthcare staff as needed.

Note

In addition to these tasks, pharmacists help develop and implement context-specific

antibiotic prescribing guidelines, based on local resistance patterns.

Chapter 8: Rational use and monitoring

196

8.4.4 Rational use of medical devices (MD) and in-vitro diagnostics (IVD)

MDs and IVDs are categorised into different classes based on the level of regulatory control
required to ensure their safety and effectiveness. These classes range from low risk to the
most critical. The risk increases according to the duration of contact with the patient’s body
and the degree of invasiveness (see Appendix 23g).

The secondary packaging of sterile products is designed to protect product integrity and
maintain sterility throughout the shelf life. (e.g., silicone gel packets regulate humidity within
the packaging). It is crucial to store them in the manufacturer-provided secondary packaging
until use.

Examples of misuse of MDs are:

– Using an inadequate catheter size, especially in children.

– Failure to properly dispose of products intended for single-patient use and reuse (e.g.,

washable, enteral feeding syringes for a single patient).

– Inappropriately sterilising and reusing single-use products (e.g., manual vacuum aspiration

cannulas).

– Using sterile gloves when not indicated and when examination gloves (non-sterile) are

available.

– Using sterile gauze for procedures when non-sterile gauze would suffice.

Examples of misuse of IVDs are:

– Overusing expensive diagnostic tests reserved for specific conditions (e.g., i-STAT checking

parameters, a handheld device used for rapid blood diagnostics).

– Using dengue and cholera rapid tests for individual diagnostics instead of outbreak

detection.

– Not performing bedside testing grouping before transfusion.

– Not ordering HIV RDTs in proportionate and adequate quantities according to the HIV

testing algorithm, prevalence, and purpose of testing (e.g., donor screening, diagnosis,
etc).

8.4.5 Rational use of nutrition-specialised food

Nutrition-specialised food products (NFOS family) are developed to meet the nutritional needs
of malnourished individuals during crises and in regular projects. These products are fortified
with proteins, minerals, and vitamins tailored to patients' dietary requirements based on age,
malnutrition severity, associated pathologies, and local dietary practices.

Examples of misusing specialised food products include:

– Using without proper indication due to overstock or imminent expiry.

– Inappropriate dilution (concentration) or handling of the product (e.g., lacking IPC

measures during preparation).

– Inappropriate dosing of ready-to-use therapeutic food (RUTF) or therapeutic milk.

g Information regarding the class of medical devices, as well as other relevant details about their characteristics

(material, packaging and labelling, shelf-life, sterilisation and disinfection, cold chain, etc.), is indicated in the
MSF catalogues.

Chapter 8: Rational use and monitoring

197

8

– Using RUTF as a general food aid.

– Prescribing RUTF to all pregnant and breastfeeding women and girls, instead of the correct

target group.

– Providing adults with more than two sachets per day of any specialised food.

Retaining leftovers of specialised food for later use (often observed among caretakers).

8

The medical consequences of drug interactions can vary widely based on their severity,
and in certain cases, the outcomes can be serious or even fatal. Concomitant use is usually
contraindicated in such situations. The risk escalates with the number of medical conditions
and medications a patient is taking.

Medical staff should be familiar with potential drug interactions. Regular review of all
prescriptions, drug labels or package inserts is advised, especially for new drugs.h

8.5.2 Pharmacovigilance

Pharmacovigilance (PV)i refers to the science and actions dedicated to identifying, evaluating,
understanding, and preventing adverse eventsj associated with the use of medications to
improve their safety.

Some adverse events can be attributed to one or more medications and are referred to as
adverse reactions or side effects. Elements to consider when evaluating whether an adverse
event could have been caused by a medication include:

– The timing of the event post-medication intake

– Disappearance of the event upon medication withdrawal

– Reappearance upon reintroduction of the medication

– Known pharmacological mechanisms, including recognised reactions to the medication or

its class

– Supportive evidence, such as laboratory results or absence of other evident causes

Adverse drug events can range in severity from mild to severe, with some being serious or
even fatal. In MSF, an adverse event is considered serious if it has a profound impact on the
patient's health. This includes events that lead to death, are life-threatening, necessitate or
prolong hospitalisation, result in disability or significant incapacity, induce birth defects or
congenital anomalies if taken during pregnancy, or are otherwise medically significant (e.g.,
blood dyscrasias).

Routine pharmacovigilance reporting

Medical staff are responsible for managing, documenting, and reporting any drug-related
adverse events, especially serious cases that may be fatal or life-threatening.

Routine pharmacovigilance at MSF includes the following 6 steps:
1) Identify a PV focal point (usually a pharmacy coordinator) who trains medical staff on

identifying and reporting adverse events.
2) Once a potential adverse drug event is identified, medical staff prioritise clinical management

to stabilise the patient. This includes stopping the suspected medication and, when possible,
providing alternative therapy.
3) The medical staff informs the PV focal point using the MSF PV report form (see Toolbox, PV

report form).
4) The MSF program PV focal point ensures that the report form is filled out correctly and

h Free resources like the FDA website, Medscape, and WebMD can be used to check for drug interactions.
i Further training on Pharmacovigilance available on MSF website for training “TEMBO” https://tembo.msf.org/

course/view.php?id=317
j Adverse events are any new signs, symptoms, laboratory test abnormality or worsening condition, occurring

during or after the use of a medication and which does not necessarily have a causal relationship with that
medication.

Chapter 8: Rational use and monitoring

200

anonymised before transmitting it to the HQ pharmacy referent, who reviews the form
and seeks clarification if needed. The HQ pharmacy referent forwards it to the PV unit in
Genevak and the MSF supply centre pharmacist if internationally procured medications
are involved. Experts (e.g., cardiologists) may be consulted during the review as needed.
Feedback may include follow-up queries, medical recommendations, changes in clinical
guidelines, medication procurement, or medication quarantine or recall.
5) Feedback and decisions on actions to be taken are collected by the HQ pharmacy referent

and communicated back to the MSF program PV focal point, who then informs the reporting
medical team.
6) Further transmission to local PV authorities, the WHO, or other stakeholders is coordinated

by the HQ pharmacy referent, the PV focal point, and the MSF supply centre pharmacist.

Active pharmacovigilance

Some MSF projects engage in active pharmacovigilancel during and after clinical trials, or
specifically for certain products (e.g., antivenom).

Active pharmacovigilance includes a scheduled series of follow-up visits or calls conducted
over a defined period with the objective of characterising known risks or detecting new
suspected adverse reactions. It is performed in specific projects, such as observational studies,
for particular medications like new antibiotics, or within populations of interest (e.g., children).

8.5.3 Medical device vigilance

MD vigilance includes monitoring and addressing unintended events resulting from the use
of MDs to enhance their safety, including corrective actions during the post-marketing phase.

Currently, there is no active MD vigilance in place at MSF. However, unintended events
involving MDs can be reported through the quality complaint form, whether or not they are
associated with a clinical event (e.g., device-related infection, abnormal glucometer reading
without patient harm, allergic reaction to tape). See Chapter 9.

8.5.4 Patient safety incidents

Patient safety incidents (PSIs) encompass any unintended or unexpected events that may
cause harm to a patient. One specific category of PSIs involves medication incidents, which are
related to the drug treatment process.

Medication incidents
A medication incident is an unintended failure in the drug treatment process that leads to or
has the potential to harm the patient.

Medication incidents can occur at various stages, including prescribing, order communication,
product labelling, packaging and nomenclature, distribution, compounding, dispensing,
administration, counselling, monitoring, and use. Medication incidents can involve the

k The PV unit provides support for individual case reviews and follow-ups, while also centralising and analysing

PV data at the intersectional level to detect potential signals.
l Active pharmacovigilance, a vital supplement to spontaneous reporting systems, entails proactive measures

to continuously detect adverse events within a specific population. This approach broadens the scope of
pharmacovigilance beyond passive reporting {Contreras-Salinas, 2021 #17}

Chapter 8: Rational use and monitoring

201

8

medication itself, the patient, the dose, the administration route, or the frequency. They may
arise from a lack of knowledge or planning, or from mistakes in the performance or execution
of a planned action.

Medication incidents leading to adverse events, like unintentional overdoses resulting in renal
failure, should be reported for pharmacovigilance. The process for reporting these incidents
(with or without associated adverse events) varies across MSF sections. Refer to the HQ
pharmacy referent for more guidance.

8.6 Case study

A new medical activity manager (MAM) approaches the project pharmacy manager with a
proposal to add new restricted WATCH antibiotics, such as vancomycin and meropenem, to
the project's medical standard list. The MAM also requests that an order be placed for these
antibiotics.

What would be the general considerations that a pharmacist and MAM should keep in mind?

Practical considerations:

– Understanding needs: engage with the MAM and medical coordinator to assess the

specific medical conditions and patient demographics that these antibiotics will address.
Ensure their use is clinically justified.

– Expert consultation: confirm whether the project has an AMR focal point or a functional

AMSC that has approved the use of these new antibiotics. The medical coordinator should
discuss with HQ for approval before adding them to the MSL and placing the order.

– Infection prevention and control (IPC): ensure a functional IPC program is in place within

the project to minimise infection risks.

– Laboratory: verify that the laboratory can perform necessary culture and sensitivity tests

and ensure that laboratory staff are trained to conduct these tasks.

– Trained staff: ensure medical staff are trained in the appropriate prescription,

administration, monitoring of adverse events, PV reporting, and safe disposal of these new
antibiotics.

– Order preparation: if approved, calculate the order based on estimated blood culture

activity and positivity rates to ensure adequate supply.

– Storage and supply management: guarantee proper storage conditions for the antibiotics

and ensure that there are sufficient stock levels to complete the treatment courses.

– Regular monitoring: develop a clear plan to monitor the appropriate use of antibiotics.

See section 8.3.

– Feedback: share results from monitoring with the medical team and AMSC. If audits and

analyses indicate misuse, collaborate with the AMSC, medical coordination, and relevant
HQ referents to identify areas for improvement and implement necessary actions.

9.1. Introduction

Quality assurance is essential for ensuring the safety and effectiveness of medical products.
It involves maintaining high standards to ensure that products perform as intended and meet
safety requirements.

A quality issue refers to any deviation, defect, or nonconformity that affects the safety, efficacy,
or performance of a product or service. In the context of medical products, it specifically relates
to issues that compromise their intended quality standards or specifications.

Below are some common examples of quality issues:

– Defective medical devices: this includes malfunctions or design flaws in medical

equipment, such as a faulty thermometer or a malfunctioning infusion pump.

– Pharmaceutical contamination: this refers to the presence of foreign substances in

medications, such as microbial contamination in injectable medications.

– Labelling errors: this includes incorrect dosage instructions or missing warnings on

medication packaging.

– Storage condition deviations: this involves exposing thermosensitive products to

temperatures outside the recommended range, affecting their stability and efficacy.

Addressing these quality issues requires a coordinated and structured approach across various
MSF roles and at all levels of the organisation (from MSF supply centres to end-user units).
MSF requests that manufacturers and distributors implement appropriate corrective and
preventive actions (CAPA)a if a non-conformity is linked to their operations. Those who do
not comply may risk not being considered for partnership with MSF. Regular follow-ups are
conducted to ensure the effectiveness of these CAPAs.

The project pharmacy manager serves as the focal point for quality issues within the project.
In the absence of a pharmacy coordinator, the medical coordinator takes on this responsibility.
According to WHO recommendations, all records of quality issues should be systematically
organised and accessible to the focal point (pharmacy coordinator or medical coordinator)
through a centralised database or a physical records system, ensuring timely and efficient
access for review and action.

If an intersectional pharmacist (ISP) is present in the country, they will assist with the
communication and documentation related to quality alerts, quality complaints, and batch
recalls.

a The corrective and preventive actions (CAPA) approach is designed to ensure that, in the event of deviations

or nonconformities from the desired standards for proper storage and distribution practices, appropriate
measures are promptly implemented, and their subsequent monitoring is ensured.

Chapter 9: Management of quality issues

208

9.2. Key processes in quality management

There are three key processes to consider when addressing quality issues: quality complaints,
quality alerts, and batch recalls. Each of these processes plays a crucial role in maintaining the
safety and efficacy of medical products, ensuring that any deviations or defects are swiftly
identified and addressed.

Figure 9.1 - Key processes in quality issues management

• An expression of dissatisfaction related to the characteristics or performance of a medical
product.
• Initiated by users (patient or caretaker), medical staff, pharmacists, etc., and directed to
suppliers or manufacturers (in MSF, most commonly to the MSF supply centres)
• Quality problems should be reported by project medical teams to the pharmacy
coordinator in charge, who must then contact HQ via the MSF quality complaint form.
Quality
complaint

• Information or instructions about a quality defect (defined below) or suspected defect of a
product
• Initiated by a manufacturer (possibly communicated via a local supplier), national
regulatory authority, WHO or MSF (internal quality alert)
• Flows typically down to the MSF programme or project via the HQ pharmacy referent or
MSF supply centre. If project teams become aware of a quality alert via a local supplier,
manufacturer, or national regulatory authority, they should report it to the pharmacy
coordinator, who must inform the HQ pharmacy referent and ISP (if available).
Quality
alert

• Withdrawal of specific batches from the distribution chain to ensure patient safety. It can
result from various reasons related to quality, safety, or efficacy, including product defects,
reports of serious adverse events, or suspicions of falsification.
• Initiated by a manufacturer (possibly communicated via a local supplier), national
regulatory authority, WHO or MSF (internal batch recall).
• Flows typically down to the MSF programme or project via the HQ pharmacy referent or
MSF Supply Centre. If the project team becomes aware of a batch recall via a local
supplier, manufacturer, or national regulatory authority, they should report it to the
pharmacy coordinator, who must inform the HQ pharmacy referent and ISP (if available).

Batch
recall

WHO: World Health Organization ISP: Intersectional Pharmacist HQ: Headquarters

Source: MSF

Quality defect: an issue with a medical product indicating non-compliance with its marketing
authorisation or MSF's internal approval.

For medical devices (MD) and in vitro diagnostics (IVD), a quality defect is defined by European
regulations1 as any issue affecting the performance or characteristics of a device available
on the market. This includes malfunctions, deterioration, use errors due to design flaws,
inadequacies in the manufacturer's information, and undesirable side effects.

Chapter 9: Management of quality issues

209

9

9.3. Handling key quality processes

It is crucial to identify the root cause of quality issues and take appropriate actions. This
includes immediate actions related to the use of the affected medical product, as well as CAPA
to prevent similar problems from occurring in the future (see Chapter 5, Part 1).

9.4.1. Handling quality complaints

All quality complaints are reported to HQ via the MSF medical quality complaint form (see
Toolbox).
At this stage, no systematic quarantineb or destruction of the suspected product(s) has been
decided. However, if there is an obvious risk to the patient, quarantine can be triggered
immediately by the MSF program.

When submitting a quality complaint, please include additional information (appendices) such
as:

– Storage and transport conditions (e.g., data loggers for temperature and humidity records)

– Pictures of the medical product

– Reception checklists

– Any other relevant information

The project medical team, in collaboration with the pharmacy coordinator, should gather and
provide as much information as possible, including any potential adverse events experienced
by patients. The pharmacy coordinator or medical coordinator is encouraged to liaise with the
HQ pharmacy referent for guidance before formally submitting the quality complaint.

Submission depending on the supply source

• If the affected product was procured via any MSF supply centre, the quality complaint
form and accompanying appendices should be submitted to the appropriate MSF supply
centre. The HQ pharmacy referent must also be informed.

• If the affected product was procured locally, including donations or loans from third
parties, the quality complaint form and accompanying appendices should be submitted
to the HQ pharmacy referent, who will guide the next steps. If an ISP is present in the

country, they should also be informed.

Once a quality complaint form is received, the focal point staff member at the HQ level will
review the complaint and inform the MSF program coordination of the outcomes and the next
steps. These may include lifting the quarantine, continuing to use the product (with or without
specific recommendations), or destroying the product. The MSF program should receive
feedback promptly and must inform any other recipients of the affected product (whether
through loans or donations).

b Quarantine is the status of medical products isolated physically or by other effective means while a decision is

pending on their release, rejection or reprocessing.

Chapter 9: Management of quality issues

210

The investigation of quality complaints may result in one of the following findings:

– A confirmed quality issue

– A non-confirmed quality issue. In some cases, it may not be possible to confirm a quality

issue (e.g. due to insufficient information). The problem may also be related to other types
of issues, such as:

• Incorrect use

• Good storage and distribution practices (GSDP)

• A pharmacovigilance suspected adverse event (see Chapter 8).

Even if a quality issue is not confirmed, it is essential to assess the potential risk versus the
benefit of continuing to use the product. Based on this assessment, decisions can be made
regarding continued use, additional monitoring, or alternative actions.

Falsified and substandard medical products

Falsified medical products are those that have been deliberately or fraudulently misrepresented
in terms of their identity, composition or source. Such deliberate or fraudulent misrepresentation
may involve the substitution, adulteration, or reproduction of an authorised product, or the
manufacture of an unauthorised product.

Substandard medical products, also known as "out-of-specification" products, refer to
products that have been authorised by NRAs but fail to meet either national or international
quality standards or specifications – or, in some cases, both.

Both substandard and falsified medical products are frequently manufactured in unsanitary
conditions by unqualified staff members and may contain unknown impurities or bacterial
contamination.

If falsified or substandard medical products are identified or suspected, the pharmacy
coordinator must inform the HQ pharmacy referent using the MSF medical quality complaint
form (see Toolbox). The products must be placed in quarantine as explained above. If the
presence of falsified or substandard products is confirmed, the products must not be
reintroduced into the MSF supply chain or the local market.

In addition to internal reporting, it may be necessary to report these products to external
authorities, such as national authorities. The decision to report to these authorities should
always be coordinated with the HQ pharmacy referent. While MSF prefers to centralise
reporting via internal channels, national regulations may require additional reporting to
government bodies. In some cases, reporting to WHO may also be necessary; this should be
coordinated centrally at the HQ level.

The reception process is of significant importance for identifying potential falsified and

substandard medical products (see Chapter 5, Part 2).

Chapter 9: Management of quality issues

211

9

9.4.2. Handling quality alerts

For MD and IVD, a quality alert corresponds to a field safety notice issued by a manufacturer
outlining corrective actions to prevent or reduce the risk of a serious incident related to a
device on the marketc.

Upon receiving a quality alert, the pharmacy coordinator must:

– Check if the specified product or batch was received, used, lent, or donated.

– Share the alert with all potentially impacted recipients and follow the provided

instructions.

– Gather additional information from the project pharmacy manager, medical team, or end-

users, possibly involving physical checks on the product.

Virtual and physical checks

Tracing batch numbers in the software stock management tool can be complicated,
occasionally requiring manual entry, which may lead to errors (e.g., confusion between
"0" and "O" or "D").

Without a physical check, there is a risk of missing batch recalls, potentially exposing
patients to hazards.

It is crucial to thoroughly check all levels of the supply chain. Physical and virtual


checks should be conducted within the MSF program, including medical

warehouses, distribution pharmacies, end-user units, and other potential stock locations

such as expat residences, vehicle boxes, and first-aid kits.

Note that

– A quality alert does not necessarily require quarantine of the product. Specific action

instructions will be provided on a case-by-case basis.

– If quarantine is required, maintain appropriate storage conditions, as quarantine does not

invariably entail destruction. The quarantine may be lifted, allowing for the subsequent
use of the product.

– Risk-benefit analysis will be performed at the HQ in collaboration with the MSF

international office levels. Outcomes, such as recommendations for continued use,
product destruction, or a batch recall, will be communicated to the MSF program as
needed.

– In case of uncertainty, the pharmacy coordinator should consult with the HQ pharmacy

referent or ISP if available.

9.4.3. Handling batch recalls

The pharmacy coordinator should check if the affected product or batch was received, used,
lent, or donated. They should also review outbound donations and loan certificates to notify
potential recipients of the affected batches and facilitate their proper removal from stock if
requested.

c Based on definitions provided in the European Medical Device Regulation 2017/745

Chapter 9: Management of quality issues

212

A batch recall notification may also request other actions, such as monitoring any patient who
may have already received the product.

If the batch recall is communicated by a local supplier or the national regulatory authority, the
pharmacy coordinator or ISP (if available) should verify with the local supplier or the national
regulatory authority to ensure that the list of affected batches is complete.

It is crucial to ensure that all levels of the supply chain are thoroughly checked. Physical

and virtual checks should be conducted at various supply chain levels within the MSF
program, including medical warehouses, distribution pharmacies, end-user units, and any
other potential stock locations (e.g., expat residences, vehicle boxes, first-aid kits, etc.).

Immediate quarantine

Recalled products or batches must be immediately quarantined, secured, segregated,
and clearly labelled as recalled products.

Product-specific storage conditions should be maintained where possible in a locked
area and clearly labelled as “recalled” or “do not use.” The quarantine status should
also be reflected in the stock management tools.

The pharmacy coordinator should promptly share essential information with the HQ pharmacy
referent as directed in the batch recall communication. This information should include details
suchas the quantity of the impacted batch already consumed or currently in use, stock levels,
and any products expected to be received, as well as confirmation of the implementation of
quarantine measures and the availability of unaffected stock or alternatives.
The pharmacy coordinator should urgently assess the remaining stock of products and place
an order if needed. If there is a risk of a potential stockoutd due to the recall, the pharmacy
coordinator should consult with HQ to identify alternatives.

When destruction of the affected products is necessary:

– The HQ pharmacy referent or the MSF supply centre will provide instructions regarding the

disposal of the product (see Chapter 5, Part 2).

– The destroyed quantities should be reported to:

• The appropriate MSF supply centre, if the product was procured via any MSF supply
centre (with the HQ pharmacy referent in copy).

• The HQ pharmacy referent or ISP, if available, if the product was procured locally.

d Situation in which stock levels for a particular product within the MSL are depleted, making it impossible to

fulfil the operational demands for that product.

9.4. Reporting and handling temperature and
humidity deviations

Exposure to temperatures and/or humidity outside the manufacturer's recommended ranges
may affect the quality of medical products, including their stability, sterility, efficacy, and other
characteristics, ultimately affecting safety and performance.

9.4.1. Reporting deviations

Any deviation in temperature or humidity from the recommended storage conditions should
be reported using the standard MSF section reporting tool, if available.
Affected products must be quarantined and can be reintegrated into the program’s distribution
circuit only after verification confirms they are safe for use.

Table 9.1 – Reporting deviations

centre

Specific to each MSF section

In-country storage
HQ pharmacy referent
Specific to each MSF section

For more details about good storage and distribution practices, see Chapter 5, Part 1.

9.4.2. Handling specific temperature deviations

Cold chain breakdown

It is crucial to immediately report any cold chain breakdown to ensure appropriate

guidance on usability and CAPA. For further guidance on cold chain and breakdown
management, refer to the MSF Cold Chain Management Guideline in the Toolbox.

Nutrition-specialised food products

Temperatures above 25 °C or 30 °C (depending on the product’s recommended storage
temperature)

– While exceeding the upper limit of the specified temperature range does not affect food

safety, it does alter nutritional and organoleptic properties (e.g., decrease in vitamin
content).

– Laboratory analysis may be necessary to verify nutritional properties and establish a new

best-before date (BBD).

– There is no predefined rule for establishing the new BBD, as degradation depends on

exposure duration and temperature peaks. Decisions are made at HQ level on a case-by-
case basise.

e Refer to the HQ pharmacy referent for further guidance.

Chapter 9: Management of quality issues

215

9

Temperatures below 0 °C

– Lipid-based products: temperatures below 0 °C do not pose any risk. The only

consequence may be a change in viscosity, which will normalise again at positive
temperatures. Examples include ready-to-use therapeutic food (peanut paste) and ready-
to-use supplementary food (chickpea paste).

– Powdered products: these products must not be stored below 0 °C. If stored at

temperatures below 0 °C, they must be destroyed. Examples include therapeutic milk
powder, F75, and F100.

Chapter 9: Management of quality issues

216

9.5. Case study

A neonatology nurse identified a recurrent problem while using a medical device:
SINSIVCST24W1 SAFETY IV CATHETER, tip, 24G x 19 mm, wings, IP.

The IV catheter often feels quite blunt when first used, resulting in increased resistance when
inserting it through the patient's skin (despite following the correct procedure). This difficulty
makes cannulation more difficult. If she fails to cannulate the vein successfully on the first
attempt, she must replace the IV catheter and attempt the procedure again with a new one.

Actions taken

– Reporting the problem:

• The nurse reported the problem in detail (as a quality complaint) to the pharmacy
coordinator, including her observations regarding the device’s performance, as
mentioned above.

– Compiling information and quarantining the affected stock:

• The pharmacy coordinator compiled all available information from the medical team
regarding the quality complaint and reported it to HQ via the standard intersectional
quality complaint form.

• As a precaution, the pharmacy coordinator quarantined all the problematic batches
while the problem was investigated. Fortunately, an alternate stock was available for
use in the meantime.

– Escalating the complaint:

• The complaint was referred to the MSF supply centre (as it was a product ordered via
international order). Subsequently, the MSF supply centre escalated the complaint to
the manufacturer for investigation.

– Manufacturer's response:

• The manufacturer investigated and, eventually, acknowledged the presence of a product
defect. MSF was informed that a new product version had been developed in response
to this quality complaint to rectify the issue.

• A credit note for the defective product was issued by the MSF supply centre, and the
MSF program reordered replacement stock.

Chapter 10: Management of medical products during opening and closure phases

219

10

10.1.
Introduction

This chapter provides general guidance on managing medical products during the initiation
and closure phases of an MSF project or programme. It serves as a practical tool to ensure that
no critical steps are overlooked.

It is important to note that the topics in this chapter are not necessarily arranged in chronological
order or by level of importance. Furthermore, the chapter does not differentiate between
emergency and non-emergency projects or programmes.

Chapter 10: Management of medical products during opening and closure phases

220

10.2.
Opening an MSF programme or project

10.2.1.
Understanding the context

– A comprehensive understanding of the regulatory framework governing activities such as

the importation, storage, distribution, administration, disposal of medical products, and
pharmacovigilance is essential. If an MSF section is already established in the country,
maintaining open communication with it can provide valuable insights and facilitate
smoother operations.

– The assessment or initial exploratory mission (Explo) conducted by MSF is a critical phase

in determining the need for intervention. During this phase, gathering preliminary
information on key stakeholders and the regulatory environment is essential for planning
supply chain activities effectively (see Toolbox, Checklist for EXPLO). Early data collection
helps mitigate risks related to import delays, blockages, financial penalties, and potential
reputational damage to MSF.

Assessing MSF’s capacity to import medical products into the country should


be a priority (see Chapter 4).

– The socio-political and security conditions of the country and local region must also be

carefully analysed to anticipate impacts on supply chain activities. Key considerations
include road access safety, the presence of restricted zones, and the identification of
strategic logistics points.

– Mapping other relevant humanitarian stakeholders in the country is crucial. This includes

identifying logistics and health cluster platforms that involve other NGOs or agencies.

10.2.2.
Evaluating needs

– A clear understanding of the medical strategy is essential, including detailed knowledge of

the planned operational activities and the specific characteristics of the target population.

– A medical standard list (MSL) must be developed, considering the potential importation

requirements of the country. Close collaboration between the medical and supply logistics
departments is necessary. See more information in Chapter 1 and Chapter 4.

– Forecasting needs should be based on available data (e.g., morbidity statistics,

consumption data from similar projects, past experiences, feedback from explo, etc.). See
more information in Chapter 2.

– After completing the forecasting process, an analysis must be conducted to estimate the

approximate volume of goods that will need to be stored (see Appendix 9).

– Storage requirements for medical products should be assessed early, with special attention

to products that require specific conditions (e.g., cold-chain products, controlled drugs,
dangerous goods). See more information in Chapter 5, Part 1.

Chapter 10: Management of medical products during opening and closure phases

221

10

In addition to complying with standard GSDP for controlled drugs, it is critical to verify
the country-specific list of controlled drugs. Classifications of controlled drugs can vary
significantly between countries.

Additionally, it is important to determine whether the country imposes any
supplementary regulations. For example, the destruction of ampoules or vials for

controlled drugs may require authorisation by national authorities in some countries.

10.2.3.
Managing stock and planning demand

– Determine the stock positioning strategy (see Chapter 5, Part 2) based on factors such

as the socio-political context, available supply chain routes, project locations, product
characteristics and overall supply chain objectives.

– Establish a demand planning process and collaboratively develop the order chronogram

with input from both medical and supply teams (see Chapter 2).

– Plan the implementation of stock management tools (e.g., software, stock cards, etc.) and

ensure staff training is scheduled well in advance to promote smooth operations.

– Ensure a robust traceability system is established throughout the supply chain, alongside a

system for reporting and addressing quality issues (see Chapter 9).

– Develop reporting and performance measurement systems from the outset to monitor the

efficiency of the supply chain.

– If prepositioned stocks (e.g., EPREP) are required, see Chapter 5, Part 2.

– Do not overlook the management of expired and damaged products.

Selection of premises

Choosing premises for storing medical products must be carefully considered based
on several critical factors, such as staff security, adaptability to comply with Good
Storage and Distribution Practices (GSDP), sufficient space to meet anticipated demand,
accessibility, and proximity to distribution points.

See more information in Chapter 5, Part 1.

10.2.4.
Human resources

– Anticipate and plan for human resource needs, ensuring that roles and responsibilities are

clearly defined and fully understood by all teams involved.

– Assess the skills and capacities of potential locally hired staff. It is important to understand

the qualifications and availability of the local workforce in the job market.

– All staff involved in the storage and distribution of medical products, regardless of

their background, should undergo tailored GSDP training specific to their roles and
responsibilities (see Chapter 5, Part 1).

– Verify whether national regulations require specific qualifications or training for certain

staff members (e.g., nationally registered pharmacists).

Chapter 10: Management of medical products during opening and closure phases

222

10.3.
Closing MSF Programmes or projects

This section provides guidance for closing MSF programmes or projects. It applies to planned
closures, including those undertaken under less-than-ideal circumstances or with short notice.

Once the decision to close a programme or project has been made, the MSF programme,
in conjunction with the HQ cell/desk, must agree on a strategy. This strategy should outline
clear targets and a comprehensive timeline for the entire process, including preparations for
a possible handover.

– This information should be communicated to all relevant departments within the MSF

programme or project.

– Roles and responsibilities must be clearly delineated and thoroughly understood by all

teams involved.

– If new arrangements or a handover process have been established between MSF and MSF

partners, the time and nature of the transfer of responsibilities and obligations should be
communicated to the concerned departments.

10.3.1.
Managing stock

– At the end of the closure process, the stock of all medical products must be fully depleted,

reaching zero in both theoreticala and physical stockb.

– Analyse the medical stock expected to remain unused by the closure date and perform a

complete physical inventory to ensure data accuracy for this analysis (see Chapter 5, Part
2).

– Determine if further replenishment is required (e.g., to fulfil the handover agreements

with the Ministry of Health) before the closure date. Assess if the order chronogram needs
adjustment and verify the status of backorders to determine if cancellations are possible.

– The medical department must assess and plan the appropriate actions for the medical

stock that will not be used once the closure is finalised. Donations may be made to
coordination teams, other MSF projects within or outside the country, Ministry of Health
structures or programmes, other NGOs, etc.

Donations must comply with the recommendations outlined in Chapter 5, Part 2. For

example, the medical products donated must be pertinent and beneficial to the
recipient and have a minimum remaining shelf life of six months.

– Medical equipment that is damaged or excessively worn should be either repaired or

disposed of before any donation. Equipment in use or stock should be categorised into
four groups: functional, requiring repairs, designated for disposal, and not belonging to
MSF. See more recommendations regarding donations of medical equipment in Chapter 5,
Part 2.

– The elimination of expired and damaged products must be conducted before the closure,

a Theoretical stock is the number of units that is recorded in a stock management tool. It represents the expected

stock level based on previous physical counts and stock movements.
b Physical stock or actual stock is the number of units of a product that are physically counted or verified during

a physical count.

Chapter 10: Management of medical products during opening and closure phases

223

10

in accordance with the MSF procedures and national regulations. If elimination is not
feasible during a project closure, the stock must be transferred to the coordination teamc.
For detailed guidance on waste management practices and destruction methods, refer to
the MSF Health-Related Hazardous Waste Management within LMIC guide (see Toolbox).

If the medical products cannot be donated, possible actions should be discussed


with HQ on a case-by-case basis. Returning medical products to HQ via hand

luggage (hand carry) must be avoided at all times.

10.3.2.
Managing premises

– Regardless of who owns the premises, it is important to ensure that all relevant

stakeholders (e.g., building owner, third-party logistics provider, license holder) are
informed promptly.

– If applicable, review the contractual agreement to verify any clauses regarding the

condition in which the premises must be returned.

– Decide on the appropriate handling of equipment such as shelves, racks, and cold chain

equipment, ensuring they are either transferred, donated, or disposed of responsibly.

10.3.3.
Transport and customs

– Conduct a self-review of all freight files to identify which ones are fully closed with

archived documents and which are missing or pending. Based on this review, develop a
corrective plan.

– Verify in advance whether any temporary importation files have not been closed at the

customs level.

– Confirm a decision has been made to re-export (donate) any medical product to another

country. In such cases, it is essential to anticipate the preclearance process required by the
importing country. Payment of transportation costs should be agreed upon between the
recipient and the supplier. For more details, see Chapter 4.

10.3.4.
Managing information

When closing an MSF programme or project, it is crucial to preserve institutional memory and
document key information to support potential future MSF interventions—whether by the
same MSF section or another.

– Handle all Information, whether paper-based, digital or in another format, responsibly,

with strict adherence to confidentiality.

– Pay special attention to the protection of personal data involved in the management of

medical products. This includes health data (e.g., patient information in prescriptions
at dispensariesd) and supply-related data (e.g., supplier contact details, such as email
addresses, telephone numbers, etc.). Refer to the MSF Health Data Protection Policy (see

c Ensuring compliance with national regulations, where applicable.
d Dispensary refers to an end-user unit whose primary function is medication dispensing. It differs from other

end-user units where dispensing occurs but is not the primary function (e.g., emergency rooms, maternity
units, etc.).

Chapter 10: Management of medical products during opening and closure phases

224

Toolbox) for more information.

Document retention (archiving)

– In most cases, retaining electronic versions of documents is sufficient. For critical

documents, both paper and electronic copies may be preserved. The list of documents to
be retained and their retention duration should be determined by each MSF sectione and
any applicable national regulations.

– The transfer of physical files should be carefully organised, with boxes clearly labelled and

accompanied by detailed packing lists.

– In the event of a project closure, while maintaining the coordination team, archives should

be sent to the coordination location for further decision or safekeeping. Proper storage of
these documents must be planned by the coordination team.

– Prior to sending documents to HQ, verify the correct address, as they may be stored in an

external storage facility.

– A backup must be ensured for essential electronic archives, such as the latest physical

inventory, stock movements, and more. If the project is being handed over, digital
consumption data should be shared to facilitate future demand planning.

Document disposal

– All documents requiring destruction, whether physical or electronic, must be handled in a

way that ensures confidentiality.

– Devices being donated or sold must contain no information related to MSF activities,

patients, or employees.

– Coordinate with designated information and communication technology (ICT) staff for

proper management of digital information.

– Conduct data wiping under the supervision of a designated ICT staff member within the

MSF programme to ensure thorough and responsible deletion.

10.3.5.
Reporting

– The process for reporting a closure should be determined by each MSF section, in line with

its specific requirements and procedures.

– A template for the closure report should be provided by HQ to ensure consistency and

completeness.

– The closure report must be well-written, comprehensive, and realistic. It should address

three key objectives: accountability, preservation of institutional memory, and providing
value for potential future re-engagement.

e Consult HQ for guidance on retention policies.

Medical products procured from local suppliers often result in limited visibility into

the supply chain. This lack of transparency introduces additional risks, such as the
presence of falsifiedb or substandardc products. These risks are particularly concerning in
countries where regulatory oversight of the market is insufficiently stringent.

Appendix 4. LMA process

The LMA process requires an interdisciplinary approach involving staff members from various
departments (medical and supply/log) at multiple levels (projects, MSF programs, HQ, etc.).
Effective communication and robust organisation are imperative.

– LMA submission request

• When an MSF program requires an LMA for medical products, a detailed request form
must be submitted to the corresponding Section Pharmacist (SP). See Appendix 5.

• The request should include a comprehensive description of the needs, along with clear
justifications and the primary objectives anticipated for the LMA. This information will
outline the scope of the possible LMA.

• The request must be submitted by the pharmacy coordinator and must be endorsed by
the corresponding HQ cell/desk prior to submission.

If multiple MSF sections are present in the country, the request form should be completed
collaboratively by all the MSF programs requesting the LMA. One MSF section should take
the lead in coordinating the process. The request should then be submitted to the SPs of the
respective MSF sections.

– LMA approval

• The request will be evaluated by the corresponding SP on the criteria outlined in Section
3.4.4. Following this evaluation, the SP will formally approve or reject the request. Once
approved, the SP will also identify the pharmacist responsible for conducting the LMA.

• The decision will be communicated to the HQ cell/desk and the MSF program(s) by the
respective SP.

If multiple MSF sections are present in the country, the request will be evaluated within the
Pharmacy Quality (PQ) platformd. In such cases, all involved SPs must reach a consensus on
the decision.

– LMA preparation

• Collecting required information: The MSF program(s) must gather the required
information to conduct the LMA to satisfy the objectives defined in a terms of reference
(TOR).

▹The MSL of the MSF programs requesting the LMA must be defined, or a list of

products requiring local purchase should be created.

▹The pharmacy coordinator should collect information about national regulations,

compile a list of products registered in the country, etc.

▹If assessing local suppliers or manufacturers is set as an LMA objective, the

procurement team, in close collaboration with the pharmacy coordinator, should
create a list of potential suppliers and manufacturers to be visited. This list should be
accompanied by the corresponding address, contact details, product catalogues, and
other relevant information.

d Formerly known as the Section Pharmacist platform, the Quality Pharmacy platform is an intersectional

medical platform whose main objective is to ensure quality throughout the entire supply chain, ultimately
guaranteeing patient access to quality‐assured medical products.

Appendix 4

231

The procurement teams of the MSF programs requesting the LMA must ensure

that the listed suppliers or manufacturers comply with all applicable procurement
requirements, including legal and commercial conditions.

• Scheduling: once all the information has been gathered, the procurement team of
the MSF program will coordinate with the pharmacy coordinator and the pharmacist
conducting the LMA to develop a schedule that addresses all the objectives of the
LMA. The procurement team will assist in arranging meetings with the local suppliers
and manufacturers, and if required, the pharmacy coordinator will also help arrange
meetings with national authorities.

This step is completed once the pharmacist responsible for conducting the LMA has confirmed
that all required information has been gathered and has agreed with the MSF program(s) on
the scheduling of the LMA.

– Conducting the LMA

• The pharmacist responsible for conducting the LMA will carry out all visits and
assessments within the defined timeline, as outlined in the TOR.

– Post-LMA

• The pharmacist who conducted the LMA will prepare a report that includes a detailed
analysis and recommendations, which must be validated by the respective SP. Upon SP
validation, the report will be subsequently shared with the MSF program(s) present in
the country.

• The MSF program(s) requesting the LMA will implement the recommendations outlined
in the report. The MSF program(s) must make every effort to maintain commercial
relationships with local suppliers and manufacturers when relevant.

Example of ToR template for LMA

Appendix 6

235

Appendix 6. Import permit for controlled drugs

Legislation governing the export and import of precursors and controlled drugs, such as
narcotics and psychotropics, can vary significantly between countries. In some countries,
these products may be subject to additional controls, beyond those applied by the national
regulatory authorities (NRA) or other national authorities. For instance, in some countries,
certain products may be classified as chemical weapons by the Office of National Security.

– The import permit is usually issued by the relevant national authority (usually the

Department of Narcotics of the MoH or NRA).

– The importation of controlled drugs by humanitarian agencies is prohibited in some

countries. Import may only be allowed if there is a stockout in the national supply
program, in which case an exceptional import request would be required.

– For certain countries, it is also necessary to provide the name and address of the

manufacturer.

– In certain countries, it is necessary to obtain, in addition to the general import permit, a

special import permit (SIP) for regulated products, such as controlled drugs. Additional
documentation may also be requested.

– Normally, a scanned copy of the import permit can be sent to the MSF supply centre to

initiate the export permit application process. In some countries, the hard copy of the
permit must be returned after the export permit is obtained.

The exporter (typically the MSF supply centres) can usually obtain an export permit only upon
the presentation of an import permit from the country of destination. The import permit may
be utilised only once by the MSF supply centre.

The import permit should be sent directly to the MSF supply centre, not to the

national authority.

Information on an import permit for controlled drugs

– Permit number

– Date of issue

– The expiry date of the permit: This should be at least three to four months from the

date of issue, by which time the importation must take place. Please note that in some
countries, it is possible to extend the validity of the import permit.

– Name and address of the importer: only the name of “Médecins Sans Frontières” should

be mentioned, rather than the name of the medical coordinator. This is to avoid the need
for the MSF supply centre to apply for a new address with the relevant European Ministry
of Health in case the coordinator changes, which could delay the supply process.

– Name and address of the exporter: to be verified with the MSF supply centre.

– Number of units (e.g., ampoules, vials, capsules, tablets, oral drops, oral solutions,

patches, or rectal tubes), specified in both letters and numbers, with detailed information
on volume and dosage.

– Name and signature of the authority issuing the permit, preferably on headed paper if

possible.

Appendix 6

236

– Total content of active ingredient (in mg or g): this should be correctly calculated. For

example, one hundred (100) ampoules of fentanyl, 0.05 mg/mL, 2 mL, is equivalent to
10 mg.

– In certain countries, it is also required to specify the base content of the drugs. For

example, if the product is a salt (e.g., morphine hydrochloride), it must be converted to its
base form. For instance, 40 vials of morphine hydrochloride at a concentration of
10 mg/mL, where 1 ml contains 400 mg of morphine hydrochloride, would equate to
40 x 7.6 mg = 304 mg of morphine base. A calculation template for the anhydrous base of
controlled drugs is available on the websites of MSF Supply and MSF Logistique.

Appendix 7. Commonly required documents for
importing medical products

This appendix provides a brief explanation of the most commonly required documents for
importing medical products.

Please note that providing new documentation without prior verification and

consultation, even when requested by national authorities, may set harmful

precedents for other MSF sections, MSF supply centres, and other NGOs.

Any new documentation requested by national authorities that is not included in the

Country Constraint Sheet must first be submitted to the respective MSF supply centres

for verification and validation. The submission should clearly specify the entity

requesting the new document, as well as the rationale for the request.

b Medical warehouse: it works as a pharmaceutical wholesaler. It manages the distribution of medical products at scale.

c Distribution pharmacy: a healthcare service that encompasses the selection, preparation, storage, and distribution of medicines and other medical products. Additionally, it

offers guidance on the safe, effective, and efficient use of these products.

d Hospital pharmacy: a variant of distribution pharmacy. Located in hospitals or clinics, it supplies inpatient departments (IPD) and end-user units for outpatient departments

(OPD).

e End-user unit: represents the final storage point before medical products are dispensed or used. The medical team holds full management responsibility, including order

placements, inventory control, and consumption monitoring. Examples of end-user units include: inpatient wards, operating theatres, emergency rooms, outpatient dispensing

pharmacies, feeding centres, mobile clinics, and vaccination campaign activities.

f Good dispensing practices.

g It depends on each MSF Section and/or MSF program.

Appendix 9

Appendix 9. Premises size estimation

Key factors to consider include:

– Operational needs: medical programmes with substantial patient numbers and high

activity levels typically require larger quantities and a wider variety of medical products.

– Stock positioning strategy (see Chapter 5, Section 5.9, Stock positioning strategies) and

replenishment orders frequency: both will determine the required volume to store.

– Type of medical products to store: the nature of the products will determine the required

storage conditions and storage capacity. For example, products like infusions or nutrition-
specialised food significantly increase storage space needs.

Sizing tools
There are stock-sizing software applications that can accurately estimate the required storage
capacity based on the average volume of products. Refer to HQ for more information.

Rule of thirds
The rule of thirds is a practical approach for estimating the required storage capacity of the
premises. The available space is divided into three equal parts (see figure 1):

– One-third for storage.

– One-third for operations (e.g., reception, picking, packing, etc.).

– One-third for staff movement (e.g., aisles and open areas for safety).

While this rule provides a helpful baseline, adjustments should be made to meet specific
operational requirements.

Figure 1- Rule of thirds

One-third for storage
One-third for operations
One-third for staff
movement and
ventilation.

To estimate space needs, divide the floor area into thirds:

Source: MSF

quality

withstand environmental factors.

Extended storage can degrade the
product and packaging.

conduct regular physical inventories,
etc.

Transfer
Moving products can cause leaks or
static electricity build-up.

Designate specific areas for
transferring, minimise vapo/dust
generation, and reduce static risks.
