Note

– The classification provided follows the EU classification system for medical devices. Please

note that the US classification system differs, and there are separate classification systems
for in-vitro diagnostics (IVDs) in both the EU and the US.

– For a comprehensive overview of medical device classifications, refer to the MSF

catalogues.

1

Glossary

ACCESS antibiotics: narrow-spectrum, recommended as first- or second-choice treatment
for common infections.

Active pharmacovigilance: proactive measures to continuously detect adverse events
within a specific population, thereby broadening the scope of pharmacovigilance beyond
passive reporting.

Active product: products holding a Unidata lifecycle status as either valid, outdated, or
discontinued.

Actual stock: physical quantity that is present at a given time. It’s the number of units of
a product that are physically counted or verified during a physical counting.

Adherence: the degree to which a person’s behaviour corresponds with the agreed
recommendations from a health care provider.

Adjusted average monthly consumption (AAMC): a refined version of the AMC that
excludes potential anomalies, such as stockouts, encoding errors and, other significant
situations in consumption patterns (e.g., higher consumption of a product used as a
substitute when another product is in stockout).

Adverse event: any new signs, symptoms, laboratory test abnormality or worsening
condition, occurring during or after the use of a medication and which does not
necessarily have a causal relationship with that medication.

Anatomical therapeutic chemical system: a classification system for active substances
and pharmaceuticals. It categorises drugs according to the organ or system on which
they act and their therapeutic, pharmacological, and chemical properties.

Antimicrobial resistance (AMR): the ability of microorganisms (bacteria, viruses, fungi,
and parasites) to adapt and resist the effects of antimicrobials that aim to destroy them
or prevent their growth.

Archiving: long-term storage and preservation of documents that are no longer actively
used but must be retained for historical, legal, or regulatory purposes.

Average monthly consumption (AMC): metric that calculates the average quantity of a
product consumed over a specific period, typically measured in months.

Backorders: either the goods that still must be received from orders that have been
partially received, or the goods that have not been received after the confirmed delivery
date.

Batch recall: withdrawal of specific batch(es) from the distribution chain to ensure patient
safety. It can result from various reasons related to quality, safety, or efficacy, including
product defects, reports of serious adverse events, or suspicions of falsification.

Borrow: practice where products are temporarily loaned from a third party (IN from
borrower’s perspective), where the products are expected to be returned (OUT from
borrower’s perspective) after a specific period.

CAPA: the corrective and preventive actions (CAPA) approach is designed to ensure that in
the event of deviations or nonconformities from the desired standards for proper storage and
distribution practices, appropriate measures are promptly implemented, and their subsequent
monitoring is ensured.

Compounding: preparation of a therapeutic product for an individual patient in response to an
identified need.

Consumable: products intended for regular use, for which a forecasted monthly consumption
(FMC) is defined in the forecasting process.

Consumption: demand and utilisation patterns of medical products. Consumption data is used to
calculate a fundamental metric like AMC (average monthly consumption).

Controlled drugs: drug or other substance that is tightly controlled by the government because
it may be abused or cause addiction. The control applies to the way the substance is made, used,
handled, stored, and distributed. Controlled drugs include opioids, stimulants, depressants,
hallucinogens, and anabolic steroids. Controlled drugs can differ from one country to another
according to national legislation.

Country constraint sheet (CCS): a set of requirements negotiated and agreed upon with the
national authorities, including any verbal agreements.

Country's lingua franca: a language that is adopted as a common language between speakers
whose native languages are different.

Coverage period (CP): coverage period refers to the time period that should cover the needs
between two consecutive order receptions. In end-user units, it is normally expressed in weeks.

Cross-contamination: unintended transfer of chemical or microbiological contaminants that can
compromise the quality of a medical product during storage, handling, or transportation.

CSP GeoApp: a dynamic Geographic Information System (GIS) designed to enhance inter-
departmental (legal, supply, and pharmacy) and intersectional collaboration regarding critical
information-related importation requirements.

Customs broker: a licensed professional or firm that facilitates the import and export of goods
on behalf of clients by ensuring compliance with customs regulations. They handle various tasks,
including preparing and submitting necessary documentation, calculating duties and taxes, and
communicating with customs authorities.

Customs clearance: process of getting goods officially authorised by customs authorities to enter
or leave a country. It involves the submission of required documentation, as well as the payment
of duties, taxes, and fees to the customs authorities.

Dangerous goods: substances that pose potential risks to staff, property, or the environment due
to their hazardous chemical or physical properties. They are usually classified with reference to
their immediate risk (UN code).

Deconditioning: removing a medical product from its original packaging.

Defined daily dose (DDD): assumed average maintenance dose per day for a drug used for its
main indication in adults.

Delivery: it typically involves moving products between storage and distribution units within an
MSF project, as well as moving products to a third party (MSF or external). It may or may not
require transportation.

Glossary

261

1

Desirable product: least critical product; a stockout would not significantly impact medical
activities.

Deviation: refers to any non-compliance with procedures, specifications, or regulatory
requirements. Deviations are typically identified and documented as part of a quality
management system. They require investigation and risk assessment to determine a level of
risk that reflects the impact of the product on the patient. In most cases, deviations can still be
addressed through a quality assurance process. While this ensures the product's quality and
safety are assessed, it may not fully meet the required quality standards.

Direct delivery: process where products or goods are shipped directly from the manufacturer
or the supplier to the final destination (MSF program), bypassing intermediary storage facilities
like MSF supply centres.

Discrepancy (physical inventory): a situation where the amount, expiry date or batch number
of physical stocks of a specific product differs from its current theoretical stock.

Dispensary: end-user unit whose primary function is medication dispensing. It differs from
other end-user units where dispensing occurs but is not the primary function (e.g., emergency
room, maternity, etc.).

Dispensing: process in which a qualified healthcare provider prepares and gives medication
to a named patient based on a medical prescription, providing the necessary counselling and
advice, and ensuring proper medication use.

Distribution: it refers to the activities associated with the movements of medical products from
the supplier (point of origin) to the intended recipients. Distribution encompasses activities
such as in-country transport, deliveries and transfer of stock. Distribution does not include
dispensing activities.

Distribution pharmacy: storage and distribution unit that works as a healthcare service that
encompasses the selection, preparation, storage, and distribution of medicines and other
medical products. Additionally, it offers guidance on the safe, effective, and efficient use of
these products.

Documentation: collection of procedures, records, and data, whether in paper or electronic
form.

Donation: a donation refers to the act of giving medical products to a third party without
expecting anything in return. Depending on the donation type, the AMC calculation could be
affected.

Dual signing: a control measure used to enhance the security. Two staff members are required
to verify and sign off certain activities.

Emergency use listing procedure (EUL): a risk-based procedure for assessing and listing
unlicensed vaccines, therapeutics, and in vitro diagnostics with the ultimate aim of expediting
the availability of these products to people affected by a public health emergency.

End-user unit: represents the final storage point before medical products are dispensed or
used.

EPREP (Emergency Preparedness): one of the components of the Disaster Management
Cycle that consists of all the actions taken before an emergency to facilitate the response and
promote readiness.

Glossary

262

EPREP Stock: stock strategically prepositioned and available for the rapid response of
emergencies. It typically includes prepositioned medical and logistical supplies selected to
cover immediate needs during sudden-onset crises (e.g., outbreaks, natural disasters, or
conflict situations).

Essential product: a critical product for the continuation of core medical activities. A short-
term stockout would not halt activities.

Expected lifespan: period during which a device is expected to function reliably from the start
of its initial use.

Expiry date: date placed on the container or labels of a medical product designating the time
during which it is expected to remain within established shelf-life specifications if stored under
defined conditions, and after which it should not be used.

Falsified medical products: a product that has been deliberately and/or fraudulently
misrepresented as to its identity, composition or source. Such deliberate/fraudulent
misrepresentation refers to any substitution, adulteration or reproduction of an authorised
product, or the manufacture of a product that is not an authorised product.

Fire data sheet (FDS): technical document of a product or material that typically focuses
specifically on fire-related hazards, properties, and extinguishing methods.

Flow management: systematic coordination and optimisation of the movement of medical
products and activities within the premises.

Forecasted monthly consumption (FMC): quantity of a product estimated to be consumed
during an upcoming period. It is a predictive metric based on historical consumption data,
trends, and anticipated changes in demand (medical activities).

Forecasting: process of estimating the number and quantity of products that will be consumed
or distributed based on future planned activities.

GSDP (Good Storage and Distribution Practices): a set of guidelines and standards aimed at
ensuring the proper storage, handling, and transportation of medical products in a manner
that ensures products maintain their quality, safety, and efficacy throughout the supply chain,
from manufacturers to end-users.

Hospital pharmacy: a variant of distribution pharmacy. Located in hospitals or clinics, it supplies
to in-patient departments (IPD) and end-user units for outpatient departments (OPD).

Host country agreement (HCA): a formal agreement based on international law, providing for
privileges and immunities similar to diplomatic missions, signed by the Ministry of Foreign
Affairs (MoFA) on behalf of the government for the benefit of all MSF sections present or not
in the country.

Humidity (relative humidity): the ratio of the partial pressure of water vapor in the air to the
saturation vapor pressure at a given temperature.

Import for export (ImpEx): process where goods are imported into a country with the intention
of exporting them to another country.

Import permit: an authorisation required before goods can be imported into a country. This
permit must be obtained from relevant regulatory authorities before the importation takes
place.

Glossary

263

1

In-country transport: it refers to the transportation of medical products within the borders of
a specific country.

In-kind donation: provision, free of charge or below production cost, of medical products and
health technologies by a commercial entity or its affiliated non-profit organisations. In-kind
donations are overseen by MSF Operational Directorates and not by MSF programs.

Indicator: specific metric that provides insights into aspects of performance. They indicate
how well certain processes or activities are performing and often point to trends or patterns.

International Commercial Terms (Incoterms): set of standardised trade terms published by
the International Chamber of Commerce (ICC) that define the responsibilities of receivers
(buyers) and senders (sellers) in international trade transactions, particularly regarding the
delivery of goods. In the context of customs clearance, Incoterms are crucial because they
determine who is responsible for specific aspects of the shipment, including the payment of
duties, taxes, and customs clearance.

Irregular donation: a one-time donation that was not originally anticipated but it is not
associated with any issue in demand planning.

Keyholder log: a formal record used to document the management and accountability of
physical keys for accessing restricted areas.

KPI: subset of indicators that are considered critical. KPIs measure performance based on
established strategic goal or objectives.

Lead time: the time span from the stock date to the reception of the order has been completed,
and the order status is marked as closed.

Loan: act of providing medical products to a third party under a contractual agreement (e.g.,
loan certificate) where the products are expected to be returned after a specified period.

Local supplier: in the context of quality assurance, a commercial entity to which MSF places an
order, and which is responsible for organising the delivery of products to MSF. A local supplier
may be an exclusive distributor, an importer, a wholesaler, a national procurement centre,
rarely, a retail pharmacy (for staff health purposes). A manufacturer may also act as a local
supplier in the case of direct procurement.

Loss: any unintentional or undesirable reduction in the quantity of product in stock. Losses
need to be identified and addressed to maintain accurate stock levels. Losses can result from
product damage (including product damage caused by cold chain breakdowns), product
expiration, unplanned donations, theft, or products being declared lost.

Medical products: in the context of this guideline, it includes medicines, medical devices
(including medical equipment), in vitro diagnostics, and nutrition specialised food.

Medical warehouse: storage and distribution unit that works as a pharmaceutical wholesaler.
It manages the distribution of medical products on a large scale.

Memorandum of understanding (MoU): a formal agreement between two or more parties
outlining the terms and details of a mutual understanding or agreement, framing the conditions
of MSF activities. MOUs are signed (1) at the local level with a representative of the Ministry of
Health (MOH) or of a local organisation to fix the conditions of a project/partnership (Project
MOU), or (2) at the governmental level with the MOH or the Line Ministry of NGOs to fix the
conditions of MSF presence/action in a country (Framework MOU).

Glossary

264

Metric: numerical value used to describe a context or situation. They provide the foundational
data for measuring performance. Metrics are used to calculate indicators.

Mixed supply: it refers to a setup in which the supply chain for medical products is not entirely
under the responsibility of MSF. Mixed supply entails a shared responsibility in the management
of the supply chain between MSF and a third party.

Mix-up: error during product picking, such as collecting the wrong product.

MML: list of products validated by the medical direction for procurement and use within the
MSF section.

Module: a module is a set of products which is either a part of a kit or the optional complement
to a kit.

MSF Program: in this guideline, "MSF Program" is used to refer to what is commonly known as
an “MSF Mission”, referring to the overall set of medical and operational activities implemented
by MSF in a given country or context.

MSL: list of medical products that are necessary for the optimal running of the medical
programs in the field, validated for procurement and use, and to be kept available at all times.

National essential medicines list (NEML): an official list adapted from the World Health
Organisation (WHO) Model List of Essential Medicines, and established by national health
authorities, defining the minimum set of medicines considered essential to meet the priority
health needs of the population within a given health-care system.

National Regulatory Authority (NRA): also known as National Medicines Regulatory Authority
(NMRA) or National Drug Regulatory Authority (NDRA), it is the national body responsible for
regulating and overseeing the quality, safety, and efficacy of medical products within a country.

Non-conformity: deviation from a specification, a standard, or an expectation. It potentially
poses a risk to the quality of the medical item, therefore, the safety and efficacy of the item.

Non-consumable: products not typically used on a regular basis, for which a maximum stock
level is established in the forecasting process.

Order closed: an order is considered closed once it has been verified and confirmed that all
the products specified in the medical order have been received at the destination defined by
the MSF program. An order may be delivered in several shipments over a period and will only
be considered closed once all products have been received at the final destination.

Overstock: situation where the quantity of products in stock significantly exceeds the expected
consumption within a defined period, resulting in an excessive amount of stock.

Packing: preparing medical products for shipment, such as placing them into containers or
boxes.

Paper stock card: a physical stock management tool used to track stock levels and movements
of individual products. It is typically a card or sheet of paper where essential information about
product stock movements, including physical counts, is recorded manually.

Passive cold chain: containers or packaging solutions that do not require external power sources
or active cooling mechanisms to maintain the required temperature range for transporting
temperature-sensitive products.

Pest control: management and prevention of pests that can infest and damage stored products.

Glossary

265

1

Pharmacovigilance (PV): the science and actions dedicated to identifying, evaluating,
understanding, and preventing adverse events associated with the use of medications to
improve their safety.

Pharmacy Coordinator: in this guideline, the term "Pharmacy Coordinator" is used as a
generic designation for pharmacists working in a coordination role, whether their official title
is Mission Pharmacist, Country Pharmacist or Coordinator Pharmacist.

Physical counting: process of manually counting medical products in stock to verify actual
stock levels. It involves recording specific product information such as expiry dates and/or
batch numbers.

Physical inventory: process of manually counting and verifying all products in stock to ensure
that the counted stock (actual stock) matches the recorded data in stock management tools
(theoretical stock).

Physical stock: number of units of a product that are physically counted or verified during a
physical counting.

Picking: process of locating, collecting, and verifying specific products from storage locations
to fulfil order requests.

Picking ticket list: a document or digital record to guide staff during the picking process. It
normally contains information such as products to be picked, quantities required, expiry date,
batch number (if applicable).

Precursors: chemicals frequently used in the illicit manufacture of narcotic drugs and
psychotropic substances and are under international control in accordance with the United
Nations Convention against Illicit Traffic in Narcotic Drugs and Psychotropic Substances of 1988.

Prepositioned stock: stocks are strategically stored in specific locations in advance of
anticipated demand or potential emergencies.

Primary packaging: packaging that directly encloses the medical product, in direct contact
with it.

Quality alert: information or instructions about a quality defect or suspected defect of a
product.

Quality complaint: an expression of dissatisfaction related to the characteristics or performance
of a medical product.

Quality defect: an issue with a medical product indicating non-compliance with its marketing
authorisation or MSF's internal approval.

Quality issue: any deviation, defect, or nonconformity that affects the safety, efficacy, or
performance of a product or service. In the context of medical products, it specifically relates
to issues that compromise their intended quality standards or specifications.

Quarantine: the status of medical products isolated physically or by other effective means
while a decision is pending on their release, rejection or reprocessing.

Real consumption: quantity of a medical product that is used for or given to patients. (e.g.,
when a hospital pharmacy dispenses medications directly to patients or when medical products
are utilised in end-user units).

Regular donation: a pre-arranged donation that occurs at fixed intervals.

Glossary

266

Relabelling: updating or rewriting the product’s information on its package.

Repackaging: unpacking medical products from their original packaging (deconditioning) and
repacking them into new or different packaging.

RESERVE antibiotics: broad-spectrum, to be used as a last-choice treatment for multidrug-
resistant infections.

Safety: protection against unintentional accidents.

SALADs: refer to drugs with names or packaging that look or sound similar, increasing the risk
of confusion and medication errors.

SDS (Safety data sheet): include details on chemical properties, hazards, protective measures,
and safety precautions for handling, storing, and transporting chemicals.

Secondary packaging: packaging used to group and protect multiple units of the primary
packaged product.

Security: protection against intentionally harmful acts.

Security Stock (SS): also known as safety stock. It is the quantity of stock that should be readily
available to address unforeseen circumstances, such as delivery delays or unexpected spikes in
consumption. In end-user units it is normally expressed in weeks.

Self-inspection: internal process used to assess GSDP compliance across all operational
areas within a storage and distribution unit. Its purpose is to identify deficiencies, propose
recommendations, and implement the required corrective and preventive action (CAPA).

Service Level Agreement (SLA): a formal contract that defines the expected performance,
responsibilities, and key metrics between a provider and a customer. It outlines measurable
targets such as response times, uptime guarantees, and issue resolution deadlines.

Shelf-life: the period, from the date of manufacture, that a product is expected to remain
within its approved product specification while handled and stored under defined conditions.

Short shelf life: a product with a shelf life equal to or less than 24 months from the date of
manufacturing.

Sleeping stock: consumable products in stock with no consumption over the last 12 months
(excluding products recently entered in stock and products within prepositioned stocks such
as EPREP).

Stock Date: stock date is the date when physical stock data is extracted from the stock
management tool. This date should typically be the same date of the physical count, unless
there is a reason that hinders that.

Stock List: the stock list is a record of products that are currently available in stock, as well as
those that have been in stock within a specific timeframe (e.g., products currently in stockout,
certain non-consumable products).

Stockout: situation in which stock levels for a particular product within the MSL are depleted,
making it impossible to fulfil the operational demands for that product.

Substandard product: also known as "out of specification", a product that has been authorised
by NRAs but fails to meet either national or international quality standards or specifications –
or, in some cases, both.

Glossary

267

1

Supplier: an entity that provides medical products or services, which may be either MSF-
affiliated or external.

Temperature and humidity-controlled environment: area equipped and designed to ensure
both temperature and relative humidity levels within predefined ranges to preserve the quality,
safety, and stability of stored products.

Tertiary packaging: outermost packaging used for bulk shipping and distribution of products.

Theoretical stock: number of units that are recorded in a stock management tool. It represents
the expected stock level based on previous physical counts and stock movements.

Third party: either an external actor to MSF, such as the Ministry of Health (MoH), other NGOs
or other types of partners, or an internal actor within MSF, such as other projects or MSF
programs from the same MSF section or a different one.

Transfer: it typically involves moving products from one stock area to another within the same
stock location (e.g., medical warehouse, distribution pharmacy). Stock transfer is understood
as a stock management process that typically does not involve transportation.

Unplanned donation: a one-time donation that was not originally anticipated and is associated
with issues in demand planning.

VED System (vital-essential-desirable): prioritisation method used to classify medical products
based on their criticality to medical activities.

Vital product: critical product for life-saving interventions, with no suitable substitutes (e.g.,
anaesthesia products, adrenaline, etc.).

Waiver: an official exemption or reduction from certain requirements, fees, or regulations
that are normally applicable. A waiver can be granted by customs authorities or other national
regulatory bodies and is often used to facilitate import, reduce costs, or facilitate the rapid
delivery of humanitarian aid.

WATCH antibiotics: broader-spectrum and higher-cost, recommended for patients with more
severe clinical infections.

WHO EML (World Health Organisation Essential Medicines List): a list developed and
maintained by the WHO that identifies the most effective, safe, and cost-efficient medicines
for priority health needs of populations. It serves as a global reference to guide countries in
developing their own national essential medicines lists and supports the rational selection and
use of medicines in health systems.
